THE RED PAGE.
LEONARDO AND OTHERS
TplOß our literary and artistic dilettanti, this
1-4 paragraph may be a signpost to “The
.JL 1 Langham Series of Art Monographs,”
edited by Selwyn Brinton and published
by A. Siegle (Lond. ; Is. 6 d. net). The books
imitate R. Muther’s “Die Kunst” series, and
translate some of the items : others are new. By
the way, one finds Muther (who has claims to be
considered the best-informed contemporary
German critic of art, and certainly is one of the
most stimulating) declaring that Leonardo painted.
in the Mona Lisa —himself. She is no vampire,
no demon, but an image of the sceptic-sensual
spirit
Having said so much (and remembering our
beloved backblocks) it is better to say more.
Here, then, is a suggestion of possibly the most
famous picture in the world :
Mona Lisa —in the Louvre, Paris.
Leonardo da Vinci, the painter (1452-1519) was
a species of universal genius in art and life, en-
dowed with all the talents that, in Dick Savage’s
view, became his birth.
[Blest be the Bastard’s birth ! Through wondrous ways,
He shines eccentric, like a comet’s blaze /
No sickly fruit of faint compliance He t
He stamped in Nature’s mint of ecstasy !..
Nature's unbounded son, he stands alone,
His heart unbiass’d, and his mind his own.
R. SAVAOIS, 1698-1743.]
In a Christian age Leonardo revivified the
Hellenic ideals, as it is told vigorously in Merej-
kowski’s The Forerunner (Loud., Constable ;
2s. 6 d. net). As a painter, for years one
face obsessed him-Mona Lisa s. Her eyes,
her smile, he re creates in a b.-iome and in a
Madonna, in Bacchus and in Pomona. Pater’s
oft-quoted description, written beautifully and
vaguely, is an inevitable quotation :
The presence that thus rose so strangely beside the
waters is expi e-sive of what in the ways ol a thousand
years men had come to desire. Hers is the head upon
which all the ends o{ the world are come and the eyelids
are a little weary. It is a beauty wrought out from within
upon the flesh, the deposit, little cell by cell, of strange
thoughts and fantastic reveries and exquisite passions.
She is older than tiie rocks among which she sits ; like
the vampire she has been dead many times, and learned
the secrets of the grave ;and has been a diver in deep seas
and keeps their fallen day about her ; and trafficked for
strange webs with Eastern merchants ; and, as Leda, was
the mother of Helen of Troy, and, as Saint Anne, the
mother of Mary; and all this has been to tier but as the
sound of lyres and flutes, and lives only in the delicacy
with which it has moulded the changing lineaments, and
tinged the eyelids and the haims. The fancy of a per-
petual life, sweeping - together ten thousand experiences,
is an old one ; and modern thought has conceived the
idea of humanity as wrought-upon by and summing up in
itself all modes of thought and life. Certainly Lady Lisa
might stand as the embodiment of the old fancy, the sym-
bol of the modern idea.
J*
And M-uther more precisely writes :
Leonardo had already given a diabolical attraction to
his Portrait of a Lady in the Liechtenstein gallery, had
composed a h.vmn to seducing grace in the Ambrosian
portrait at Milan. And now in the Mona Lisa lie pro-
duced a work which like an eternal enigma fascinates the
spirit. Is she woman at all, or merely an hallucination?
Four years Leonardo laboured at the picture, studying in
the face of his model the reflection of all the thoughts
that music and conversation called up. liut what
are these thoughts? Is her expression tender and
voluptuous, or chill and mocking? This smile radiant
with bliss, does it not hold the sinister calm that follows
the storm ? Is she Aphrodite or Medusa? Helen or the
goddess of Death? There are works of art for which every
word “is noise and smoke, obscuring Heaven’s light.” It
is futile to wish to interpret the mind of this siren who
gazes at us like a phantom from kingdoms world-remote.
Every artist paints himself. Mona Lisa is the feminine
double of Leonardo. His own unfathomable Faust-nature
took shape in his work.
Leonardo has not yet arrived in the Langham
series : we meet Whistler and Millet : the former
by Hans Singer; the latter by Muther. The
illustrations are numerous and good—within their
limits. Singer’s attitude to his subject is that of
an honest man striving to comprehend the uncon-
genial. “Perhaps Whistler's human soul was
occupied by a double portion of pettiness, invidi-
ousness, and malice, so that his artistic spirit might
be entirely free and unfettered in its greatness.”
Muther, as elsewhere, justifies his reputation;
and his little study is written with the incisive
power that in criticism is always demanded and
often lacking. “ Millet was the first to paint
a race that knows neither Heaven nor Hell,
only earth and labour...He created the first
religious pictures of our time—and consecrated
them to Labour.” Mother's commentary falls
into line with Henley’s before he went astray
in the studios and decided that “ it was Millet s
weakness, in fact, that he was not less poet than
painter.”
THE CELTIC SPIRIT
TO Feb. Contemporary Review Havelock
Ellis contributes an article on “ The Oeltio
Spirit in Literature,” which has the effect
of co-ordinating many scattered observa-
tions upon this page. What are the characteris-
tics of Celtic Literature? “In the first place,”
says Ellis, “always there is presented to us the
remote as remote. This sense of remoteness is
deliberately sought in the finest Celtic romances.
In the second place, the Celtic poet’s method is
always decorative. That is to say, he is always
concerned to find the beautiful and harmonious
detail. The pages of Celtic romance are like a
woven tapestry, with bold outline and strong
colour, as in the Irish stories, or in the Welsh
with softly harmonised colours and delicately
flowing lines ; in either case, they produce more
nearly than anything in literature the exact effect
of an old tapestry.”
This seems a just statement; and the “remoteness” of
Quinn, the decorativeness of Daley, are familiar to us
locally. O. Wilde’s Sphinx was recently compared to a
sumptuous tapestry.—But essentially, one would find
the noblest and rarest expression of the Celt rather in
the remote than in the decorative ; and some writers disso-
ciate the qualities. Quinn’s “ Camp Within the West,”
the best local example of remoteness, is simple ; Daley’s
decorative verse, however fanciful, is always firmly held,
always attached to the present.
“ These qualities of Celtic literature rest on cer-
tain psychic qualities of the makers of Celtic
literature, of which we may especially set down
inventiveness and quick sensibility, two qualities
that are allied, or indeed identical. Quick sensi-
bility, or rapid _ feminine response in harmony
with, or in reaction against, external stimuli, is of
all qualities that which we most readily attribute
to the Celt. It is a quality of nervous texture,
even to some extent a mental quality, and
by no means a pure quality of feeling This
quick sensibility is, for instance, well illus-
trated by the Celtic eye for nature imagery, so
often used for decorative purposes in the Welsh
and especially in the Ossianic literature.
But there is more to the matter than this. Inventive-
ness and sensibility account for the decorative Celt: they
account for Daley ; but they do not account for Quinn or
Yeats. Both of these have at times the air of remember-
ing in dream, of writing what they saw as they walked on
the edge of an old ancestral world. This power is not in-
vention, is not sensibility : it seems to be a scarcely con-
scious imagination, an involuntary mental projection into
the other world remote.
“ When we have clearly defined to ourselves
these precise qualities of the Celtic mind as it
displays itself, in literature—that in vision it re-
gards the remote as remote, and in method is
decorative—we begin to realise the truth that
underlies many of the rhapsodical utterances of
the writers on 1 Celtic glamour.’ For instance,
we hear much of fairyland, of twilight, in this con-
n®^on' Mr, Yeats has called one of his books
‘ The Celtic Twilight.’ The atmosphere into
which all genuinely Celtic things —the Ulster
cycle of legends least of all—brings us is quite
accurately and precisely described as one of
twilight. Twilight has the curious property of
making the scenes it envelops appear at once
both near and remote For a moment a
kind of musioal silence seems to fill the air ;
we are conscious of the presence of mystery ;
we feel as if we had caught a glimpse of a land-
scape in another world. This impression-—fan-
tastic as it may seem, and yet explicable by the
conditions of the atmosphere during this
period of diffused light— very exactly corresponds
to the special impression which Celtic romance
makes upon us.
Yet the “glamour” is not necessarilv “decorative.”
Instead of declaring that the Celtic mind'is both remote
and decorative, it would be better to say there are two
orders of Celtic mind, the remote and the decorative—the
former very rare, and merging (sometimes in the same in-
dividual : e.g., Keats) into the latter, which is common.
With the Celtic spirit Ellis contrasts what he
calls the Nordic spirit, because it is as closely
associated with the fair long-headed peoples of
ISortliern Europe as the Celtic spirit is with the
peoples of Central and Southern Europe now or
speaking languages of the Celtic family.
J he Isordic spirit in literature is manifested at
first in the Scan unavian lands, then in North-
western France and Germany, as well as Eastern
England and Scotland. The chief of its more
primitive embodiments are the Icelandic Eddas ;
its highest artistic achievement, unmixed with
other influences, is probably the ‘Chanson de
Jtioland. . ..
“ Celtic literature takes us into a world
where bright sensations, a restless invention,
dominate from first to last; profound human
passion, with all its painful and stupid limita-
tions, is not there, is not even conceivable there,
for we are m a world where all things are
possible Nordic literature is dominated from
first to last by emotion, and where emotion
is there is limitation, tension, pressure; if the
fountain leaps high in the air it is because of the
oppression at its subterranean heart.
Ihe Jsordic poet is, however, an artist in his
methods as well as in his conceptions. Those
realistic and emotional qualities which in
the sagas grow somewhat monotonous, in the
more developed manifestations of Nordic art
become self-conscious and deliberate. The realism
- remains, but the emotion is more artfully wrought
to a climax ; and the monotony, instead of being a
helpless accident, becomes a method of heighten-
ing the total effect, so that on the basis of a
primitive realism, human emotion and monotony,
it becomes possible to erect an architectonic poem
far beyond the reach of pure Celtic art.
[Mrs. Sophia, Bryant, admitting the artistic imperfection
of the Irish, traces it, ingeniously and perhaps truly in
part at least, to their positive activity in creativeness
annulling self-criticism, and allowing imperfect work to
stand, “ the vividness of the ideal making up for the
inadequacy of its realisation.” The deliberation and
hesitancy of the Nordic mind, on the other hand involve
perpetual self-criticism and progress.— E.]
“To the Celtic mind, bloodshed and slaughter
are as empty of emotional human content as to
the child who knows not what they mean • he
remains light-hearted throughout, and when the
hosts of Queen Maeve are flung against the might
of Cuchullam and thousands fall in a moment it
is all sheer gaiety, and not one pulse of the blood
is stirred. To the Nordic mind, every stroke is
j i° vibrate through the fibres of human flesh
and becomes an appeal not to the decorative im’-
agination, but to all the emotions that make us
men and bind together the world with links of
sympathy....”
Ellis proceeds to illustrate his contrast by reference to
Shakespear, Sir Thomas Browne and others. The com
plet.e article is an important and stimulating piece of
ST. JUDAS ISCARIOT
[Watts and Co., the Lond. religious publishers, of 17
Johnson’s Court, Fleet-street, E. 0., have issued recently
The Idol of Four, by the Rev. Ernest Pomeroy, hon.
treasurer of the British Union for the Abolition of Vivi-
section. It is a collection of theological essays for which
every acute reader is counselled to remit forthwith 6s. to
the publishers—reliance upon local booksellers, domi-
nated by the demand for the baser fiction, being unlikely
to be revyarded. The first and most striking essay is an
explanation and defence of Judas Iscariot. This follows
and expands De Quincey’s similar essay ; and the Rev.
Pomeroy sees much that is to be regretted in the current
dogma. Some excerpts follow.]
rpHEOPHYLACT, the elder Lightfoot,
I Bahrt, Niemeyer, and Schmidt contended
•J- that Judas, convinced of the miraculous
powers of Christ, thought that the Messiah
would use the occasion as a fitting opportunity for
discomfiting his enemies, and would there and
then establish the kingdom of heaven on earth.
Therefore he was bitterly disappointed when he
heard the actual result of his having pointed out
to certain authorities the hiding-place of Jesus.
He could hardly believe his ears. Needless to
say, these writers do not recognise the story of
Judas kissing Jesus publicly in broad daylight to
show the authorities which he was. Jesus was
one of the best-known figures in Jerusalem, and
J udas would have never received ninety guineas
[Archbishop Whately’s estimate of the value of
thirty pieces of silver] for pointing out a man
that everyone knew, and that everyone could
point out as well as he could. From sunrise to
sunset Jesus was safe, the idol of the mob. At
night he hid. Judas disclosed his hiding-place to
the Council really pour le bon motif and for the
best. This should be clear enough from Judas’s
sorrow and death, except, perhaps, to the bigoted
and biassed.
The ancient Gnostics, and Herr von Noack
among the moderns, credited Judas with desiring
to bring about the triumph of Christianity by the
redemptive death of Jesus—that is to say,
Christianity triumphing because, and in expiation,
of his death. In this case, Judas succeeded, and
Lord Beaconsfield said that he deserved a statue.
Paulus, Winer, Hase, G. Sehollmeyer, and
Whately believed that Judas became impatient of
delay, and attempted too hurriedly to bring his
Master’s name eternally before the world. Judas
undoubtedly succeeded in this, though the event,
proximately considered, did not appear to have
done 50....
J*
It may be said, then, that there are three
authoritative theories as regards Judas’s unfortu-
nate scheme and its fatal termination.
1. Judas over-estimated the miraculous powers of his
Master. He became convinced that he had only to
seize a fitting and momentous occasion for the display of
those powers in order to secure for his Master the posses-
sion of the throne of David, together with the discom-
fiture of all the authorities, Jewish and Roman, particu-
larly the Jewish ones ; while important concessions would
be obtained from the Pro-Oonsuls and procurators of
Rome.
2. Judas viewed the procrastination of his Master with
alarm, and conceived that, by forcing His hand, a definite
movement would be instituted which could but be crowned
with a brilliant and undying success.
3. Judas wished to bring about the final triumph of
Ohristianitv by the redemptive death of Jesus as Saviour
of the world.
In the first two cases it may be said that Judas’s
move is pardonable in aim, though deplorable in
result. He may be censured but forgiven. In
the third case the glorious triumph of the result
may have exceeded Judas’s wildest dreams, but
his motive was a vicious one, and he must, there-
fore be censured, but with military honours.
From a soldier’s point of view, the bravery of
Judas is undeniable. For stubborn fearlessness
in conception, and cool strategy, no battlefield
can supply his equal. Both as regards this world
and the next he stood undaunted. Picture to
yourself his position. . . . Remember the divine
attributes and the miracles of the Master. He
must have frequently heard tell of these, and
occasionally have witnessed them. Imagine for
one moment the bitter horror wh ch must have
seized him in reflecting that he himself might
shortly, in his own person, afford a manifestation
of these supernatural gifts. He had seen a
withered hand rastored, a maimed man walk,
the blind see, and several xlevils removed from
Mary Magdalene. He also knew only too
well what had happened at Gadara. Might
it not be, to put it lightly, that on the Monday
succeeding the betrayal he himself would be
afflicted with a withered hand ; on Tuesday he
might be distinctly halt, if not. maimed ; on
Wednesday he would find his vision defective ; on
Thursday he would be a leper; on Friday he
would note the entry into his system of a con-
siderable number of devils; and on Saturday he
would discover himself running down a steep
place into the sea, en route for the realms below.
The bare possibility of such a succession of events
must have been simply appalling. Judas, to my
mind, is the bravest man who ever lived....
J*
In what may be called Theory No. 4 the asser-
tion is that Judas actually came to dislike
Christ, “ that he had become repulsive to him,
and grew more so everyday.” (See Meander’s
Life of Jesus.) . . . As is well known, several
writers entirely deny the traditional beauty of
Jesus as handed down by the Divines. Justin
Martyr, A.D. 100, who may be regarded as a con-
temporary of Christ, describes the Messiah as
being “without beauty, without glory, without
honour.” The body of Jesus, according to Origen,
A.D. 185, “ was small and ill-shapen and ignoble.”
Tertullian, A.D, 160, writing of Jesus, says that
his person “had no human handsomeness, much
less any celestial splendour.”
In Rex Regum, by Sir Wyke Bayliss, K. 8.,
F.S.A., the likeness of the Master has been dealt
with fully. Sir Wyke Bayliss says it is a question
whether we possess any authentic likeness of the
Master. He then collects all the scattered evi-
dences—portraits historical, archaeological, and
artistic—and considers that they all indubitably
show the traces of one common figure or ancestor.
Some writers consider this method of framing for
our mind’s eye a portrait of Christ to be both
difficult and incorrect. A well-known exhm-f.
holds that the face of Jesus so handed down to ns
is not an authentic record of likenesses but a
series of imaginary faces. Moreover, this’authm-
lty considers that the face of Christ finally decided
upon is little more than the residuum of a long line
of more or less incapable artists. 6 0
In any case, it must be remembered that Judas
was ■ ithout this portraiture, regarded as authen
tical, and he was, therefore, without the creation
of the ideal as depicted in modern Christian
art. He had before him only the actual founder
not the ideal, as we have ; and the judgment 0 f
Judas may have been more hasty, biassed, and ill
considered. On the other hand, it was’derived"
from the original, and not from a copy...
Before condemning Judas we should attempt to
discover his excuses and his point of view. I n
addition to the person and appearance of Jesus
annoying J udas (assuming with Clemens of Alex-
andria that these were base) some of the teaching
of the Messiah may have been particularly hateful
and even exasperating to Judas. A few of Christ's
favourite texts may afford suggestions for his
heretical disagreement, and possibly excuses.
1. “ The publicans and harlots go into the Kingdom of
God before you. For John came unto you, and ve
believed him not ; but the publicans and harlots believed
him.” Now, Judas may have particularly disliked nub
licans and harlots, and thought it to be nothing in favour
of the teaching that they were to precede him in the next
world ; and he may have bitterly resented this assurance
from the Master.
2. “If any man will come after me, let him deny him-
self and follow me.... If any man come to me and hate
not his father, and mother, and wife, and children and
brethren, and sisters, and his own life also, he cannot be
my disciple.” This statement may have, as it were, been
turned suddenly on Judas, when he was quite unprepared
for it. Judas was a Southerner, a native of Kerioth in
the tribe of Judah Among the Twelve Apostles Judas
was the only Southerner. He may have been more hot-
blooded and less able to withstand the figurative language
of his divine Master. Judas may have dearly loved his
father Simon, and also his mother, wife, children, and all
his sisters down in Judah. Such a course as prescribed
by Jesus may have been harder to a native of Kerioth
than it was to the colder and more placid Galileans who
composed the rest of the flock.
3. Jesus said unto one man, “Follow me!” But he
said : “ Lord, suffer me first to go and bury my father.”
Jesus answered : “ Let the dead bury their dead : but go
thou and preach the Kingdom of God.” This, following
close to the last, may have added fuel to the fire of Judas!
Possibly he had hut lately lost a parent, or a near relative"
and had contemplated burying them...
The key to Judas’s character is over-zeal, not
treachery; and every right-minded man must
hope that the term “ traitor” will never be more
applied to this most estimable Apostle. He, the
maligned, maltreated, and misunderstood, can
never have imagined that for 2000 years his name
was destined to be associated with all that is
ignoble, dastardly, and base. Possibly in the
near future some novelist will rise to the occasion,
and we shall have a new Dombcy and Son in which
a little Judas will come to the front and com-
pletely supersede that idol so dear to a mother's
heart, our sweet and lovable, but rather pallid,
little Paul.
Judas, then, does not deserve his lamentable
position. He is worthier, rather than less worthy,
than many so-called saints and heroes. For instance,
take fSt. George, the patron saint of England.
He is esteemed as the patron saint of Chivalry.
Nevertheless, he has nothing in particular to do
with Chivalry or England, nor has he much to do
with Christianity and the Master. The accounts
of him seem very obscure, and for the most part
legend.. . . Why should we not substitute St.
Judas of Kerioth for St. George? Judas un-
doubtedly existed. He is connected with the
Holy land and with our religion. And if there
had been no Judas, there might have been no
Jesus. At least, He might have died quietly and
naturally, and have been forgotten. At all events,
Judas is inseparable from the Messiah, and he is a
far more estimable man than George of Cappadocia
or Chwolson of Tammon. St. Judas for England
would be but a fitting tribute to a man whom we
have so grievously misunderstood and maligned
for so long. ...
“ However, Judas cannot be left where he was.
Judged by military standards, and comparing his
methods with those accepted in love, in war, or in
statecraft, he must appear the soul of honour.
Even accepting the most ordinary theory, we must
recollect that Judas, in the hour of victory, after
having seen the Master secured and marched
before Pilate, even then Judas was for merhy.
He returned to the Council of the Elders;
he flung the silver pieces on the floor, and said :
“ Give me back my Master .” His request was
refused, and he committed suicide. Nevertheless
even in this account Judas was undoubtedly the
victor. He sacrificed Jesus to win the battle of
Christianity for all time for the Christians. He
sacrificed his own name for ever, and gave up his
life. Such an end is earnest, upright, and noble.
Our next great general or our next great states-
man might draw a lesson from these pages. He
should pass over such titles as Lord Roberts of
Candahar, Lord Kitchener of Khartoum, or Lord
Curzon of Kedlestone. He should look the world
in the face, and boldly style himself Lord Judas of
Kerioth ; family name, Iscariot; his crest, a
cross. Such a man, if he comes, will indubitably
deserve his peerage.”
©
The panegyric of Miss Roosevelt, ready to be
an American bride, presents certain features. “ Constant
outdoor exercise has given her unusual strength and en-
durance. Notwithstanding her willowy figure, she is as
muscular as a greyhound.” This both promises and me-
naces Mr. Longworth, the adventurous American bride-
groom. He stands in the arena of the American story—
“ and which came out of the open door, the Lady ? or the
Tiger?”
Frank Morton, settled in Dunedin, expresses
another journalist’s admiration of Maoriland. Gresley
Lukin has exhausted unflattering comparisons ; and G.
B. Barton exhausted flattering superlatives: despite
Julius Vogel, there was no place like it to be a journalist
in—“ far preferable to Australia.” Yet the Maorilanders
are uneasy in their paradise, or the young ones. Perhaps
it is only when one gets elderly and realises what a large
and important organ the liver is, that one really appre-
ciates physical stimulation and intellectual—no, we
won’t say torpor... calm.
A.O.S.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Moral of the Two Mooltans.
Kindly give me your attention
For a moment, if you please,
For which gracious condescension
I will thank you on my knees,
And will tax my dull invention
For a story of the seas
And the moral of the two Mooltans.
She was quite the finest steamer
Of the good old P. and 0.,
A leviathan they’d deem her
Over forty years ago,
When they launched with flag and streamer
And a rousing cheer or so
The original and first Mooltan.
Oh, they thought her very splendid !
Five-and-twenty hundred tons!
And her beams the longest ended —
Forty feet between the guns—
Two small cannon which extended
Friendly greetings on the runs—
Very good runs —of the old Mooltan.
Yes, they used to think her ripping,
Did the officers in charge,
But, compared to modern shipping,
She was nothing but a barge !
For last Thursday there came skipping
In a ship four times as large !
’Twas her namesake; ’twas the new Mooltan !
Yet the old Mooltan, my brothers,
Was a beauty in her way ;
But you see, like all the others,
Ships or dogs, she had her day !
That’s a law that every mother’s
Son, like steamships, must obey—•
The effacement of the old Mooltan !
When the old ship sailed the ocean
Cobdenites were all elate,
With the vain and foolish notion
Of convincing every State.
But what once caused much commotion
Is as surely out of date
As that bygone craft, the old Mooltan !
There is nothing lasts for ever
As it is, for all things change;
And mankind will still endeavor
To achieve a higher range.-
These remarks are scarcely clever,
Nor particularly strange—
They’re the moral of the two Mooltans !
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.
If This Be Love.
If this be love, I challenge you, Good Master,
That ’plains of charms decayed and glamor flown;
Then ’tis a gourd, of growth and fading faster
Than that o’er which a prophet once made moan.
If this be love ’tis scarcely worth the winning,
It is not worth the price we often pay ;
Is this heart’s need that tempts our souls to
sinning,
A cowslip ball, but made to throw away ?
Methough true love was older than the oldest,
That elixir of life was in its breath ;
That it could lend fresh daring to the boldest,
And look unflinching in the face of Death.
Methought true love was no mere weeping willow
That east or west winds as they list might rook ;
Methought true love could scorn the rudest billow
That dashed against it. tho’ with Titan shock.
Sure, perfect love is not mere passion heated
That wakes and cries in Spring-time o’ the year,
Then wails itself faint-hearted and defeated—
Give me the love that casteth out all fear.
MAORILAND.
ROSLYN.
Counter Rogues.
She serves the laces and beside her, he
Deals out the satinette,
Both have an air of rigid industry ;
But, oh ! she does coquette,
And while the customers choose anxiously
His eyes on her are set.
Sometimes a message on a bill-back bears
Her latest, tenderest thought;
She sighs (in pencil) of her hopes, her cares,
Her spirit overwrought,
Her fluttering heart —and all the things she
dares
To pencil down, in short.
And he with stealth and smile reciprocates.
Brief notes between the bills
Are pressed, or by her side he hesitates,
And some address forms fills,
While, all the time, his joy, sub rosa, states,
And hints at warm heart-thrills.
The days are never tedious or slow;
With hearts uplifted they
Can serve their “lines,” endure the tidal flow
At bargain times, can play
Their smiling quick “Yes, Madame” parts,
. while oh !
Love has his secret say.
Against all customers cross-grained and tart
Thus they are fortified ;
Such worries end at last, the cats depart,
And they that might have sighed
And groaned, exchange sly looks, and, minus
chart,
Steer on Love’s Counter-tide.
That he is married is a trial; she
His lot commiserates.
Responsively he sighs that she’s not free,
And her booked Gussie hates.
But ah ! they tease each other gladsomely,
These jolly Counter mates.
VIC.
E. STONE.

How to Address Betters.
Correspondents to Tun Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them, to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for "copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
ahvays is.

THURSDAY, MARCH 22, 1906.
THE MERE AUSTRALIAN.
It is discouraging to be an Australian in
Australia. This scantily-populated continent has
in its time bred men of quality somewhat plenti-
fully—men of quality enough to pioneer big
enterprises in other lands. In all the odd corners
of the earth, as well as the big cities, Australians
can be found, mining, engineering, administering,
doctoring, teaching, singing, painting, writing;
and in an extraordinary number of cases they are
doing notably well. No other such small patch
of population in the same time has given to the
outside world so many persons above the level
of mediocrity. But the Australian (using
the term in its broadest sense as the native-born,
or the immigrant arriving here young enough or
plastic enough to become Australian) does very
poorly in his own Commonwealth. He must
submit to be jostled aside by any imported per-
sonage who happens to come on the scene, unless
he is one of those very thorny sorts who
cannot be jostled with safety. The man of excep-
tional ability and a proper appreciation of it can-
not, of course, be denied, even though he is
an Australian in Australia. But excluding
them, and considering only the talented—rang-
ing from a little above ordinary to distinguished—
the handicap of being an Australian in this
Commonwealth is a very serious one. The proper
road to advancement is to be British born and
educated ; to arrive with a conspicuously English
carpet bag, trousers that announce the prevailing
wetness in London, and an accent of haw-ty
superiority. Then are the doors of preferment
opened unto you and soft nests prepared, whilst
the Australian of talent prepares to emigrate and
to find in Europe, South America or Asia the
appreciation of his abilities which his own land
refuses.
* * * *
The explanation is probably to be found in the
failure of the Australian in that art which has
come down from antiquity witif its old physical
name still gross upon it—back-scratching. The
flatterer or the parasite of to-day no longer
actually wields an ivory spatula to tickle the
sensorium of a patron, as in older and more
verminous days. But the word back-scratcher has
worthily survived. It penetrates at once to the
mind and satisfies it with ample and true present-
ment of the idea. Toady, sycophant, flatterer,
parasite, cringer, crawler—no one of these titles
gives quite the full value of back-scratcher, with
its appropriately rough sound, its suggestion of
knowledge of how and where to scratch, and
its hint of the ample delights of the
patron at being skilfully tickled. The
Australian is a bad back-scratcher. The com-
plaisant word is foreign to his lips, and his
knees are reluctant to servility. “It’s hard to
get Australians into the way of saying ‘sir’,”
attested one British officer at a military inquiry
now proceeding. In saying it he gave a true and
fine testimonial to the Australian character. It
certainly is hard to get ‘ * sir ” and a doff of the
cap from sons and grandsons and great-grandsons
of men, who were convicts because they were not
duly servile to the squire in the old world, or
exiles because they craved a larger air to
breathe than Europe gave. But the im-
ported gentleman who comes from Oxford
with an adventurous carpet-bag has learned
to say “sir,” and to bow and to scrape.
He knows the art, for it is an art native to his
land, where the Duke is honored by being called
upon to look after the trousers of a King, and an
Earl is respectful to a Duke, and a Mayor genu-
flects to a Baron, and hardly any flea but has a
smaller flea to look up to him as a sort of earthly
Providence. Knowing the art of back-scratching,
the imported person has but to insinuate his spa-
tula into the crack of a departmental door, so to
speak, and he rises rapidly to a fat post of ease
and affluence.
♦ * » *
To allow of the success of the back-scratcher there
must, of course, be backs eager for scratch. They
are to be found, alas, in plenty in politics and in the
Government departments. For two generations
politics in Australia were perceptibly foreign to
Australian ideas. The cities made the Parlia-
ments, and at the time just before the Labor party
irrupted, Australian public life was curiously out
of touch with Australian ideas. Cabinet Minis-
ters were open and shameless in making a British
title the object of their dearest ambitions, and to
their minds any Hon. Percy was, by divine right
of being the younger son of a titled person, wel-
come to any Australian civil post he aspired to,
provided it was not already occupied or claimed by
a still more honorable Percy. Now things are
changing. But the old habits linger. The Hon.
Percy still claims, and receives asa right, rich plums
for which no Bill, however talented, has a chance.
Even politicians who profess to be Australian are
to be found mechanically appointing Percy as
though it were a habit which could not be shaken
off, and in the Federal Administration itself the
reverence for what is hall-marked British and
the impatience for what is Australian shows
markedly.
* * * *
In the early appointments to the Federal Civil
Service the imported Johnny and the rare degene-
rate Australian with a facility for imitating his
servility, scored notably, and whenever since there
has been a hint of public service scandal it will be
found almost always that the root of the trouble is
in the recognition of the imported British person’s
divine right to get the plums of the Australian
service, and to be treated as a being apart from
the common run of “colonists.” Probably the
latest Papuan scandal can be traced back to the
same cause. That part of Papua now in the hands
of the Commonwealth is governed temporarily by
Captain Barton, who is said by those who know
him to be an estimable and likeable gentleman,
but who, judging by what we gather from several
correspondents in the Possession, is not a success
as a governor. There has been much friction of
late in connection with his administration, and one
incident culminated in a serious difference be-
tween Captain Barton and Mr. Richmond, a mere
Australian in the Papuan Government service.
A Board of Inquiry was appointed by the Federal
Government to look into the matter. It sat in
Melbourne—obviously not the best place to get
evidence on a Papuan matter. Still the Board took
evidence of a sort. It had the impressions in
favor of Captain Barton of Mr. Atlee Hunt —
the gentleman who, as Foreign Under-Secretary,
hasn’t been a miracle of discretion. It also had
the sworn statements of four Papuan civil ser-
vants, and these four all took Mr. Richmond’s
view of the case. And yet it decided against Mr.
Richmond, who was subjected to a punishment
savage enough to please the most ardent friend of
British Johnny administration.
♦ * * m
The impression among those who know most of
the matter is that Mr. Richmond has been sacri-
ficed in obedience to English influence working
through what the daily press delights to call
“ exalted personages.” That impression may not
be right. It is to be hoped that it is not, for it
would be deplorable to think that Australian
administration, not content with advancing the
Hon. Percy type unfairly, is willing to give Bill an
undeserved kick in Percy’s interests. When Parlia-
ment meets, the truth will be dragged out —the
more cheerfully for there will then be another
Civil Service scandal to investigate, pointing to the
working of the same influences. The Bulletin
disavows any sympathy with the somewhat stupid
A.N.A. worship of “Australian natives.” It
welcomes the Britisher, or any other decent white
man of parts, who cares to come here and share
our national life and aspirations, welcomes him as
Prime Minister, Chief Justice, or in any other
high office he can reach. But there has been in the
past, in connection with the State Governments, an
unfair system of patronage and favor, of which
the beneficiaries were young Englishmen with
titled friends “working” Australian Governors
and Australian toadies on their behalf. That
system has loaded the public service with
some of its choicest incapables, and crowded it
with the Hon. Percy. And we do not wish to
see the system perpetuated under the Federation.
In a local capacity the Hon. Percy, whose quali-
fications consist solely in being Percy, is bad
enough. Spread over a wider field he would be
deplorable.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Bent’s Surplus.
There is no more amazing figure in Australian
politics to-day than Thomas Bent, Premier of
Victoria. He looks like one of Nature’s jokes,
and suggests that Nature is of Scotch descent and
is humorous with difficulty. It is impossible to
do other than jeer at his clowning, his elephantine
witticisms, his awful comic songs, his wonderful
reminiscences, and his oft-told anecdotes whereof
the point was broken off in the Dark Ages. And
yet, in some respects, it is impossible to get away
from the fact that Bent —this vast mass of meat
and contradictions—is the greatest figure in pre-
sent-day Australian State politics. He is the first
man who has apparently acquired a desire to get the
great Debt Problem down and either strangle it
or else talk it to death; and an Australian Pre-
mier who recognises that the pawning of his
country is an unmitigated evil, and that the get-
ting of it out of pawn is the greatest possible
good, is a man who might be forgiven even
if he bought a book of comic songs and sang
the collection right through. Australia just now
is in collision with the worst financial problem of
its history. The loans that it has floated so gaily
for the last 50 years are falling due in multitudes.
It isn’t only one curse, but a drove of curses and
a herd of maledictions that are coming home to
roost. Formerly it was only a question of raising
money to spend, and when, as happened now and
then, the London money-lender refused to make
an advance, the only thing that happened was
that we didn’t spend the money. But now, in 34
out of the next 44 years, Australia has to raise
huge loans, not to spend but merely to repay old
loans that are falling due. Even if the country
leaves off fresh borrowings there are —unless
it does something more than merely leave
off fresh borrowings—34 chances between now
and 1951 for it to go bankrupt, simply
because any year London may be hard up,
and decline to float the new loan with which to
repay the old one. A great war or any other
large international trouble would make Aus-
tralia’s public insolvency almost certain, as
things are now. And Bent, the comic
songster of Brighton, the man with strange
recollections about cabbage, the ex-actor, the
individual who loves to be a municipal councillor
and to scrap with Councillor Ferdinando about
a drain or a culvert—this Bent seems to be the
only man who realises the situation. Victoria
has £4,000,000 of old English loans to repay in
1907 and another £2,000,000 in 1908. Bent has
shut down on new borrowings, and his present
ambition, he says, is to scrape up £1,000,000 of
surplus revenue with which to pay off a quarter
of the £4,000,000 which falls due in 1907. If he
can do that he will not only save £40,000 a year
in interest, but he will almost certainly be able to
renew the other £3,000,000 with greater ease and
at a lower rate of interest. Probably, too, he
will be able to renew it locally, whereby he will
not only make an absolute saving of £40,000 a
year, but he will also prevent another
£120,000 a year going out of the country.
It is with this end in view, apparently, that
Bent refuses to reduce the income-tax, and
speaks sourly to municipal beggars to whom he used
to be affable. Victoria has a lengthened rush of big
loans falling due—£4,ooo,ooo in 1907, £2,000,000
in 1908, £2,000,000 in 1911, nearlv £5,000,000 in
1913, £3,000,000 in 1917, £4,000,000 in 1919,
£6,000,000 in 1920, £6,000,000 in 1921, £7,000,000
in 1923, and £8,500,000 in 1929. Over any of these
renewals, if it is wholly dependent on the London
market, it is liable to be remorselessly squeezed,
and told to take its choice between paying 6 per
cent, and bankruptcy, whereas if it can pay off
part of each loan in cash, and renew the balance
or a great part of the balance locally, it can
always tell London to go to Sheol if London seeks
to drive a hard bargain. Among all the State
Premiers, Bent seems to be the only one who
looks forward with an intelligent eye to the future,
and for a man with so much waistcoat to obstruct
his'.vision this is all the more creditable. If he
would only go a little further, and pass an Act
declaring that there shall be no more loans
except for renewal purposes, that a payment of
(say) £250,000 a year towards the reduction of old
loans shall be a first charge on the revenue, and
that a second charge on the revenue shall be a
payment of (say) £500,000 a year for new public
works—if he would do those things he would be
the greatest and best Premier that Australia has
seen for something like half a century.
♦
The “Trade” Mania,
London, Wednesday.— Mr. Edmund Sachse reached the
Island of Perim, in the Red Sea, on Sunday after an
African trip. He is reported to have visited Menelik,
the Negus of Abyssinia, with a view to ascertaining the
possibilities of opening up trade between Australia and
Abyssinia. — Recent Cablegram.
The Bulletin doesn’t remember very clearly who
Edmund Sachse is, but he seems to be an
energetic man, and the fact that he has been
struggling for “ trade ” among the huge mountain
ranges of Abyssinia is something to his credit. It
shows that he means well, even if he is on a fool’s
errand. Abyssinia is a land vaguely supposed to
contain 3Jj millions of population, though they may
be only a million or less. It has no real roads, and
hardly possesses any towns with more than 5000
people. Trade is carried on mostly per donkey or
mule. Abyssinia is reputed to contain 12,000
monks, and it has a speck or two of commerce.
Britain has been struggling after Abyssinian
business for a long while, and in 1903
its exports to that obscure country were
worth exactly £s692—mostly coal. That
isn’t much, but probably Britain puts some value
on it. In Britain, “ trade ” always means foreign
trade—it is a way the followers of Cobden have
got into, and they can’t believe that commerce is
real unless it involves dragging something off a
ship that has come a long way, or shoving some-
thing on to a ship that is going a long way. By a
sort of instinct, and without realising it, the
British Freetrader would rather import £lO
worth of stuff from Greenland and re-ship
it to Bagdad at a loss than sell £4O
worth of locally-made stuff at a profit to the
man next door. So Britain, by neglecting its
local market and leaving it open to every alien,
has reached a point at which it spends £140,000,000
a year in buying foreign-manufactured goods for
its own use—mostly goods which, it used to make
in its own factories —while it sends £5692 worth of
raw material’(coal) to Abyssinia. Australia has
got the same foolish idea, though it may not have
got it quite so badly. There is a market right at its
door, in Australia itself, for about £40,000,000 worth
of Australian goods. This country imports goods to
that value, when it might, if it had a decent Pro-
tectionist tariff, manufacture them for itself, and
sell them to itself. If it manufactured them for
itself the work would employ a good 400,000 or
500,000 workers, and support them and their
families —something approaching 2,000,000 people.
And they would probably support another
2,000,000 of farmers, artisans, shopkeepers, &c.,
who would want about another £4o,ooo,oooworth of
produce, and thus double the population and in-
dustry again. This is the market that we know
of and are sure of, and it is a sad thing that it
should be so much neglected while Mr. Edmund
Sachse takes the chance of breaking his neck as
he shins up awful precipices in Abyssinia to see if
there is a chance to sell a bag of potatoes or a bale of
wool at the top. The country that Sachse has
been exploring is a dreadfully poor place, and very
backward. A great deal of its commerce has to be
let down precipices with ropes, or butted up huge
ranges by main force, and the merchant is often
liable to be eaten by the spotted hyena, or the
goods trodden on by the two-horned rhinoceros.
And then, at the end of a dreadful journey into
the interior, the chances are that the trader is
swindled by a tribe of alleged Jews, who say they
came into the country in the time of Reiioboam,
and who look like it. There is more good, reliable
new trade to be done with Parramatta or Thargo-
mindah, or Wangaratta, if only the importer werq
sufficiently discouraged, than all Abyssinia is
likely to supply this century—but first we require
to get out of the habit of believing that commerce
must consist in dragging goods down a plank off
a ship, or shoving them up a plank on to a ship.
4.
A Public Service Trouble.
Postal employees in the clerical division are rais-
ing Cain and other inhabitants over the increment
question. The Federal Public Service Act de-
clares that increments should be automatic, but>
in the case of officers receiving over £l6O, should
be given “as a reward for earnest application
to duty and meritorious public service.”
Nevertheless, in the Home Affairs, Treasury,
Customs, etc., etc., increments have been
given to everyone. The amount placed on
the Estimates for postal increments was, however,
cut down by the central office to one-half, and it
was evident that in that department there would
not be nearly enough increase to go round. This
was done before any inquiry had been made to
how many officers merited increments. The
postal people saw their political friends, and
Hume Cook asked, in a firm and numerous tone,
what it meant. The secretary of the Treasury
wrote to him (the letter is held as Exhibit A by
the postal employees), and told him that though
only half the necessary amount had been set on the
Estimates, the Treasurer would pay the other half
of the increases out of savings. With this assur-
ance any proposed opposition to the Estimates was
withdrawn. The lists of officers to receive
increments were prepared by the deputy Post-
masters-General and they included everybody.
The central office sent the lists back with an
intimation that they must be cut down by half.
This was done, and seniority being taken as the
basis of merit, all the senior officers in each class
got a rise, the poorly paid ones being
left out. Whereat the left-out officers-are
standing high on their hind legs and shouting
earnestly. They claim that the action of
the central office tends to nullify the Public Ser-
vice classification, since junior officers in other
departments, by virtue of the increments which
were given indiscriminately, have become senior
to postal officers who got no increments. Also, they
claim that the Act itself has not been obeyed, since
the increments that were given in the Post Office
were given for seniority and not for merit. The
neglected officers say they will leave no stone
unturned to get satisfaction, and they are already
looking under a multitude of rocks for their lost
cash.

The British Labor Pilgrimage.
When Ramsay Macdonald, Keir Hardie, and
co. visit Australia the Federal Labor party should
make sure of these fundamental points:—
(1) What is there in common between Australian and
British democracy?
(2) How can Australian and British democracy mutually
benefit each other?
So far Australia and Britain have been racing for
different stakes. Our goal is not Britain’s. Our
first aim is the building up of a new nation,
that one day may have a record to compare with
the champion countries of old time. Asia
threatens; and we must fight hard for the simple
preservation of a White Australia. For if Aus-
tralia is not to be a white and free man’s country
we can never work out the future we like to talk
about. Then, as a logical deduction, prohibitive
Protection is essential in order that we may do our
own work, and thereby multiply and increase, in-
stead of buying the products of sweated or colored
countries, and remaining stagnant. Our problems
are primitive, but vital. For we must secure men
and materials before we can build our house;
and we must build the house before we
can attend to the mural decorations. The
Australian democracy is the only party that fights
for Australia’s future. It is really the nation.
But British democracy is still a class party that is
fighting for the most elementary of social rights—
a free franchise, reasonably fair hours and wages,
payment of members, protection of the weak and
helpless, etc.. On bigger problems it has still little
to say. It has allowed the British Constitution,
the Monarchy, the House ot Lords, the Estab-
lished Church, and other curious relics of barbarism
to submerge the majority of the population and to
refuse them work because it “ would destroy their
moral fibre.” Unemployment means cheap labor ;
and 12 millions of starving poor testify to the
Briton’s noble self-reliance. In sheer despair
British democracy took to soaking itself in
a beer-barrel, and it will now take some time
to shake off the froth. No good democrat
can support any of the traditional British parties.
Campbell-Bannerman means well, but, fed on
Cobdenism and large phrases, he upholds a vague
kind of ‘ ‘ liberty ” that consists in letting things
drift, and in having England swarmed over by
the refuse of Europe. Morley is worse—he is a
painful repetition of Gladstone, one of the most
pathetic ineptitudes in politics, and cheerfully
advocates “liberal” measures that date from
Noah, and opposes the mildest modern movement
as “extreme.” Chamberlain is the one man
who has anything to say; and if he had allied
himself to democracy, he might have been at the
head of affairs ; but for hanging behind the Tory
caravan he deserves to be left in the ditch. Bal-
four is more humorous than George Reid in his
efforts to retain power. He believes in nothing,
does nothing, suggests nothing ; explains that he
didn’t mean what he didn’t say ; and dabbles in
golf, politics, and elementary metaphysics. But
the bright particular star is the cheerful Duke of
Devonshire. He retains all the deadly, tradi-
tional Tory notions; but because Toryism
threatens to shed Freetrade, he forms a party of
his own that advocates each and all the vices.
The Irish National party believes in Home Rule,
and in nothing else. It would support Joe Cook
if he had a chance of granting Home Rule, and,
outside of Home Rule, it doesn’t care if it snows.
The mission of the Labor Pilgrims is very
English and very vague : “To rescue the Empire
and Imperial spirit from being exploited by
reactionary anti-social classes at home,” and “to
make the Empire stand for peace and democratic
justice in the eyes of the whole world,” mean
about as much as Reid’s policy of “Anti-
Socialism.” The Empire is large enough
to suggest peace; but in face of Japan’s aggression
and China’s awakening, to preach “ peace ” to
Australia is like telling the householder to snore
soundly, as the burglars are only in the garden.
“ To get our colonial fellow-workers to understand
the Labor movement of the Mother country, and
to share in its fortunes,” means that Australia
should not prohibit British-made goods and should
still allow England to do the things for us we
should be doing ourselves. The Mission also
proposes “to study various social experiments
which have been started in the colonies.” If
it keeps its eyes open, it should learn that Aus-
tralian democracy is something that Britain never
dreamt of.


The Reform Chow.
“Squidge ” writes :
The reason for the Victorian Chow discarding his
conspicuous Chinese toggery (B. 15/2/’O6) is to be found
in the propaganda work of the Chinese Reform Club.
This association preaches to the Chows the wisdom of
discarding all Chow customs and adopting those of the
foreign devils among whom they live. The society in
Melbourne is engineered by barrister An Kett —no relation
to “ Dr.” Hackett of Perth West Australian. Aii Kett
has with him all the Chow missionary crowd and all the
wealthy Chinese merchants. The Reform Club has
grown to be a powerful organisation, and it is mainly
due to its efforts that the opium traffic was squelched.
The reformers make no secret of their object. They
reckon that, the opium-smoking Chow, sodden with
Oriental conservatism, has no chance of getting a grip
on Australia. But they also reckon that Australia
was made for the Chow, and that the way to obtain this
heritage is to meet the Australian on his own ground.
The Reform Club having removed the opium habit, has
made arrangements with the various hospitals for the
treatment of those Chows who are going to suffer pangs
through being deprived of their pipes. The Reformers
can thus assure the opium smokers that their welfare is
the only object in view, while the merchants point to
their own personal loss as evidence of their earnestness in
the matter. A Chinese newspaper is printed (by the
way, during the Jap-Russ war this organ is said to have
always contained earlier and more authentic news than
the “ great dailies ”), and it devotes itself to the regener-
ation of the Chow. He is urged to wash himself, to cut
his hair, and to be always presentable. In Melb urne at
the present time there are quite 300 pure-bred Chows
who talk good English, who are well-read, well-
dressed, well-groomed, and have a fine outward
veneer of civilisation. Ah .Kett is the chief, of
the cult—their model, their adviser, their generalissimo.
What plans are hidden away in his brain only he knows ;
but he has made it his business to lift the Chow out of
the gutter. Over 1000 Chinamen attended the Reform
Club’s last concert (they are given at regular intervals) in
the Temperance Hall. All the performers were Chinese,
but all the performances were European—including a
lecture in English by a Chinese big-pot on “ The Reform
Movement.” Nearly every night a Reform orator preaches
to a big crowd of Chows from a balcony in Little Bourke-
street. There are about 10,000 Chinese in Victoria, and
all of them can acquire votes ; most of them probably
have votes now. The Reform idea is to concen-
trate these votes on a ticket of their own, and
build up a great Chow political power. If An Kktt’s
work had started 40 years ago it might have come to
something, and the educated Chow inside Australia
might have acquired sufficient influence to enable him to
keep the national gate open to the barbarous Chow out-
side. But now Ah Kett and Ah Bang and Ah Plug and
the other Chow organisers are only preaching to a
diminishing remnant. Also it is a remnant which grows
very old for want of young Chows to leaven the venerable
mass.

Seven Bushels—and 24.
Thrown in among the beetles and other farmers’
worries, of which the annual N.S.W. Report on
Agriculture is mostly made up, are a couple of
sentences or so that are worth hard thinking over :
The average wheat yield for the counties of Clarendon,
Mitchell, Wynyard and Bourke, which lie round the
Wagga Experimental Farm, was a trifle more than 8$
bushels to the acre, whilst the average yield on the Ex-
perimental Farm was 18$ bushels.
The average yield for the counties of Bathurst and Rox-
burgh, which surround the Bathurst Experimental Farm,
was nearly 7 bushels to the acre, whilst the average yield
at the Experimental Farm was 24J bushels to the acre.
There was no reason, so far as I (Director of Agricul-
ture) can ascertain, why the yield on the farms where the
average was so low should not have been equal to that on
the Experimental Farms if proper methods of farming had
been carried out.
Yet when Hyde Park and the Domain overflow
with “dossers,” the Freetrade papers tell them to
“go on the land.’’ When young men without a
trade to their hands accumulate, till an advertise-
ment for a lad to “make himself useful ” brings
in a cloud of applicants (and some have to scrape
off their greying whiskers to get themselves down,
so to speak, to weight), there is still the same cry,
“Put them on the land.” Anybody can be a
farmer —all he has to do is to take in a daily
paper with a “Soil 5 ’ column, plough when it says
plough, sow when it says sow, and reap when he
can. In other countries the always-present
crowds of unskilled laborers are absorbed in fac-
tories or about them ; in Australia Calico Jimmy
says that agriculture is “ the great natural in-
dustry,” wherefore factories are not required ; and
the unskilled army, which elsewhere does good
service at the rough work of manufacture, is told
to “go on the land.” There is hardly an occupa-
tion in the world for which this army is, as a rule,
less suited. Besides calling for capital, farming
calls for patience and persistence; and mostly
these men have neither capital nor persistence.
In very few cases have they deliberately set out
to get where they are ; they have just drifted, and
both their experience and their temperament
usually unfit them for a business in which a man
must sow many days before he can reap. And even
if a man is constitutionally suited to be “on the
land,’’ there is only toil and tribulation ahead of
him if he goes green to the work. Wheat does
not sell at 4s. a bushel these days; the greater part
of the cultivated land has been more or less impo- •
verished by ill-treatment; and success in the busi-
ness calls for skill and scientific training, as it
does in almost every branch of industry, ihe
difference, however, between farming and most
other employments is that farming is, to a
very great extent, a one-man industry. In other
branches it is sufficient for one man in every two
or three to have special skill; for the others there
is the rough work to do. But in farming, where
success calls for just as much skill as in any busi-
ness, practically every man (certainly every man
who has his own farm) needs to be a craftsman ;
so the cry of “ Go on the land ” becomes a more
hopeless solution of the chronic unemployed pro-
blem than ever. There is this to be said, certainly :
whereas the unskilled, untrained man, set by
himself to make, say, a watch, would fail com-
pletely, and would produce nothing even m the
shape of a watch, a man similarly unskilled and
untrained, set on a plot of land, often grows some-
thing. But what a life to condemn him to ! On
the Bathurst experimental farm the average of
the crops, grown as trained, skilled men
would grow them, was 24 g bushels; in the
surrounding country the average on the
farms, including those of men untrained
and unskilled, was seven bushels. That was the
average ; consider w’hat the poorest must have
been. It seems certain some men must have
reaped Is. where the skilled man reaped 13s.
Hardly another class of work would show such a
vast difference between the real earnings of
skilled and unskilled workmen. And yet this is
the business that is supposed to be suitable for
any “dosser” who happens to come along; and
having this business, the Freetrader says there is
no need to provide any other to absorb our un-
skilled population.

Fire stick Civilisation.
Evert few days N.S. Wales breaks out into a
heated discussion as to whether it has or has not
more than, say, 50 acres of suitable land available
for settlement. Premier Carruthers, talking up
North in his usual sloppy fashion, said that, pass-
ing between two townships, he had “found over
and over again replicas of Coolangatta on a large
scale,” Coolangatta being one of the rich patches
on the N.S.W. South Coast grabbed m the early
days by the Hay-Berry family. Then Lands
Minister Ashton, who isn’t quite as sloppy as his
chief, unrolled the maps and found that all the
alleged “replicas” w'ere collared just as thoroughly
as the original Coolangatta itself ; and it isn t
much satisfaction to the man who hasn’t got
any land to be told that somebody else has
enough for a dozen. Thereupon Ashton re-
iterated his four-months-old statement that
in all N. S. Wales (area, 198,638,000 acres; popu-
lation, 1,490,000) there isn’t available one single
block of public land large enough to settle 20
families. There certainly is the Dorrigo Scrub—
but that is not ready for settlement, because it is
timbered country ; yet, as it is apparently the only
large block of suitable State land available, there
is a cheerful demand to get rid of the timber-
burn it, destroy it, do anything with it; only get rid
of it. It is valuable timber, and N.S. Wales hasn’t
any too much valuable timber left, as the result
of wholesale destruction ; but even valuable tim-
ber isn’t to count. And all the time a wandering
Premier can find, “ over and over again, replicas
of probably the best agricultural estate in Aus-
tralia, and in spite of these, empty estates,
the fool-country is still expected to hack
and burn its valuable timber assets in order to
make room for another handful of farmers. A
progressive land-tax would open the lands already
cleared to the waiting farmers, and save to the
State a splendid asset; but evidently this country
prefers to hack and burn.

A Harbor Board.
Rockhampton Harbor Board is Queensland’s
prime idiot of the hour. It has been in existence
about ten years, and, though it has contrived to
go almost broke, it hasn’t improved the Fitzroy
River two cents. First it purchased a job lot of
dredging machinery—portion of a cast-off plant
which had been piled up for years somewhere
on the Yarra. It was to be a suction
dredge, and the hull was to be constructed
in Rockhampton, at a cost of another £5OOO.
After, in one way and another, spending £14,000
on the great bargain, the Board towed it gingerly
down the river, where it promptly broke down.
The paint which the Board put on the pipes
wasn’t strong enough to stand the strain. It was
overhauled, it was patched, it was painted some
more, and finally it was hung up to rot with the
other great permanent and reproductive assets.
After that Rockhampton Harbor Board splashed
out into a purchase (for £50,000) of one of
Lindon Bates’ suction dredges. Bates, an
enterprising Yankee who was on his w r ay back to
America from India, dropped into Queensland as
he passed, talked dredge, and unloaded £200,000
worth upon the State. Anyway, he got £200,000
for what he unloaded. And again Rockhampton
and Queensland didn’t find out until it was too
late that this wasn’t the sort of dredge they
wanted ; so another mass of decaying dredge is
reckoned amongst the national, assets. Finally
Rockhampton saw that what it needed was a
training wall, and the contract it let to railway-
man Willcocks landed it in a worse mess than
ever. The contract was for £32,000; and when
£13,000 was spent it dawned upon the Board that
£60,000 wouldn’t see the job through. Old surveys
had been relied upon, and Willcocks was dump-
ing stone (at so much per yard), into 25ft. of water
where the Board had reckoned upon 10ft. Trouble,
arose at this point, and the contractor took las
case to Q. Supreme Court, but mercifully agreed
to a quiet settlement during the first day s pro-
ceedings. The Board paid Willcocks £BOOO odd,
most of which was for his plant—more plant!-
And now the Board proposes to go on with the
work itself; and on its past record that is a
decision which ought to simply fill Rockhampton
up to the chin with confidence.
The Land.
“ Dick” writes :
Your two or three hundred thousand readers in Maori-
land will feel much refreshed by the statement (8., 8/2/’O6)
that you have always favored land nationalisation, and
will fight for a sane, comprehensive scheme whenever. the
chance offers. Permit me no\v to point out that Tub
Bulletin is the only forum in which the democrats of these
States can discuss this vital question, and to express my
hope that you will give a small proportion of your space
to it even in the press of questions that are actually
before the Legislatures. Nationalisation is not before
the Maoriland Legislature, but it is very much in the
minds of Maorilanders, because we have seen half meas-
ures tested by the comparatively advanced land legisla-
tion of the past 15 years, and have reluctantly realised
their futility. * , , ~ ..
Your reference to a “ sane ” scheme of land nationalisa-
tion possibly indicates your doubt that the total btate
resumption I advocated was madness. I submit, how-
ever, that to continue on the present course is the leal
madness—sheer midsummer madness. Private owner-
ship of land wrecked the Roman Empire, and is the root-
cause of the conditions which are exhibiting in switt
succession in the British Empire the worst characteristic
symptoms of Roman decay'. The proletariat refuses to
breed, luxury saps the citizens’ fighting force, the Senate
enlists Barbarians, makes treaties with them, tamely
suffers their insults. Yet the British race is as virile and
martial as ever, where it is raised under tolerable condi-
tions. At the outskirts of the empire, plentiful land delay-
ing for a generation or two the ill-effects of private owner-
ship it produces the bush-bred Australian, the up-country
Maorilander, the Canadian of the farm and the forest.
No need to despair of the Republic ; great need to
educate the citizen, and make him vote private owner-
ship out of existence. . ...
We have now the Federal Premier s promise that the
tariff shall be adjusted—presumably in the direction of
right and reason. Should we have got so far but for The
Bulletin’s steady advocacy of fiscal reform ? Not much,
I reckon. Let us have your help, and “ a sane, compre-
hensive scheme” of land nationalisation will be ham-
mered out in the minds of the people, before ever Parlia-
ment sees a land nationalising Bill.

Brummagem on the Rhine.
It is with jewellery as it is with iron, machinery,
and woollens. Australia should not only supply
itself but should be an exporter of the manu-
factured article. It has gold and silver and most
of the precious stones (of some precious stones
almost a monopoly); yet it imports jewellery by
the ton. Worse, both employers and employees
united in declaring to the Tariff Commission that
fraud, deliberate fraud, is rampant. Jewellery is
made in Germany, sent to Freetrade London
to be hall-marked, then returned to be
“loaded” with solder. London Hall will
not stamp an article under nine carats, not
considering it gold at all; yet barrow-loads of
imported hall-marked stuff assay only six or seven
carats. And, in competition, some of the Austra-
lian makers have been driven to be as bad.
Shappebe, president of the Vic. Manufacturing
Jewellers Association, swore he had handled Aus-
tralian-made articles stamped 18 carat, but which
were made of brass. Shappeee and other repre-
sentatives of the trade are importers as well as
manufacturers; yet they said they were Protec-
tionists by conviction. To manufacture here
would pay them as well, directly, as to import;
but as it would benefit the country more, it
would indirectly pay them better. The duty
they asked was 40 per cent, as against the exist-
ing 25 per cent. America levies 60 per cent.
Then, having got Protection and the trade, it
would be a comparatively easy matter, by estab-
lishing an Australian hall-mark and a system of
inspection (for which the trade is prepared to
pay), and by registering the brand of every maker,
to safeguard the public, as it is obvious no foreign
hall-mark can possibly safeguard it.

A Moderate Protectionist Manufacturer.
The new progressive policy of the Vic. Chamber
of Manufactures has moved brother It. G.
M’Cutoheon to an explosion of Tory wrath which
will probably prove fatal to him at the next State
election. M’CUTCHEON, who misrepresents St.
Kilda in the State Assembly, was one of the few
hopelessly incurable members of the Chamber,
and when he learned the other morning from his
beloved Argus that President Atkins had invited
three of the Political Labor Council to meet three
manufacturers on terms of amity, he(M‘CuTCHEON)
refused to countenance the shocking proposal.
He immediately wrote to the secretary of the
degenerate Chamber resigning his membership.
The letter was not published next day, so
M'Cutcheon fatuously sent a copy to the press
that the public might know what manner of per-
son he is. The simple candor of M’Cutciieon is
quite pathetic :
I am a manufacturer, a moderate Protectionist, and am
desirous that any important tariff anomalies should be
rectified as speedily as possible in Australian interests.
But, for reasons of general public policy, I am opposed to
'any alliance for the above purpose with the Trades Hall
(or the Political Labor Council) such as is asked for now
by the president of the Chamber of Manufactures.
This fully explains what was the matter with the
Chamber in the long years that are fled. The
M’Cutcheon idea was the matter—a bloated
enmity to labor organisation was the matter. And
though the Labor policy in Victoria is thoroughly
Protectionist, there are still a few pig-headed,
Labor-hating Protectionists of the M'Cutcheon
type, who call themselves moderates and secede
from the Protectionist movement rather than
shake hands with the Trades Hall men on the
question of tariff reform.

“ Our Glorious Principles.”
A featuke of the Australian Socialist, who calls
himself a Socialist and belongs to an avowedly
Socialistic body, is his dreaminess and impractica-
bility. At the recent Labor Conference the
Socialist, no matter on what subject he spoke,
was apparent after his first half-dozen sentences.
He was as dour and as deadly in earnest as was
ever a Scotch Covenanter sitting out on a wet
hillside for four hours listening to an interminable
preacher droning an eternal sermon, and, like the
ancient Scotchbyterian, he was hopelessly un-
compromising. He was firmly opposed to land
taxation —it was a mere palliative which would
help to postpone the day of complete land
nationalisation. Factory legislation and Arbitra-
tion Courts were a snare ; the only course was to
make the factories belong to the people ; and so
on, right down the list. And with pitiful earnest-
ness he besought the Conference to hold fast
to “the glorious principles,” which were worth
living for, and were worthy of dying for, if neces-
sary ; and to have no compromises, no alliances,
only a grim, unyielding struggle for all or nothing.
It was sublime, but pitifully unpractical, and
tlie eminently practical EEughes attacked it
with pitiless logic. He admired the devotion to
principle—he hoped the party would always haveit,
and that the party would die the day it lost it. But
the solemn fact of the position was that the Labor
party had to appeal for votes to people who never
had enough clothes to wear, and not always enough
food to eat. These voters wanted material benefits,
and when one man came along with a load of
glorious principles to be realised in the
dim future, and another with a practical
scheme for giving them an extra shilling
a day in the immediate present —they fol-
lowed the man with the sordid practical
scheme. Therefore, he wanted the party, while
keeping its glorious principles, to put its visible
practical good works to the front. The Australian
Socialist generally comes to see things by a pro-
cess of democratic evolution, and to recognise
that the real work of practical Socialism consists
in grappling with the multitude of evils and
abuses one by one. The first stage is to provide
regular employment at a decent living wage for
everyone who wants it. And the first step to that
stage is to have a fiscal system by which tbe country
will make as nearly as possible.evcryfihing it uses.
When the proposed earthly Eden has thus been
fenced against theoutsideworla-whenitisassured
that what has been gamed cannot be lost by any
foreign invasion of cheap goods or_cheap
steady progress can be made towards the idea _
securing to worker, overseer, thinker and 01 gams
the full products of their respective labor but
the progress must be step by step. I heoretically
a vast and ornate building can be put up in a day,
provided enough workmen are employed, practi-
cally it can’t. It is easy enough to work out the
sum on paper: “If 1000 men can build a palace
in 600 working days of eight hours each, how
many men could build the same palace in one
minute?” Answer, arrived at by rule of three,
“ 288 000 000.” But we know that they coulan t,
despite rule of three. Theoretically, too, the
great fabric of a re-organised social system can be
built in a year, if enough earnestness is shown by
politicians. Practically the “ glorious principles
of Socialism must be content to win their way
little by little.
Various excitable but incompetent persons in
Melbourne have been flogging themselves into the
notion that they are being villainously treated,
and threaten to enter on a campaign ot passive
resistance ” —whatever that may mean. J. hey are
mostly small shopkeepers, and under the new Vic.
Factories and Shops Act they must dose at 6 p.m.
most of the week, and at 1 p.m. on either Wednes-
day or Saturday. One of them, who runs a furni-
ture shop, expressed her “passive resistance by
hanging a placard on her shutters—‘ Must close
at 6 Black Thursday. When will working men
buy their goods?” The Melbourne working
man is evidently such an affluent person
that he can’t possibly buy all the furni-
ture he wants in one night a week, but
must gallop out every evening and acquire large
quantities of tables, overmantels, and .other
passementerie. Another “passive resister,
dwelling in a quiet city street, sells tops, fire-
works, photos, of Greek studies, etc., and the
absence of the large crowd that nightly attended to
buy his wares moves him to great grief. It sucn
necessary businesses as those of the baker, butc er,
grocer, etc., can be carried out without difficulty
between 8 a.m. and 6 p.m., surely the ornamental
ones can do likewise ; and fruiterers, lolly-shops,
etc., are already exempted. Somebody ought to
take these small but obtrusive middlemen into a
quiet place and explain to them that they are
little use and less ornament; that the community
already has about three times as many shops as it
needs : that as they produce absolutely nothing,
they only make life harder for those who do ; and
that if the aggregate volume of business hitherto
done in a trade in 12 hours can be compressed into
eight, there will be a saving of labor, gas, and
wear-and-tear, not to mention feather dusters.
The action of Freeman and Wallace against
NSW Postal Department reopens the questmn
as’to whether the freedom of the press can be dis-
associated from freedom of the post. Here, as m
the case of Tattersall’s sweeps, we have postal
authorities assuming the authority of haw, and
treating as illegal a business which should be sup-
pressed by direct law action or not interfered
with at all. If Freeman and Wallace are com-
mitting a crime with their business, how comes it
that no criminal proceedings are instituted against
them ’ And if there is no crime in the business,
why should the Post Office refuse to deliver letters
to them ? The Bulletin wouldn t argue for a
moment in favor of allowing any harmful business
to be carried on—if it is proved to be harmful.
But, according to the High Court decision, the
Postmaster-General has absolute and unchallenge-
able authority to stop any busmess-by refusing
it postal facilities—that he thinks is harmful. It s
not necessary for him to prove his case; his
opinion has only to be expressed to have the force
of Law. It is too arbitrary a power for any man
to have.
A tannery witness lately told the Tariff Com-
mission that he had noticed marine-store collectors
gathering cardboard, and had followed them to
boot-factories. Next he produced a boot-heel ot
six “lifts,” four of them made of cardboard.
After that he swore that it was the importation of
shoddy cardboard boots that compelled the local
manufacturer to use brown paper rubbish ; it he
didn’t he couldn’t compete in the matter of price.
This is what the Freetrade Labonte drives his
fellow-worker to in the effort to placate his great
god, Cheapness. And then the Australian work-
man, himself “out” half his time, thinks he is
buying cheap leather, while in reality he is buying
dear cardboard-sown probably with the seeds of
pneumonia or pleurisy. And all m order that the
foreigner shall have the job Australia badly wants
itself!
“ T.S.” : It looks like the beginning of a new era
when we read that the British House of Coinmons
has resolved, by 348 votes to 110, in favor of pay-
ment of members. Hitherto the House of Com-
mons has been the preserve of the rich, it has
suited the rich man better to be a member with-
out payment, and to drop in and legislate a little
when he felt inclined, than to have payment of
members and to lose his seat to some beastly
Labor member. Besides, the House of Commons
is a useful institution to the rich man ; it has
existed for a very long time to maintain the law ot
primogeniture, to look after the rights of the
Eldest Son, to keep the big estates and the big
fortunes together and the mass of the people in
poverty, and to see that no gang of ruffians passed
any laws which would tend to a steady diffusion
of wealth. The House of Commons doesn t
even provide seating accommodation for nearly
all its members, so little does it expect
from them any active attention to their
duties, but they' have always been expected to
roll up and oppose any attempt to promote the
active diffusion of wealth. Now it begins to look
as if a new House of Commons might be possible
—a smaller House, in which payment of members
will give the poor man almost as good a chance as
the rich one, provided he can show equal merits.
And if payment of members, comes anything may
come in time—even Protection, a graduated land-
tax, a division of estates among all the children
of the late owner and consequently a steady
cutting up of the land, perhaps even the abolition
of th e blessed House of Lords.
