The Curse of Competition.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
The musical and elocutionary competitions were con-
tinued yesterday. —ltem from everywhere.
The halls are lit in the hours of night,
And the wondering stars in the heavens twinkle,
While the maidens chant, and the boys recite,
And pianoforte solos thump and tinkle;
Genius in embryo buds and spreads,
And Art (with a capital) finds fruition ;
For the only thought in our children’s heads
Is to win first prize in a competition.
They spout their stories the livelong day—
The Revenge goes down in a style time-worn ;
The good news hurries from Ghent to Aix,
And Poppa’s Letter to shreds is torn ;
From Enoch Auden's wonderful isle
The Hesperus drifts in a wrecked condition,
While happy fathers and mothers smile
At Willie’s success in the competition.
“ Ora pro nobis ’’—the maidens sing
In homes suburban (we pray for them) ;
And wooden cottages nightly ring
With hosannas high from Jerusalem;
Essays are hatching in every nook
In numbers to stagger a statistician
(They mostly allude to Captain Cook),
And they all are bound for the competition.
When we were youngsters we used to play
At peg-top, marbles, or hare-and-hounds;
Climb trees, steal apples, or spend the day
On gravelly cricket and football grounds.
But the small boy now is a different thing—
He’s a inusicial monster, a weird logician,
Who will strum, write, argue, recite or sing
For the sake of a prize in a competition.
Oh, bury me, bury me, bury me far
From halls where these echoes loud are chiming;
Let me lie in a grave where no essays are,
And never reaches the sound of rhyming.
Then when I go to my final bourne,
If I in mercy escape perdition,
And angels are singing, I only yearn
That they will not sing in a competition.
VIC.

SYDNEY ' W
ened by the falling of masses of literature in
which Ardill, secretary for the Public Morals
Ass., states that his Ass. wishes the Council to
E revent the sinful pastime of dancing getting a
old on the borough. He usually encloses a
model bye-law which, he believes, will scotch
the reptile, if not kill it outright. Its effect
is to prohibit dancing assemblies at which a
charge is made for admission. The bye-law
provides, however, that the Mayor may,
on the special application of any respectable rate-
payer, grant permission in writing for the allow-
ance of dancing for one day or one night only,
from time to time, and specifying the place and
time (not being a Sunday, Xmas Day or Good
Friday). This, of course, leaves the mayor a loop-
hole if any person with a philanthropic mind and
two frisky legs should wish to organise a ball in
aid of the funds of any of Ardill’s 14 societies.
The Bulletin is waiting to receive the first
balance-sheet of the Public Morals Ass. Some
years ago it was seized with a desire to see the
balance-sheets of a number of Mr. Ardill’s chari-
ties. Looking back down the vista of time it
can’t remember having met with much luck over
those balance-sheets.
♦
The Victorian Pure Food Act is a remarkably
good Act, but it has struck the usual snag—the
wretched and useless honorary “beak” won’t
enforce it. There is nothing on earth much more
useless or more wonderful than the Victorian
honorary beak. When he finds a swagman sleep-
ing out in a perfectly honest, healthy, respect-
able way, because he has been unlucky for
a few weeks in his search for work, he
gives him six months in a stuffy cell.
When, the other day, he convicted the mate
of an American ship of assaulting and biting
a sailor who tried to prevent him stamping on a
helpless lunatic, he fined the ruffian 10s., and he
lately assessed the case of an oft-convicted wife-
beater, who had got close up to murder, at two or
three pounds. Therefore, when he strikes a bad
case of adulterated milk—about the worst
kind of adulteration, seeing that a very
large proportion of infantile mortality is
caused by adulterated milk, and this kind of
adulteration is closer up to murder than even the
average—it is quite in keeping with the dreadful
character of the Victorian honorary beak that the
fine is sometimes just Is. The law allows £2O for
the first offence, £5O for the second, £lOO and im-
prisonment for the third, but the honorary horror
makes it Is. The law also makes special provision
for the wide publication of the names of persons
convicted of adulteration, but the honorary person,
in his deep sympathy with the meaner kinds of
crime, usually refuses to enforce this section of the
law at all. One of the chief curses of Victoria is
its honorary magistracy, with its deep sympathy
for the criminal —provided he is only a swindler
or a brutal assault rowdy, and isn’t a poor but
reputedly honest man who has committed the
crime of sleeping in the open air. At giving 12
months to an old woman charged with insufficient
means of support he has no rival, but in other
respects he is an unmitigated disaster, not to say
a disgrace.
«.
The Commission that is inquiring into the Kil-
kenny-cat administration of the N.S.W. Railways
is getting down very close to blank perjury. At
least Mr. Kneeshaw, of the Sydney tramways,
said he saw Mr. Kirkcaldie open Mr. Oliver’s
drawer in Mr. Oliver’s absence, and take out and
read a paper, and Mr. Kirkcaldie declared with
tears that he never did anything of the kind. It
was a case of deciding between the damp witness
and the dry testifier, for Mr. Kneeshaw didn’t
weep while giving his evidence. The curious
feature of the case hereabouts is that though Mr.
Kneeshaw said Mr. Fehon was present when
Mr. Kirkcaldie explored Mr. Oliver’s drawer,
the Commission seems to have closed with a
bang without Mr. Fehon being asked to
give his opinion one way or the other. Judge
Rogers, who runs the inquiry, is reported as hav-
ing remarked that neither Mr. Kneeshaw nor
another person “ recommended themselves to him
as witnesses.” Perhaps, however, it was Mr. Knee-
shaw’s hurried aspect that unfavorably impressed
Judge Rogers, and he might have been only allud-
ing to the personal aspect of things. Judge Rogers
himself always looks as if his manners had been
oiled, while Mr. Kneeshaw is mostly too busy for
small amenities. It is quite understandable that
Mr. Kneeshaw’s lack of mild circuitousness might
not recommend itself to J udge Rogers.

From W. Webster, M.H.R.
N.S.W. Lands Minister Ashton is reported to have said
the other day (re clause 17 of the 1905 Land Act) —“ The
Minister has no power to prevent the board from modify-
ing the residence conditions up to five years ; but he has
unlimited power to further modify them, and to extend the
term.” Tnis is a perpetuation of that system of legisla-
tion which was wholly responsible for the Lands scandals.
Similar powers enabled the Minister to grant improve-
ment leases under the ’95 and ’99 Acts, and to authorise
the transfer and traffic in settlement leases, &c., and yet
the lessons have had no effect on legislators. Without
such Ministerial power, the land agent, and
more particularly the political land agent, would find
his occupation gone ; for it is when an appeal
is necessary to tlje Minister that the influence of the
agent is seen. He who can pay will reach the court of
appeal; he who will not or cannot pay, goes without a
hearing. Thus even an honest Minister cannot fairly
adjudicate under such conditions ; and what may happen
if the Minister be not above suspicion ? Bight through
our Land Acts and their administration such powers have
been open to abuse, and until our Lands administration is
either left in the hands of the boards or appeal court, or
removed altogether from political influence; it is hopeless
to expect equity or justice. Mr. Carruthkrs has fathered
this particular non-residential proposal, yet he, when a
budding Minister for Lands (free, I suppose, from the
influence of land agents), said he would insist on per-
petual residence. If the land were unoccupied for a cer-
tain period each year, a person finding it so unoccupied
should have the right to enter and take it up. Why this
change ? Why?

Robert Brough’s health is still a long way
less satisfactory than it would be if the matter
were left to a popular vote, and pretty often in
the last week he has had to stand out of the cast
of “Little Mary,” his place being taken by E. B.
Payne, the fine old ratty chemist of the nights
when Brough is on deck. Under these depressing
circumstances, “Little Mary” is to be taken off
at the end of this week and “ Dr. Wake’s Patient”
substituted. The Broughs appear in this—
Mrs. as well as Robert, of affectionate memory.
** * *
A lot of the essential oil of childhood
remains to lubricate the mental joints of middle
and old age. A man loves to mislay his mind, so
to speak, and is pleased with a feather and
tickled with a straw (writer is here indebted for a
word or two to poet Pope, and, worse luck, he’s
the only one of writer’s creditors who can’t come
round to collect). So when you give him pictures
that are almost alive, he’ll show his teeth in a joy-
ful grin all the evening, and go home to bed
smiling, and laugh in his sleep. The oft-men-
tioned West’s Pictures and the Brescians
opened at Sydney Palace on Saturday
evening, and the “ house full ” placard was early
flaunted downstairs. The Biograph show begins
the programme, and persons who have the globe-
trotting disease, yet can only afford to wander
inexpensively in their minds, can, by calling on
this instrument, save their money and take their
choice of routes. It throws in Venice and the
Carnival and the Bridge of Sighs, the Pope and
his Noble Guard, and the fat little bambinos of
Rome, and supplies kings, wild beasts, volcanoes,
mountains, dogs, rivers, crowds, jungles, prawns
and cataracts, till it seems like extortion to take
so much of the grand tour for the money.
As for the Brescians —a group of eight performers
wearing more or less Italian costume most of the
time—the sentimental section of the audience
wants to weep on their eight necks by the time it
has listened to their singing of sweet old favorites
such as “Sweet Spirit Hear My Prayer.” Some
of Saturday’s audience confided to their neigh-
bors that they had first heard that in eighteen
forty something, and wished they might hear it
every day for the rest of their lives. Scotch and
Irish airs, violin solos, humorous monologues and
character sketches make up the rest of an attrac-
tive and first-class drawing-room entertainment.

“ While London Slept ” there was a sud-
den incursion of Australian politicians. A circus
arrived, and George Reid remarked, genially,
“ Here.we are again,” and everyone was convulsed
with laughter, which was repeated when he said
it again, the seriousness of his face vainly trying to
conceal the rich humor of the remark, “Yes, here
we are again.” J. 0. Watson came on the scene,
and the sanctity of the marriage tie vanished
in a puff of smoke, and ladies in tight
tights and loose morals appeared. Then the
flames of Smithfield were lit by Bill
Wilks, and Bruce Smith fanned them furiously,
proudly claiming, that he never had kept a nigger
who was a Papist. Austin Chapman went to
Rome and imposed telephone toll rates. Where-
upon the virtuous heroine seized the telephone
wire and escaped from the flames of Smithfield,
and virtue was vindicated, crime confounded, and
they lived happily ever afterwards.
* . * * *
The above is as clear an impression of
“ When London Sleeps ’’—the melodrama revived
at Sydney Royal on Saturday by William
Anderson’s Co.—as could be arrived at after fairly
close study. It may conflict with other views of
the play. If so, that is the play’s fault for being
so elusive as to its meaning. And in this case the
play’s not the thing. “ When London Sleeps ”is
just like any other marble in the Andersonian
barrel of fits and starts. It has its crimes
and creeps, its shocks and its Noachian jokes,
and comes to a happy end about 11
p.m. like hundreds of others of its type.
What was most important to the audience
on Saturday was the return of Miss Eugenie
Duggan, Miss Mabel Lane, Miss Georgie Leigh-
ford, and Miss Ethel Buckley, to picture again
sober virtue and gaudy vice; of Harry Quealy to
be funny man in general, and George dross
and H. O. Willard to represent the opposite roles
in ethics. Melodrama, plain frank melodrama,
has come back to Sydney, and the crowd which
likes that sort of fare warmly welcomes the return.
But the next production will be somewhat dif-
ferent—“ Camille,” with Miss Duggan in the
name part, a role in which she is new to Sydney.
* sfc * *
“The Shop Girl,” at Her Majesty’s, Syd-
ney, is now in its second edition. George Lauri
has discovered some new jokes, and Fred Leslie
has been given a chance to show what those
amazing legs of his can do. One other change
has been made some nights lately, but for another
sort of reason ; Miss Clifton falling sick, her
rather heavy part has been taken by Miss Katie
Potter. Looking at the matter in a cold, calm,
superior way, “ The Shop Girl ” doesn’t deserve
it; yet it is doing remarkably good business. It
was easier to turn up late and find an empty
perch at “ Veronique ” than at this later frivolity ;
and that’s a pretty sad admission to have to make
about the city of Sydney.
** * #
There is an outbreak of girl at the Tivoli
—singing girl, dancing girl, girl who ties herself
into knots and sings while she is untying herself,
girl who knots herself and doesn’t sing, girl with,
a freak voice, and girl too numerous to mention.
Among them are two of some Australian interest
—Brightie Smith, who has been “kidding” in.
Blandholtodrama and has blossomed unexpectedly
into “serio” business; and the widow of late
conductor Leon Caron, who sings “ Dear Little-
Shamrock ” and such. But the feature of the bill,
is still the trick-cycling turn of Lotto, Lilo, and.
Otto ; lately Lotto (if it isn’t Otto) has been up-
ending a dachshund sort of tandem built for
three, and racing round the stage perched on top-
of the shivery thing. But the bicycle football,
with its sweeping waves of low comedy, pleases
the house better even than the more remarkable
feats of juggling with the wheel.
** * *
Wirth Brothers’ Circus, now on its way
from the west, will open in Alfred Park, Sydney,
on the evening of March 31st. This is an entirely
new show, manned with fresh people. A fair
portion of the time will be taken up by aerial per-
formances ; those which take place on the invi-
sible wire are said to be somewhat weird in their
effect. The elephant herd has increased to eight,
including a baby. The young crocodile that George
Wirth used to lead round on a chain has got too
big for that sort of thing, and has been relegated
to a cage. Altogether there have been quite a
number of additions to the menagerie, which is
now under the care of a new tamer, Mons. Wilde.
Mons. looks quite tame, in spite of his name, and
is a pleasant little Frenchman of tubular propor-
tions.
“ The Fatal Wedding,” on its demerits as
a narrative, is only fit to be compared with the
bygone ’cellodrama of Van Biene, entitled
“The Broken Melody,” which came from Eng-
land. The present piece of foolishness comes
from America, and is a kiddie-drama in the same
degree that the other was a ’cellodrama. It gets
the chances of success from the exceptionally
clever child, Maisie Posner —who, coming to light
in the second act, plays an important part
with an air of naturalness and a quick
sense of humor —and from an irrelevant party
of children, ranging from nippers to damsels
of uncertain age, who are an object lesson in stage
management. Some of them give a “ turn ; ” all
of them have been taught to make themselves use-
ful in this novel scene ; and the general effect is
refreshing. A few remarks on the grown-up
section of the new company at Melbourne Royal
had better be postponed for a week to give Miss
Dina Cooper time to improve on acquaintance.
Miss Cooper is the lurid lady who performed an
unintentionally funny character m an uncon-
sciously funny manner on the first night. This is
intended as a week’s warning to her.
** * *
At Her Majesty’s (Melbourne) the con-
verted burglaress continues to win the affection
of her political gentleman. It is hard to say why
Monsieur Sylvaine should get so struck on Leah
Kleschna that he can’t leave her to her maiden
meditations among the cabbages of her native dis-
trict. But the fact remains that she mashed him
once and for all when she broke into his house and
had a few minutes’ conversation on the subject of
crime. Perhaps she won his heart by saying
“D —n you !” Up to then the girls had smiled
sweetly on him, and when Leah swore at him he
got an indescribable sensation which left a strange
yearning behind it.
* * *
“There and Back ’’ opened its innings at
the Princess’s on Saturday night, and within the
limits of its capacity the harmless farcical comedy
scored freely. You get the usual escapade of the
usual pair of married men, Henry Lewson and
William Waring, who have received medical
orders to run over to New York and back. They
are model benedicts nowadays ; but during their
bachelorhood they had, unknown to each other,
frivolled not wisely but too well with a lydy of
the music-halls, one Marie Antoinette Smith. This
light of other days arrives unannounced at the
Waring domicile to borrow ten pounds from the
master of the house. She finds Lewson there too,
and gets another tenner from himforoldsake’ssake.
Both men are in mortal dread of their wives meet-
ing Miss Smith, and they keep one another in the
dark regarding that vision of the past. The
ghost seems to be laid satisfactorily, and the
haunted men are packing their traps in peace,
when a letter comes to each from Marie Antoin-
ette, announcing that she also has taken her
passage for America. The horrified husbands,
rather than travel in such compromising company,
decide to give up America, and go instead to
Scotland to visit a mutual crony, Jock McCrea
(Gregan McMahon). Not being able to explain
their sudden reluctance re the original tour, they
allow their wives to believe they are bound for
Yankeeland, as arranged. After a tender parting
from the two deceived ladies, the fugitives set out
for Scotland, where a shock awaits them.
Marie Antoinette is there before them ! Jock
McOrae has persuaded her to marry him in-
stead of sailing for America. The complications
consequent upon this encountersoon unravel them-
selves, for the new Mrs. McCrea, anxious to cover
the tracks of Marie Antoinette Smith, is a wasp
robbed of her sting. The visitors have a good time
among the bonnie heather, ye ken, and return to
their wives on the date their ship is due to arrive
from New York. In the meantime, however, the
lorn grass-widows have been on the move,
too. The old bachelor uncle of one of
them arrives suddenly from Bourke (Australia)
and persuades his niece and her chum to accom-
pany him to Scotland to look at a Highland pro-
perty. This jaunt brings the wives and husbands
into the same Scotch inn, though they do not
meet, for Uncle and ladies (Maud Chetwynd and
Florence Redfern) are suddenly called back to
London by a telegram announcing the foundering
of the ship on which the husbands are supposed
to be returning from America. Wailing and
tearing of hair ensues and continues until the
absentees, unaware of the fate of the ship, arrive
home with tales of theatricals on board, and
generally tangle themselves up in a maze of
mystery.
* * * m
“ There and Back ” is pleasing nonsense,
hinging on that stage chestnut, the abject fear of
husbands in peril of wifely anger re pre-nuptial
philanderings. J. J. Dallas, a host in himself,
sings a frisky rendering of “Love’s Young
Dream,” and Florence Lloyd sings, too, and
dances, and altogether gets a move on that
hackneyed stage heroine, Marie Antoinette Smith.
Maud Chetwynd is natural, vulgar, bouncing
and rather Austrylian as one of the grass-widows.
Gregan M‘Mahon does a laughable caricature of
a Scotchman, and Robert, an argumentative
Caledonian servant, is convincing in the hands of
Fred Cambourne. Norah Delaney, as Jane, a
London slavey, answers for her share of the fun.
** * *
Miss Beatrice Miranda, a charming young
artist, who is not much longer for this country,
will be the star of an Italian opera performance at
the Princess’s on April 7. She is to appear in acts
from “Rigoletto,” “Lucia,” and “Martha,”
with the assistance of a small co. and a large
orchestra.
* * *
A good deal of reminiscent interest
attached to Mrs. Palmer’s recent concert at Mel-
bourne Town Hall, described in the local press as
a “benefit” concert, organised by a “committee,”
although it was managed on ordinary business
lines by a member of the staff at Allan’s. The
name of Mrs. Palmer must have been a greater
attraction to the audience—a fairly numerous one
—than that of Mrs. Gilbert W ilson, her daughter
from Queensland, a singer sufficiently matured to
excite comment on the appearance of mamma,
who has aged very little within the past dozen
years. Mrs. Palmer is said to have rivalled Armes
Beaumont in platform popularity, when the tenor
was at his best, and she is still able to command
an encore. The remains of her voice are more
effective than a good many modern fresh
young warbles. R. S. Smythe affirms that he
first heard Rosina (Mrs. Palmer) and her late
sister Fanny Carandini (Lady Morland) at the
Mechanics’ Institute, Ballarat, in 1869, but if we
accept a lady vocalist as being no older than she
sings, it is evident that Smythe’s memory ought
to be ashamed of itself.
** * *
At the Town Hall, a short revival of
“Living London ” will date from Thursday of the
present week, and on the following Thursday the
first of the Andrew Black concerts in Melbourne
is to eventuate. A musical farce has broken out
at the Bijou, and a Flying Machine is the latest
and breeziest addition to the stock-in-trade at
Princes Court, where the resident lioness has
thoughtfully given birth to twins.
* * *
The re-appearance of manager Rickards
for five nights is happening at Melbourne Opera
House this week, and Friday will be the fifth
night of that virile identity in his latest assort-
ment of songs. The other happenings are a repe-
tition of previous occurrences, the Scottish Meisters
being the newest of them. Last Saturday’s pro-
gramme included an eminent Irish tragedian of
unheard-of fame, who appeared under the special
patronage of St. Patrick, and was said to be in-
spired, but seemingly he had mislaid his inspira-
tion at the critical moment, and the patriotic
fervor of the original Robert Emmet didn’t burn
within his breast.

George Ireland’s forthcoming benefit show
at Melbourne Princess’s will include three acts of
Sheridan Knowles’ play, “The Hunchback,” in
which the veteran made his first re-appearance 50
years ago. One of the lean kind, he carries his
years remarkably well, and when he takes the
stage again as Master Walter, the “ Hunchback ”
aforesaid, he will be, to all intents and purposes,
a man of not more than 45. Old time playgoers
ought to make a muster at George Ireland’s jubilee
ben., if only for the satisfaction of feeling some-
what rejuvenated by his re-appearance. Of the
THE FATAL WEDDING.
Sing a song of Melbourne Royal, where the villains formed
a plan
To part a stainless matron from her spotless married
man,
And The Bulletin reporter, in an agony of grief,
Heard his fellow-creatures roaring at the “comedy re-
lief.”
Sing a song of Melbourne Royal, let the solemn dirgey
strain
Give an adequate expression to the present writer’s pain,
As he sat in dumb amazement at the crudeness of the
plot,
And wondered why its author wasn’t crucified or shot.
But the tears that he was shedding
When he struck “The Fatal Wedding,”
Though so bitter whilst they lasted, had an ending to
their flow:
For there came a little kiddie—
Sure, her parentage is Yiddy—
And the brilliant Yiddy Kiddie brought salvation to the
show.
O, the lurid villain-lady in her satin robe of red !
O, the shrugging of her shoulders and the shaking of her
head 1
O, the idiotic conduct of the other five adults,
As they shaped their course of action to preposterous re-
sults 1
O, those wondrous comic servants doing aggravating
things
To the lurid villain-lady with the nervous pair of wings !
’Twas the sickest-looliing drama human eyes had ever
seen,
Till the kid called “ Little Mother ” took possession of the
scene.
And the plot, so tommy-rotten,
Was forgiven and forgotten
When a swarm of other youngsters, full of unaffected
glee—
Five and twenty, maybe thirty,
Clean and festive, gay and dirty,
Came and treated Little Mother to a glad corroboree.
Soon the actors went on acting, went on. talking high
and low,
And they really didn’t matter since the kid had saved
the show.
They travelled by a tight-rope—but for one it wasn’t
tight,
For the lurid lady cut it, and he tumbled out of sight.
Then the dead man, getting better, robbed the lady of
her life,
So the other woman’s husband never kissed his second
wife;
“The Fatal Wedding” climaxed in the murder of the
bride
By the resurrected dead ’un, who committed suicide.
And the public drew a moral
From the lovely tributes floral
Which were showered upon the ladies—unexpectedly, you
know.
There were tokens, large and many,
For the one who’d earned not any,
And a puny little posy for the kid who saved the show.

Salt for the Children, increases their size.
Salt of the Earth, without a Fault,

Ireland is seemingly the only survivor, and of the

Melbourne (March, 1862). Later in the same
year he was one of the young supporters to Barry
Sullivan and Jefferson in what must have been a
really great series of tragedy and comedy produc-
tions, as regards the acting. Take, for instance,
a “School for Scandal” play bill, dated Sept. 13,
1862, the last night of the season. The first seven
names in the cast are Barry Sullivan, Lambert,
Joseph Jefferson, Robert Heir, Stewart, H. R.
Harwood, and Fitzgerald—and then comes
Ireland as Careless. Evidently he wasn’t as
Careless as he pretended to be on that occasion,
or he wouldn’t look so comparatively fresh and
frisky in his old age.

“ Squidge ” supplies his version of the tale
of George Coppin, deceased
Old George Coppin, after numberless farewells extend-
ing over half a century, made his absolutely final appear-
ance on any stage last week. His life was a history of
ups and downs. He grew up amongst scenery and grease-
paint, and at 15 played in the orchestra of his father’s
theatre. At 17 he struck out on his own account, and
one of his most treasured relics was a portrait of “ George
Coppin at 17 years,” in which he was shown working hard
at his first job—second violin at a provincial theatre.
From the orchestra he climbed on to the stage, and
appeared while still a youth as second low-comedian in a
tenth-rate company playing at Richmond on the Thames.
An engagement at the Queen’s Theatre, London, gained
him some amount of prestige, and, after a successful tour
of the provinces as low-comedian of a fair-class company,
he got an engagement as principal comedian at the Strand
Theatre, London. His pay was very low, and Coppin,
disgusted with the stage, gave it up and went in for busi-
ness. A series of speculations in London lost him the little
money he had saved on the stage, and he took a job as
manager of a store in Glasgow. There he met some hand
bell-ringers and as that form of entertainment had not
been exploited up till then, he scented money in it,
organised a company and got together a decent little store
of cash.
Then he left for Australia and reached Sydney in 1843.
In Sydney Coppin was starred as a comedian, did big
business, made a lot of money, left the stage and opened
a pub. and lost everything. He resolved to return to the
stage, went to Tasmania, organised a company and
opened a theatre in Launceston. He did well and in 1845
transported the company to Melbourne. J. T. Smith,
Melbourne’s famous Mayor, had a theatre in Queen-street
—the Queen's Theatre—but Smith and Coppin couldn’t
come to terms, so the comedian rented a hall behind the
Royal Hotel, in Oollins-street, and opened there. He
bumped Smith from the start and in a few months Smith
capitulated and took Coppin into partnership. He
couldn’t rest, however—within a year he was in Adelaide.
There he ran up an alleged theatre in the ridiculous time
of five weeks and did big business with a company of
which he was the comedian. He sold the theatre,
opened another pub. and started speculating in mining.
In a few years he had again lost all he possessed and
was up to his eyes in debt.
The gold rush broke out in Victoria and Coppin caught
the fever, bought a pick and a shovel, and set out to dig
up a fortune. But the gold didn’t come his way, and he
was offered an engagement and a partnership by Elmes,
owner of a theatre in Geelong. Coppin went back to
Adelaide, fixed up an arragenient with his creditors, and
accepted the Geelong engagement. Money was plentiful,
and he soon collected a good deal of it. Then in 1854,
having paid off all his Adelaide debts, he returned to
England, and tried for an engagement in London. No one
would listen to him for a long time, but at last he was
taken on at the Haymarket. Australia was in every one’s
mouth, and Coppin was starred as “ the Australian Com-
edian.” He went all over England doing well every-
where.
In Birmingham G. V. Brooke and he foregathered, and
he persuaded Brooke to join him in another attack on
Australia. An iron theatre was put together in Man-
chester, sent to Melbourne, and run up in Lonsdale-
street in six weeks. It was called the Olympic, but was
always known as “ the old Iron Pot.” Brooke,
Heir, Younge, Coppin, Fanny Cathcart, and Mrs.
Brougham opened there on Monday, July 30, 1855.
They played, to enormous business, and piled up money
rapidly. The Theatre Royal was built in opposition, but
Brooke and Coppin bought it for £21,000, and at the same
time bought the Cremorne Gardens, on the Yarra Bank.
After spending £60,000 on the two properties they
dissolved partnership. Brooke took the Royal and went
broke a few years later, while Coppin took the Olympic
and the Gardens and started speculating. He promoted
the Melbourne and Suburban railway, backed its bill for
£40,000, and only averted ruin by the sale of the line.
Then he built the Haymarket Theatre, Bourke-street, at
a cost of £ll,OOO. It was next door to what is now the
Eastern Markets. Everything went well for a few years.
Coppin got out the Keans and made a fortune. With
this he started speculating, and in 1864 was again
“ broke.”
He left with the Keans and toured the United States
with them, returning two years later to be received with
great public acclamation. He joined Harwood, Stewart
and Hennings in the management of the Theatre Royal,
but this time fire came along to wreck him. The Hay-
market Theatre was burnt down in 1871, and six months
later the Royal went. Coppin formed the Theatre Royal
Proprietary Association, got a 99 years’ lease and rebuilt
the Theatre Royal, opening it within eight months after
the fire. There he stayed as manager until 1882, when
he had a big farewell appearance (about his 20th) and
gave up active management.
** * *
Adelaide Royal has at last induced the
Moving Pictures to move out of the building, and
has started on its winter season of drama.
Manager Pollock promises that all the attractions
at present in Australia shall come along during
the cold weather. A light start is made with
Anderson’s “Sindbad” pantomime, now on its
second lap of this continent, and still going strong.
Marie Campbell replaces Miss Ravensberg as
hero, and Charles Bates has dropped out, but
Fred Graham is still on deck, and Bert Bailey is
at the wicket. The Tivoli crowd has gone west,
much regretted. Leroy, Talma, and Bosco were
the chief item that kept the house packed, but
the Carmos and others also furnished bright spots
in a programme which had very few dark spots.
“ Squidge ” writes :
George Lansell, the Bendigo Quartz King, died of
sorrow. His one daughter, Edith, the apple of his eye,
has been ill for weeks, just kept alive by strychnine and
oxygen. The old man, heart-broken, hourly expecting
the worst, gradually broke down. Silent and dour
he sat alone and refused to eat. As he became
worse, his daughter, by a strange fate, grew gradually
better, but Lansell could not be made to understand that
his trouble was passing away, and died full of grief.
That mother who was committed for
trial by Coroner Candler, of Melbourne, because
she alcoholised her baby by getting drunk
and staying drunk for months before and after
the child’s birth, was last week acquitted by
God and her country. Yet the medical evidence
went to show that the child’s death was due to a
whole crowd of inflammations in its little inside,
all caused by the big percentage of alcohol in its
mother’s milk.
Holman Hunt’s picture “ The Light of the
World may create in the minds of the easily
deluded a mild summer of multi-colored emotions.
But D.C.L. whisky is the best medium for gently
directing the mental forces into a wise and placid
direction.. D*C.L. being the whisky of the wise
exerts an improving influence upon every sort of
mind— intellectual, commercial or artistic. The
world has many lights and D.C.L. is one of them.*
A BEAR ITEM.
When the French police and soldiers vjent to take an inventory of the sacred properties in
one country church , they found that the devout peasantry had filled the place with three hungry
hears. The authorities escaped with difficulty. The church hear is a new development.

Maud Beatty, the amazingly abundant Maud who
appeared in the Rickards show some time ago, is said to
have been married to the boss policeman at Manila.
Comedian Huntley, remembered in Australia for his
sketches of vapid English Johnnies, has started on the
turf in England, and he will find that no joke. A horse
of his, Thurifer, ran an excellent last in the Surrey
Steeplechase recently.
“ Avon ”: How many of Shakspeare’s plays have
positively never been acted in Australia? I reckon
“Titus Andronicus,” “Troilus and Cressida,” ‘f Henry
V 1.,” “ All’s Well that Ends Well,” and Pericles.” The
following, though very rare on the stage, have been
acted on this continent: “ Love’s Labour’s Lost,”
“ Timon of Athens,” “Measure for Measure,” “Two
Gentlemen of Verona.”
Miss Marion Winchester, an American girl who made
£60,000 out of a deal in sugar, has paid the management
of the Gaiety Theatre £lOOO as a premium, and is singing
a French chansonette and dancing a pas seul in the
revised version of “ The Spring Chicken.” Many estab-
lished artists who were never before out of harness are
doing nothing now on account of the advent of wealthy
dabblers, combined with the present craze of putting
girls from 14 to 17 years of age into the leading musicarv
comedy roles.
Marie Narelle (who hopes to reach Australia by the end.
of May) has been having a talk with President Roosevelt,
who, according to a New York paper, “ discussed with
profound interest matters relating to the social and
financial phases of Australian life.” “ Teddy ” is further
credited with the opinion that the N.S.W. warbler is
“one of the most charming and interesting women he
ever met.” Marie, by the way, was booked to sing at three
separate places in London on St. Pat’s night.
Miss Stewart confessed to a San Francisco interviewer
—who calls her Madame —that she was born in Woolloo-
mooloo. Being so, Madame Stewart is perilously near
the half-hundred. In 1858, Mrs. Guerin, the mother,
lived at 97 Woolloomooloo-street, south side, midway
between Riley and Grown streets. Soon after, the
Stewarts and the Guerins removed to Melbourne. At the
same interview, Madame Stewart introduced her daughter
to the interviewer. “ A little Miss Stewart ? ” quoth he.
“ Oh no 1 ” was the reply, “ Miss Musgrove.”
Mr. Douglas Gerrard, the actor with the fatal beauty
and the fascinating brogue, explains that his break with
the Tittell Brune co. is not due to any unpleasantness,
but his forte is “juvenile lead,” and as plays are being
put on in which the necessities of an actor who is a
specialist in “juvenile lead” have been unfortunately
overlooked, he is not wanted on the job. The great actor
who only plays juvenile leads must be a good deal like
the distinguished author who only writes about giant
gooseberries—his opportunities are limited.
George Coppin made his actual last appearance—at
Melbourne Princess’s—when he was 82J. It was a heavy
undertaking for old George, suffering from gout at the
time, to come on as Mr. Mould in “ Not Such a Fool as He
Looks ” and remain on for an hour or so. Sitting in the
managerial room with Charles Arnold before the ordeal,
he was so agitated (and so bulky) that the floor shook
and the furniture trembled. But he got through his task
all right. The performance was in aid of the Old Colonists’
and the Actors’ Homes, founded by Coppin in 1869 with
State assistance.
The late F. W. Haddon was a good friend to R. S.
Smythe all his days. R.S.S. was around in S’Africaand
Asia at the time that Haddon became editor of the
Argus, but when the Much-Travelled returned to Mel-
bourne in 1869, Haddon “ spotted ” him as a young- man
or parts—something more than merely foreign parts—and
it was on the editorial recommendation that Proctor (the
astronomer) and Archibald Forbes passed through
Smythe’s hands. Forbes gave Smythe a big advt. in
London by speaking of him as the Moltke of Managers.;
Died in England recently playwright Benjamin Charles
Stephenson (“Bolton Rowe”). Among other early
occupations, Stephenson was for some years secretary of
Lloyd’s, and wrote a history of the “A 1 ” institution. In
1872 he supplied Alfred Cellier with the libretto of the
successful musical comedietta, “Charity' Begins at
Home” ; and in 1886 the pair turned Out “Dorothy.” In
London “Dorothy” had 931 consecutive performances,
and that opera and the rollicking “ Charity Begins ” are
still sure draws for occasional revival. Stephenson helped
Clement Scott to adapt “Diplomacy” from Sardou (it
was played here by 7 Brough and lioucieault), and he
collaborated with Haddon Chambers on “ The Fatal
Card ” (produced by Bland Holt when Hilda Spong was
the heroine of his co.). Stephenson was responsible for
various other dramas known in Australia.
“0.K.”: A Poverty Point par attributes the death of
strong man Hajek, from cancer of the stomach, to
the fact that his performance included the placing of an
anvil on his naked abdomen, upon which anvil blows were
struck. The cancer may have been caused as stated, but
for that Hajek, either from carelessness or lack of know-
ledge, had only himself to blame. Before the Sandow era,
professional strong men gave little or no attention to the
development of abdominal muscles, and feats which de-
pended entirely on the strength of those parts, were per-
formed at great risk, owing to the enormous vibration
when a blow is struck, and to the comparative weakness
of the muscles in play. Clarence Weber has performed
the “ anvil ” feat, on and off, during the last three years,
and on several occasions before medical men, who all
agreed in the opinion that if the abdominals are fully
developed, and tensed at the moment the weight is placed
in position, and kept so during the feat, there is no risk.
The performer must have complete control of all muscles
employed, in any trick attempted. It is the half-finished
strong- man who suffers for his foolishness in attempting
feats which his fully-equipped rivals can perform w 7 ith im-
punity.
Great is the stage management of the Salv’army. Miss
Eva Booth has been lecturing to huge audiences in New
York about the horrors she witnessed in the slums of
London. By way of realism, she lectured in rags, while
her orchestra wept at intervals. If it hadn’t wept it
would probably have lost its job. Towards the end of
the address the lights went out suddenly, and the gloom
was like a black cat at midnight. Then a huge cross on
the stage burst into a glare of electric radiance, and Eva
was seen clinging to the implement in prayer—just like
the soap.advertiseinent of the lady who clutches the rock
in the middle of a raging- sea. There was a pause while
the thing soaked in. Then Eva heaved her arms heaven-
ward, and the collection was taken up.
“Chrysos”: In Poverty Point (8/3/’O6) you ask for a
contradiction of the rumor that Bland Holt had tried to
get Julius Knight and Miss Jeffries for his production of
“ The Prodigal Son,” but that J. C. Williamson put a stop
to the negotiations by demanding an enormous rental -for
sub-letting the artists aforesaid. Here is the anticipated
contradiction. Bland Holt never had any idea of offering
a special engagement to Knight and Miss Jeffries, or of
omitting any of the leading members of his stock co. from
the cast of “The Prodigal Son.” Moreover, if he had
wanted to engage J.K. and M. J. it was not in the power
of manager Williamson to make any terms for sub-letting
their services, seeing that they were under engagement
to Meynell and Gunn at the time, and will not he released
from their present contract until Easter week, or there-
abouts. Strange that Bland Holt should have been
credited with all sorts of plans for which Rumor
had no foundation. In Melbourne it is quite generally
believed that Bland’s co. was disbanded last week, that
he and Mrs. Holt are going to England at once with a new
co. in view, and that Walter Baker and Miss Ross have
hooked passages for foreign parts. All these notion*
originated, seemingly, from the fact that Holt happened
to break the long continuity of his Melbourne-Sydney
season by giving himself and co. a four weeks rest, during
which time Meynell and Gunn will play “ The Fatal
Wedding ”at Melbourne Royal. He resumes business as
usual (in Sydney) at Easter. Next year at Easter he will
exchange theatres with Anderson in the ordinary way,
and return to Melbourne for six months. His present
lease of the Royal expires in November 1907.
“ Melb.” : The late George Coppin’s first wife was Mrs.
Watkins Burroughs, widow of a well-known English
manager and actor. Her powers as a leading actress
w'ere considerable. In England Coppin was for a time in
the company of Davenport, the original of Vincent
Grummles, and Ooppin knew the Infant Phenomenon, a
highly-talented, amiable little lady, who afterwards did
well in America. The toss of a penny decided Coppin on
shifting to Australia, instead of America. W. S. Lyster,
as a midshipman, heard him sing “ Billy Barlow ” both in
Dublin and Sydney. In 1845 Ooppin brought his
company, musicians, and effects per schooner Swan,
from Launceston to Melbourne. Coppin’s Com-
pany included Mr. and Mrs. Ooppin, Mr. and Mrs.
Rogers, Mr. and Mrs. Thomson, Mr. and Mrs. O
Young, and nearly all the others were husband and wife.
Their opening play in Melbourne was “ The Lady of
Lyons,” followed next by “The School for Scandal.”
Cast of “ Lady of Lyons ” : Melriotte, Young ; Beauseant,
Thomson; Damas, Rogers; Glavis, Coppin; Pauline,
Mrs. Coppin. The Melbourne Public Library has a bill of
the farewell of Mr. and Mrs. Coppin, in 1846. The pro-
§r amme included “ The Ambassador’s Lady,” “ The Four
isters,” and “The Turnpike Gate.” Coppin first met
G. V. Brooke in England when they were both very young,
and Coppin acted Osric to Brooke’s Hamlet. In 1854 they
met again. Coppin engaged him for Australia. Coppin
returned in the steamer Argo, with his Olympic Theatre,
the “ Iron Pot,” made in Manchester. Brooke came with
R. Younge and Fanny Cathcart in the steamer Pacific.
They opened at the Melbourne Queen’s, in “ Othello,’
February, 1855. The Olympic opened with Wizard
Jacobs, ” and Brooke did not act there till
later. Coppin had been long a widower. His courtship
of Fanny Cathcart came before the public through her
marrying Robt. Heir, and an embroglio ensued. The
Heirs went to the Theatre Royal. Brooke represented
that Mrs. Heir treated him unfairly by breaking her pro-
fessional engagement, which was as his pupil in charge
of Mrs. Brooke. The public took sides, but there was soon
a happy reconciliation all round. Playgoers gloated over
“ The Honeymoon," with Brooke, Coppin, and the Heirs
all in the cast. Coppin married Mrs. Hillsden, a widow,
and afterwards married her daughter, the mother of the
large Coppin family ; one of the daughters married Geo.
R. Ashton (brother of Julian R. Ashton), who was
for some time The Bulletin’s Melbourne artist.
(I am only filling gaps in the obituaries which
have been published). Coppin and Brcoke, as
partners, got the Theatre Royal in 1856. It had opened
in April, 1855. The partnership lasted till 1858, when
there was a rupture. Brooke disastrously carried on the
Royal by himself. In 1860 they were friends again.
Brooke farewelled for England as Virginius. Ooppin’s
next important venture with stars was with the Keans,
in his new Haymarket Theatre. They opened (1863) in
“The Gamester.” Another star of his was Dillon, the
great Belphegor. Coppin went to San Francisco with
the Keans and Cathcart. It is said that, on
the voyage, they were reduced to living on potatoes.
His next great scheme was fetching out Brooke again,
but he did not take final steps in this till he had returned
to Australia in 1865. Barry Sullivan meanwhile kept on
at the Royal, since 1863. He advertised that he had sent
a liberal offer to his old friend and comrade, Brooke,
which could not fail to bring him to the Theatre Royal,
but this was all “ guiver.” Brooke was under engage-
ment to Coppin, who heard the awful news of the wreck
of the London when he was in Capell’s bookshop,
Collins-street. His most notable stars afterwards were
Anderson, Celeste and Mathews, successively.
“ N.” : Like a man with a tune in his head, I have been
trying to think what the little girls on the stage are like
off the stage. They are like biltong, the Boer meat, hard
as wood, and cut into strips. Especially “Sang-and-
Da-a-nce Gurls.”
“ G.”: The most ridiculous feature of the ridiculous
last act of “ Leah Kleschna ” is the English North Country
dialect put into the mouths of the peasants working in
the Austrian cabbage-garden, where Leah lives down her
acquisitive past. Surely the Yankee author was not
responsible for this banality ? Premier Bent was present,
the other night, and beamed over the cabbage-garden
scene. Fat Thomas saw a replica of his youthful self in
the bucolic lover who was scorned by Leah, and nodded
his head as if satisfied that the tiresome young man would
some day be Prime Minister in Vienna, making inter-
national complications off his own bat.
“ Alba ”: Charles has been a big favorite and failure
in Australia. Williamson and other managers have
imported him in the bulk. A few: Charles Ryley,
Leumane and Kenningham come to mind. Charles
Godfrey, Santley and Warner have kept the name to the
fore, while Holloway, Faning, Cartwright, Turner (of
Turner and Montagu) and Arnold have taken Charles
across a lot of land and water. Bill seems to have gone
in for play-writing, while Charles took the limelight—on
the stage.
“ Hamer ” : The reputation of “ East Lynne ”is mighty,
and there are quite a number of people who impute it to
William Shakspeare. When first I struck this idea I
thought it a chance delusion, hut, on telling a country
magnate, in return for a drink, how a woman had
informed me she “ had heard a great deal of 1 East Lynne,’
but didn’t care for Shakspeare,” he remained quite
serious, and said, pompously, that he always supported
the classic drama, and would be at the show that night.
Other people have treated the anecdote just us seriously ;
but one woman laughed at it, and remarked that she’
would know Longfellow’s work anywhere I
THE “GODS” OF GREECE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I wonder if the gods of Greece, in ages long ago,
Ate peanuts and then dropped the shells on baldheads
down below.
I wonder if they whistled loud, and stamped upon the
floor,
When Homer J. Buskinius refused them an encore.
I wonder if the gods of Greece, like our gods, had
a taste
For watching high-class “drammer ” with an arm around
the waist
Of a much-befeathered female, who just thought the hero
slow.
And did they howl the villain down in Athens long ago ?
I wonder if they sympathised with all the wronged one’s
cares,
And if they murmured “ Bosker ” when coming down the
stairs;
If each girl hung upon her bloke, and each bloke on his
“ piece,”
And if they supped on peas and chips, those giddy gods
of Greece.
N.S.W.
J. F. DWYER.

A cheerful slab of information from the
Hawker Military Inquiry, in Melbourne, was the
guileless admission that officers could obtain their
liquor from the canteen at a lower rate than
privates.

A languid lieutenant —lately from Sydney
—came before the Majah Haw-hawker Board at
Queenscliff (Vic.), the other day, to be asked by
barrister Maxwell: “Have you any recollection
of this incident?” The lieutenant remarked:
“Ai recollect it just so far as one recollects any-
thing which he regards for the moment as being
of absolutely no importance.” Then old Senator
Styles exploded, and declared himself “able to
j udge that stamp of man.”
AN IMPEDER OF TRAFFIC.
MORTHETH MOTHETH (to small boy): “ Can’d you
move on dere! D'yer vant de whole of der
foodpath to yourself ?”
A colored brother named Sheik Mahomed,
who sells chutney, appeared last week before
N S. W. Justice Simpson and applied for a divorce
from his white wife, on the ground that she was
an habitual drunkard and had neglected her
domestic care of Mr. Mahomed for over three
years. His Honor might have established a valu-
able precedent by deciding that the fact of a white
woman being married to a person named Sheik
Mahomed was enough to drive her to drink, but
there is nothing so brightly original as that about
Justice G. B. Simpson. He granted the decree,
and in due course the plaintiff will be free to marry
another white woman and support her on the pro-
ceeds of the chutney industry.
Hawthorn (Vic.)
owns a drain that, in a
way, rivals even the
famous Reilly - street
drain of Collingwood.
This main drain of
Hawthorn was once a
creek. It is about
three miles long, 15ft.
wide and 6ft. deep.
Strips of it, from 50ft.
to a quarter of a mile
long, are covered over
so as to form tunnels.
The drain flows under
streets and shops, and
through gardens, back
yards and sundries, and
in flood weather it runs
an adventurous course.
It once overflowed on
to a man’s property,
and cost the Hawthorn
Municipal Council
nearly £5OOO in law
costs. Also, three years
ago, it washed a coffin
out of an undertaker’s
shop and carried it
down-stream, bobbing
gaily on its muddy
tide. No one that mat-
tered was drowned in
it until last w eek, vdien
a sudden heavy shower
in the upper water-
shed of the sewer
brought down a wall of
water 3ft. high, and a
15-year old boy who
was mooning in the
drain was swept away.
Shouting and scream-
ing for help, he was
hurried along through
back yards, under
houses, and under
streets, until, after
keeping afloat for about
two miles, he gave up
and was drowned. A
dozen people raced
after him, lost him in
the little bit of tunnels,
nearly got him but
were delayed shinning
over walls and fences,
nearly got him again,
but fell against barrows
and obstructions, and
finally didn’t get him.
The body was washed
into the Yarra, where
it waited for the police to pick it up, and the
Hawthorn Council is almost thinking of covering
its amazing drain now, or of hanging life belts
near it or something.

The other day the Geelong branch of the
“Women’s NationabLeague” held its inaugural
meeting, when it was treated to
inspiring addresses by Janet Mrs.
Clarke, Grace Watson (the organising
secretary), and other fervid Reidites.
There was a large attendance at the
refreshment room, the establishment
of which was a distinctly bad innova-
tion for the cause of anti-Socialism.
This room adjoined a solicitor’s office
in the same building, and some of
the anti-Socialists, with their tea and
ice-cream, took possesssion of the soli-
citor’s waiting-room. A clerk, passing
through the room after filling an ink.
pot, left a large picturesque blot of ink
on the elegant train of a prominent
member of the league. Then George
Reid and anti-Socialism were com-
pletely forgotten and the ladies took
to talking of a subject of real in-
terest. The sins of the Labor Party
faded away before a considera-
tion of the “outrageous clumsi-
ness” of that unhappy clerk,
and the meeting dispersed talk-
ing dress and the relative merits
of salt and milk to remove ink
strains.
The Commonwealth regula-
tion about advertisement matter
in'imported magazines does not
seem to have affected Uncle
Sam’s publications to any serious
extent. M'Clure’s for February
runs 128 pages of advts. to 102
pages of reading ; Cosmopolitan
for February, 114 pages of advts.
to 118 pages reading ; and Harper
for January, 140 pages advts. to
162 pages reading. Blackwood’s
and the Fortnightly of London
enter with only about 20 pages of
advts. It is said Uncle Sam pub-
lishers have a way of getting over all difficulties.
* * * •
The Bulletin, the other day, re-printed
from the Sydney Herald the advertisement of
the ISlorthern Wairoa doctors, warning brother
medicos not to accept posts at the local hospital.
It now learns the facts of the “strike.” The
Wairoa Hospital—founded, we blush to say, by a
trades union—pays its doctors £3OO a year “ with
the right of private practice,” but anyone can be-
come a “ hospital member ” by the payment of
265. a year, and a member has the right to free
attendance for himself and his family in his own
home if he lives within a radius of eight miles of
the hospital. Operations, major and minor, are
included in the free attendance. Practically,
therefore, there is no hope of private practice, and
a doctor has to work like a galley slave for £3OO a
year, bare, serving not only a hospital, but a big
district as well. It’s about the worst case of
medical sweating that this paper has ever heard of.
* * * *
The landlord of Selborne Chambers, Mel-
bourne, where the. devil’s brigade nails up its
plate, is thinking of raising the rent of one or two
chambers which seem to possess a mascotte. The
room once occupied by recently-elevated Judge
Eagleson was previously occupied by two barristers
who both reached the Supreme Court bench, so
that room has three Judges to its credit. The
last new County Court Judge, Box, had a room in
the south-east corner of the second floor ; and, on
his elevation to the bench, barrister Cussen took
it. Now Cussen goes to the Bench, and there
is a rush for the empty chamber.
A KINDLY SOUL.
“’ Earin' as 'ow the young man was took ill, I called to see if I can
’elp. I ain’t any good at nursin', but I’m real good at layin’ out! ”
This quaint and rather unaccountable
statement is from S. M. Herald’s woman’s page
(14/3/’OG): —
If you state an age in an obituary or other public
announcement, and it does not agree with the age of the
person from whom you would inherit (particularly if it is
through a benefit or insurance fund), the money is legally
forfeit. A hospital nurse has just lost £567 through a
discrepancy of this sort.
It would be almost worth spending 6s. Bd. on a
legal opinion on this matter.

A British medical journal (the Lancet)
comes out with a declaration against the barmaid
because of the “moral and physical dangers”
of her calling. What work-conditions are
for barmaids in London The Bulletin
does not know, though it has sus-
picions, but in Australia
there are no more
“ moral and physical
dangers ” to be
met by the girl
behind the coun-
ter in a respect-
able hotel than
by the average
woman - worker.
She has to meet
more temptations
than the girl who
stays at home, cer-
tainly ; but not more
than the shop-girl or
the housemaid at the
Temperance Coff e e
Palace; and, as she is
far better paid than either
of those skirted toilers, she
is to that extent better able
to resist temptation. There
is a vast deal of cant talked and
written on this barmaid question. But practically
there is no more reason why a woman should
suffer harm from selling whisky-and-soda than
from selling ribbons.
* * * •
A police sale by auction of confiscated
liquor was held lately, at the Central Police
Station, Sydney. There were over 1000 bottles,
consisting of Chinese spirit, whisky, beer, and
champagne. The liquor was admittedly of an
inferior quality; in fact, the alleged “champagne”
was regarded as so inferior that it fetched less
than the current rate for plain “tanglefoot.” A
publican selling such stuff would probably hear
from the Law. Why shouldn’t the Law itself be
run-in when it is found trading off bad grog?
The inquiry into the Gembrook (Vic.)
railway break-up, which seems likely to stretch
almost to eternity, has now developed into a
dispute between the different departments, each
one trying to throw the blame on another. But
last week John Sloss, of the one-time well-known
Yarra-side engineering firm of Campbell, Sloss
and M‘Cann, threw at the board a theory which
had hitherto escaped the notice of the depart-
mental experts. He showed that in the broken
wheel of the first truck was a fracture,
caused by defective casting, extending from
the rim to the boss, and this, judging by
appearances, must have existed some considerable
time. His idea was that a jolt caused by the
slight—and admitted—depression in the road had
caused this wheel to break, and that the truck had
thereupon canted down and lifted its front wheels,
which, on descending again, did not find the rails!
Sloss’s suggestion seems feasible, but, putting that
aside, what sort of exainination are Tait’s trains
subjected to if excursionists are sent out in vehicles
with cracked wheels when a simple tap with a
hammer should find out the defect? Has the
hammer been sold in the interests of economy ?
• * * •
The engine that left the Gembrook (Vic.)
narrow-gauge line, caused the smash, and made a
job for Bent’s friend Croker, has had an interest-
ing history. It was built at Newport workshops,
and started running on July 13,1900. It shunted,
it ballasted, and it drew trains to such an extent
that by December 31, 1905, it had run 64,000
miles. Never once since it left the shops has the
engine gone back for repair. In March, 1903, the
wheels had become grooved and worn. They
were taken off, trucked to Newport, and turned
afresh. When anything on the engine broke it
was repaired at Fern Tree Gully. A fitter'was
sent up and he patched the job somehow. The
work was done out in the open, for there is no
engine-shed at Fern Tree Gully. Always, summer
and winter, rain or shine, the loco, that wrestles
with the most dangerous line in the State
stands outside braving the weather. That is the
sort of treatment Tait the economist gives his roll-
ing-stock and that is one reason why his
rolling-stock is so often breaking down some-
where or other. A superintendent, after
telling the past history of the fallen engine,
estimated that the department would spend about
£5 in repairs and then put it on the job again. He
considered it “ good for another 7000 miles.”

One of the saddest things about the alleged
journalism of these days is the “Children’s
Letter,” or “ Aunt Polly’s Postbag,” or “Talks
with Uncle Tom,” in which youngsters are en-
couraged to send on for publication profuse
accounts of how many chickens “Topknot” had
last time, and descriptions of how father drowned
four kittens, and fed Poley’s new calf. Poor
kiddies, everything is “so nice,” and they never
have anything but a “ pleasant day ’’—even when
father drowns the kittens. The other week a
bush laborer was charged at a Gippsland police
court with indecent assaults on girls of
eleven and nine, and was committed for trial.
The prosecution says he professed to be giving the
children an outing, took both of them across the
lakes, and camped out with them. And then
somebody dug up a “ Polly’s Postbag ” letter
which those kiddies had written about the
allegedly horrible proceedings, and in which they
said they had had “a pleasant time.”

The alien imported to Australia keeps his
native vices and loses his native virtues. A
Turk, before Melbourne City Court for vagrancy,
was said by the police to have been “ina drunken
condition for the past two months.” Now this
renegade son of the teetotal Prophet must be
supported in gaol by Victoria for six months.
THE KING’S MESSAGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Admiral Rawson wrote to say that at his first audience
with the King, His Majesty asked many questions about
the people of Australia and their welfare. His Majesty
commanded him to apply for a second audience before
leaving for Australia, and at the second audience King
Edward told him to say that he wished that he could
himself have visited Australia, but that, not having been
able to do so, he had sent first his two sons to visit this
great and important part of the Empire, and then the
Prince and Princess of Wales to represent him at the
opening of the Commonwealth Parliament. “He wished
me to say, also,” Admiral Rawson wrote, “ that to do
honor to the State, and to show his affectionate interest
in its welfare, he had specially given me this second
audience.” —Report of Sydney Mayoral Banquet.
He thinks of us !
How kind of Royal Ned to treat us thus !
Australia never saw his placid face,
But still, he’s quite aware there’s such a place.
What ho, let’s make a fuss !
We’re growing large ! We’re getting on apace 1
Ned’s noticed us !
He thinks of us 1
And so we haven’t wholly missed the ’bus.
At Lord Mayors’ dinners tears run down our cheeks,
The while in tender words His Gracious speaks—
Gor—save—His—Gra-ci-ous !
And loyalty in every tear-drop leaks 1
He thinks of us !
He thinks of us !
He sent his son on journeys perilous !
His Royal Not-too-Highness yawned a lot,
Shook hands, and vanished from this grateful spot;
We saw him well —oh, yus !
We know just what we think of him, and what
He thinks of us 1

Quinlan, the strange freak-murderer, kept
a cheerful and unclouded countenance through all
the investigation of his squalid crime. He was a
polite little boy to the last. “Thank you !” he
said to the constable who opened the dock trap-
door to let him go to the condemned cell. It is
hardly likely that the N.S.W. Government will
allow him to be hanged. He is obviously insane,
a rare mental accident who puzzles all the
alienists; they can only take refuge in the
dictum that he is at least not sane.
Quinlan seems to have no moral sense, no
idea of remorse, of shame, of sorrow. His
mind is clear enough, but it lacks something of
the human ; it is more akin with the mind of
the ox, which does not “lie awake at night
weeping overits sins.” An uncanny phenomenon —
Quinlan, whom the community must, for its own
sake, keep from future crime, and yet more a vic-
tim of Nature than a criminal. Among other
strange side issues he was religious in a way, or
else he was afflicted with religiosity ; and at the
same time he read. Deadw'ood Dicks. He had
about 13s.' 6d. a week, and out of that he partly
supported a brother and says he helped to pay off a
money-lender with whom his mother was involved,
and something went in tram-fares when he
went to see his brother in a monkery.
Also he intended to go to sea. Perhaps if he had,
the. sea-breezes would have blown a soul into him.
It is difficult to be a lift-boy and have much soul,
especially when one’s mother is in debt to
money-lender Booker.
MONEY COVERS MANY THINGS.
SHE: “ These capitalists have no principle.”
HE: “ When you have capital it is unnecessary
to have principle.”
Sydney’s Dill Macky paper has been
speaking very severely about Miss Ena Batten-
berg’s mercenary conversion to Rome. But, after
all, is the difference so very great ? She was con-
verted to Roman Catholicism for the sake of a
crown in Spain. It would have been all right if
she had been converted to Protestantism for the
sake of a crown in Paradise, and a share of the
golden streets, and the pearly gates, and the
jasper sea. The church is always talking about
gold, and its strongest spiritual influences are
derived from the garish wealth mentioned in
Revelation.

Professor Anderson Stuart, heading a
deputation of Sydney Zoological Society to ask
the City Council to grant an extended lease of the
Zoo premises:—
' This continent was not a great distance from the more
ancient and more fully peopled parts of the world, which
made it necessary’ that if the people of Australia now
growing up were - to have the education which brought
thenrto the peoples of the older lands, they should have
such facilities as the Zoological Gardens to help in their
education.
Perhaps the Professor didn’t mean his confused
statement that way, but Sydney Zoo is justly
proud of its fine collection of monkeys, and the
small kiddy ought to be taken to see Britain’s new
ally.

“6xB” writes : Rumors still persist that
N.S.W. Chief Justice Darley will retire at the
end of this year, and that his staunch friend and
colleague, Judge Owen, will also seek repose from
his arduous labors. Wise being out of the way,
it is safe for the Chief to retire. The prospect of
Wise succeeding him would—and once did —recall
him from the brink of the grave. Pilcher, if he
likes, may, it is said, have the Chief Justiceship.
Sly will have a puisne Judgeship. Gordon, K.C.,
who has now the largest practice in all appeal
courts, and who is as experienced a Common Law
as an Equity practitioner, is well in the running
for anything going in the shape of J udgeships;
but his health is not too robust, and he will
probably decline.

“Alec. Zander”: The Chinaman
mated to a white woman is a wonderful multi-
plier of the birth-rate. I know a case in
Surry Hills, Sydney, where the family
l resulting from this cross numbers
eighteen, ranging in ages from 40
years down to three. Nine of the
progeny are married, some to
Chinese, others to whites, and these
have families ranging from nine
to one, with the promise of a
prolific crop in the future. Several
of the grandchildren are now of
a marriageable age—they marry
young in the bottle-gathering
suburb—and'more birth-rate is ex-
pected. So far, this one old China-
man’s descendants number 59 pie-
bald Australians.

The duties of the police in
Victoria sometimes take a peculiar
turn. The local “John Hopkins”
had to act recently as auctioneer
in the disposal of the effects of a
deceased old-age pensioner. Among
other property was a coffin, which
was knocked down for Is. The
deceased —an old lady —was re-
solved that she should pay for her
own burial, hence the coffin. It
was kept under the bed, and as the
mattress was simply of bag, and
sagged a bit in the middle, she had
been lying on the implement for
months. Unfortunately, she was
removed to the hospital a week
Before her death, and as the box was not for-
warded with her luggage, the hospital authorities
arranged for her burial.
# * * *
The disease of squire worship—the bob-
curtsey and hat-touching complaint—is spreading
fast on the big estates in Victoria’s Western Dis-
trict. Even the State school children are taught
by sycophantic teachers to regard the local land-
grabber (the landlord of their parents) with the
sort of humility that might have existed in the
Middle Ages. So it is not surprising to find Mel-
bourne Argus publishing a long par from Camper-
down, describing how W. T. Manifold and his
wife visited the school, how they were fawned on
by teachers and scholars, and how they received a
“ silver-mounted brush and comb” that the young-
sters’ pennies had gone to buy. Then the Mani-
folds, who are going to Europe, promised that
they would not forget to send Christmas prize-
books, “as usual.” In years to come you will find
the district peopled by horizontal male beings,
who, as they go along the lanes on all-fours, touch
their hats and say “ Sir” to everybody they meet,
and by crushed females who make the bob-
curtsey and wear the abject bonnet. And you
will know them for the people who bought the
land-grabber a hair-brush in their childhood.

The diminutive weatherboard structure at

clump of gums, and it is supposed a casual swaggie

* ¥ * *
A horrible story of an American ship with
a lunatic on board was told lately in South Mel-
bourne Police Court. The narrative was to the
effect that a sailor went mad soon after the vessel
left the free and enlightened shores of “God’s
country,” and was put in charge of the second
mate. The second mate’s nursing is said to have
consisted partly in chaining the lunatic up every
night, and he was also alleged to be given to kicking
and standing on the patient. He was fined 10s. by
the usual awful Bench of Victorian honorary
magistrates for assaulting and biting a French
sailor who said he tried to prevent him standing
on the lunatic, and who was supported by other
members of the crew, who also said they saw the
lunatic being stood on. Of course, the deranged
mariner can’t be landed here, and it isn’t likely any
life assurance institution would care to issue
a policy payable if he doesn’t get back alive to
“God’s country.” Whatever may be the merits
of this particular case it is certain that American
sailing ships have a horrible reputation for
brutality, both in fiction and in police-court
reports —a reputation that would almost justify
the hanging on spec, of at least one American
mate, as an example, at every civilised port on
earth. The American judge who sells justice and
the American sea-officer who “lays out” the
crew are two good, reliable stand-bys of U.S.
fiction, and if these individuals are really desir-
able characters they have a case for large damages
against somebody.
* * tr *
Already it is believed that another case of
anthrax has been located in Ballarat, and the
patient is under observation in the local hospital.
There is some doubt about the case, but if the
patient is not suffering from the disease he is
accused of then he is a very lucky man. He was
at the same old game —skinning a cow that had
died of anthrax—and he contracted some form of
blood poisoning. It is this economical skinning
habit that keeps the disease moving, and the man
who takes the hide off such an animal and sells it
is a menace to society. The fact that he is a
menace to himself is little more than a side issue
under the circumstances, though if a man wants
to commit suicide there are pleasanter weapons to
cut his throat with than a deceased cow—especi-
ally that kind of deceased cow.
* • * •
There was something humorous in the
selection of Minister for Education Barnabas B.
O’Conor to open the Bangalow (North Coast,
N.S.W.) show. It is essentially a dairy-cattle ex-
hibition, Bangalow being the centre of the Big
Scrub dairying district, and consequently of the
infamous child-slavery of the North Coast. This
is the district where many children are too weary
to join in games at school, where they doze over
their lessons because their poor little bodies must
have the rest of which they are robbed by having
to rise at 4 a.m. to worship the Cow Joss ; where
schools have almost no attendance before 11 o’clock
nor after 3, all by reason of the Cow Curse;
where teachers do not expect home lessons done,
because they know the children fall asleep over
their tea, utterly worn out by Cow, and where the
birth of champion Bluebell’s calf is a more im-
portant event in the domestic circle than an addi-
tion to the human population. It is a continuous
battle between Cow and Education, and Minister
Barnabas B. O’Conor, who represents Education,
is found wallowing at the shrine of Cow.
THE LIPERTY OF THE SUBJECT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An official notice stated that if the men shaved the
upper lip it would be regarded as disobedience and
punished. —Report of Military Inquiry, Melbourne.
There are various moustaches. There are some that droop
and drip
Every time their careless wearers take an appetising nip.
There are others badly needing the improvement of a
clip,
Not a barbarous curtailment, but a neat tonsorial snip.
A moustache, of course, needs training; ’twouldn’t do to
let it rip
Till it bristled like a hedgehog with a lizard in its grip.
But a neat moustache looks manly as a mark of soldier-
ship,
Though the old-time Tommy Atkins had to shave his
upper lip.
Wellington preferred a whisker—just a little narrow strip
From the ear across the cheek-bone —and a shaven upper
lip ;
While the dear old Duke of Cambridge (if my memory
doesn’t slip)
Liked a face all over whiskers bar a patch below the lip.
With immensely large moustaches it is difficult to sip,
Let us say, pea-soup, for instance, and to land it every
trip !
Every time you take a spoonful it requires some skill to
tip
At the scientific angle with a bushy upper lip.
Regulations may be needed on a British battleship,
Or in fashionable regiments which they stylishly equip ;
But for volunteers I’m thinking (this should have them
on the hip),
If the skill and drill’s efficient, what’s the odds about the
lip?
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.
ALWAYS BILIOUS.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
“Just at the beginning of Summer my health
started to break down. Every morning I woke
with a splitting headache,” said Mrs. Gf. Cheese-
man, Mockton, 94 Morehead Rd., lied fern, Sydney.
“ Often I was too sick to lift my head from the
pillow. I would lie the whole day long, and never
have a bite to eat. My stomach was too weak to
keep down even a cup of tea. I was utterly worn
out with vomiting. I never wanted anyone to
come near me. When I tried to speak, my head
ached worse. The rattling of the carts going by
made my brain feel as if it would burst. Many a
time I went off in a dead faint. I spent pounds
trying to cure my Headaches, before a very old
friend got me to give Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People a trial. When I had finished half-a-
sovereign’s worth, my Headaches were gone, and
my appetite was three times as good as ever it
was before. To-day I couldn’t ask for better
health.
“For weeks my health had been going from
bad to worse,” said Mrs. Cheeseman. “All
through the day, I was heavy and drowsy. Try
as I would, I simply could not rouse myself. I
never felt like starting my work. There were
pains in my arms and shoulders, and my legs
felt too heavy to lift. I wanted to drag my feet
all the time. If anyone called on me, I left orders
to say I was too ill to see them. It was no use
for me to try to talk to people, for I couldn’t
think what to say. Some days I hadn’t a scrap of
memory. No matter what I picked up, I did not
know where I put it five minutes after. And
when I had one of my bad Headaches, I was fit
for nothing.
“ When I woke in the morning, all up the back
of my neck felt stiff and sore. There was a dull
throbbing pain behind my ears, and the top of my
head was ready to lift off. Wherever I looked, I
could see sparks of fire, and my head burned like
fury. As soon as I started to dress, my brain
began to swim, and everything in the room seemed
to go round and round. I knew the only place
for me then was bed. There I had to lie from one
end of the day to the other, too sick to move. I
had to have the room pitch dark, for the least
light hurt my eyes, and made my head ache worse.
My brain felt on fire. I couldn’t find a cool spot
on the pillow to lay my head. I would get hot
and cold in turns, and break out in a cold sweat.
My throat was dry and parched—yet if I took a
drink it set me vomiting. I was as weak as if I
had been laid up for months.
“For days after I had one of these bilious
Headaches, I was wretchedly down-hearted,”
added Mrs. Cheeseman. “ Gradually my whole
health went to pieces. At night I couldn’t sleep,
and soon I was nothing more than a bundle of
nerves. I could never look on the blight side of
life. There seemed to he nothing but trouble
ahead of me. I felt that no one wanted me, and
I was so short-tempered that I hated anyone to
speak to me. Nothing pleased me—l found fault
with everything that was done in the house. Yet
I couldn’t do the work one-half as well myself. If
I tried to sweep the verandah, the blood rushed
to my head and turned me dizzy. It was just the
■ same way if I stooped to take the meat out of the
oven. A mist came before my eyes, and every-
thing went black. Many a time I would have
fallen into the fire if someone hadn’t been near to
catch me. I hated to go out alone, for fear one of
my dizzy turns would come over me and I would
drop in a heap on the ground. My heart was so
weak that I never knew when I would faint.
“ For months I kept on taking all kinds of pills
and medicines. They made me weaker, and
never eased my Headaches for an hour. At last,
to please an old friend, I started Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People. She said if anything
could cure me they would—so I bought some from
my chemist in Elizabeth St. They suited me
from the very first. They did my head a world
of good, and gave me an appetite that fairly
surprised me. Gradually I grew stronger, and
began to gain in weight. I picked up heart, and
kept on with Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. My
nerves grew steady, and my head was no sooner
on the pillow than I was fast asleep. Every
morning I got up able to go through iny day’s
work. I never remember having such good health
as I have now. It will soon be two years since I
left off Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, but I have never
had a headache all that time. That shows that
I am cured for good.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills don’t act on the bowels. They
don’t tinker with mere symptoms. They contain just the
elements that actually make new blood. That is why they
are the surest cure for all blood diseases like anaemia,
biliousness, ind'gestion, rheumatism, lumbago, kidney
and liver troubles, and skin troubles like pimples and
eczema. And, for just the same reason, they are the
greatest help in the world for growing girls who need new
blood, and for women who are troubled with irregular
health, especially those of forty-five or fifty, when the
blood becomes deranged again. But you must get the
genuine Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People—price
2s. 9d. a box, six boxes 155.3 d., from all chemists and
storekeepers, or direct by mail from the Dr. Williams’
Medicine Co., Sydney. If you are in doubt about your
illness, write to the same address for free medical advice.
Sydney, so far as civic administration goes,
is undoubtedly the frowsiest, most unkempt city
of the Commonwealth. Its natural beauties are
great, but man does much to spoil and little to
enhance them. At Circular Quay is a triangle of
land which used to hold an old, gone-to-the-devil
sort of hotel —now condemned and destroyed. It
was proposed to make a little park on the spot,
but for more than a year now it has remained a
mud or dust patch and an eyesore, and it seems
likely to so remain for another generation.
