The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Representatives of the Victorian fellmongeries
and tanneries told the Tariff Commission a gloomy tale
lately. In the Geelong (Vic.) district alone there were, a
few years back, some 700 hands ; now there are lessthau
SO. Prom 1901 to 1905 inclusive'more than 32,000,000
sheepskins and hides, to the value of nearly £1,000,000,
were exported from the Commonwealth, mostly to Ger-
many, France and Belgium. If those skins and hides had
been worked in Australia they would have employed, on
an average for the five years, 1282 men, earning some
£120,000 a year, independent of other trades that would
have benefitted indirectly. Hides are frequently carried
as ballast, and so keen are the European Governments
to create remunerative work for their people that they
subsidise shipping companies for conveying raw material
from Australia, and also give bonuses to those bringing
the largest quantity in a given period. If these countries
wanted all these pelts for themselves, there could be little
excuse for Australia’s folly in thus losing so much needed
work ; but it not only exports hides and bark, but in the
last three years actually took back £1,500,000 worth in the
shape of leather. Australia grew the skins and the bark,
and those 1282 men badly wanted the work of converting
the skins into leather ; yet this fool country let the jobs
go to France and Germany to be done. Said the tannery
representatives further : “ The number of foreign buyers
of skins increases yearly. Directly a local buyer
goes into the sale room the foreign buyers
form a ring to crush him out. ... What little
he does buy he pays outside prices for, and
as soon as he is crushed and his works closed down the
price of skins drops again.” The tanners asked for an
export duty on raw 7 hides and skins of from Id. to ljd.
per lb., and they proved that such a duty, being neces-
sarily paid by the buyer, will not only raise considerable
revenue, but also give a large amount of work to local
tanners. In the last 10 years the price of hides has
steadily risen from ljd. and 2d. to 6d. and 7d. per lb.,
chiefly due to the enormous demand and the scarcity of
supply. If the foreign buyer can’t get our hides he will,
therefore, have to take our leather at Our price ; if he
determines that his own people shall have whatever fell-
mongering and tanning work is available, then he will
have to contribute to the Australian revenue in order
that they shall do so.
k * * *
That section of the A.M.P. Society which is
bent, on pursuing business to Europe and Africa won
again at the confirmatory meeting. But the majority
was reduced; and there doesn’t seem to be any doubt at
all that if the opponents of “ extension ” had had the
same facilities as its supporters—including the run of
the society’s cash-box —the result would have been
“No,” as it had always been previously. However, the
A.M.P. is a democratic society, and it has a perfect right
to govern itself badly if it wants to. It is tolerably cer-
tain that the present trouble will lead to the affairs of the
society being more closely scrutinised, and that will bean
advantage anyway. The bad workman habitually quarrels
with his, tools, and Australia is the A.M.P.’s tool; and
a whole lot of members will watch to see whether it is
Australia that has something wrong with it or the
management—in other words, whether the tool is at
fault or the men who have to use it, and who have been
grumbling that it is no longer good enough.

Taking everything into account, this office has had a
rather better year than might have been expected ;
though that isn’t very high praise. Early in the year it
issued notices to policy-holders from which most of them
appear to have gathered, not only that no additions at
all had been made to members’ bonuses during the last
five-year period, but that they had actually shrunk. The
whole explanation was that the society had lately adopted
a more stringent valuation basis, so that, more reserves
being required to earn the amount necessary to provide
for policies falling due according to expectations, there
was less money available for bonus additions. But it
isn’t easy to argue with a man who was told he had £45
of bonuses five years ago, and now is told he has only
£4O ; it isn’t much use even telling him that his widow
would have got the £45 if he had died meantime, and
that it is simply his obstinacy in living on which has done
her out of £5, So a bad year for the M.L.A. was ex-
pected ; and it isn’t as bad as it might have been.
There was talk of large reductions in the
M.L. A.’s expense rate ; but they are not conspicuous so
far
Expenses Compared with Total Income.
1896 .. £2l 3 11 per cent. 1901 .. £l9 19 3 per cent.
1897 .. 19 19 7 „ 1902 .. 19 910
1898 .. 19 13 2 „ 1903 .. 17 16 0 „
1899.. 18 17 10 „ 1904.. 19 19 7
1900.. 2011 2 „ 1905 .. 19 15 4 „
This isn’t what it ought to be. In 1895,. when the rate
was £l9 10s. 2d., the funds were only £1,087,768 ; they are
now £1,959,375, and £30,000 more interest, which costs
very little to collect, is coming in. Therefore the expense
rate ought to be less now than it was. Certainly, on the
other hand, a somewhat larger proportion of new business
is now being done; but then Australian conditions are
better. The cost is extremely high for a large office.
Charging 5 per cent, on renewal premiums, 3 per cent, on
annuities, and reckoning the balance of the expenses as
chargeable to new business, it cost £52,002 to get £26,059
of new premiums. That is to say, the new business cost
(on this basis of calculation) almost exactly two years’
premiums to obtain—the cost was 199 and a fraction per
cent. This is distinctly an unsatisfactory showing, both
absolutely and as compared with the record of other
Australian offices of standing; and the A.M.P. share-
holder who looks at his own 112 per cent, and at the
M.L.A.’s 199 per cent., and then reads that the M.L.A. is
pursuing business outside Australasia, may be inclined to
begin to feel a little sorry for himself for deciding to take
the same course.
Like a good many other offices, the M.L.A.,
though it tells the amount of new business done, does not
tell the number, &c., of policies lapsed and forfeited;
so the real value of the new business cannot be
judged. But it looks as though the M.L.A.
has had to argue a good deal with its members during
the year. In 1903 the surrenders paid out were £11,937 ;
in 1904, £10,014 ; in 1905, no less than £15,188. Again,
after the previous quinquennial investigation, £2943 was
paid in cash bonuses; last year, £10,501. Much of this
was unreasonable, no doubt—the circumstances have
been mentioned already. There, however, are the facts
of the case, reasonable or otherwise. But there is a
brighter spot:—■

The rate isn’t what it was, but it shows a steady improve-
ment lately, though there has been little change in the
forms of investment. In mortgages the tendency is to
add a little to the proportion held (51 p.c. of total assets,
against 49& p.c. in 1903); this is a trifle above the Aus-
tralian life assurance office’s average. In public securities
the office has about 20 p.c. of its assets, which is under
the average; but its freeholds (foreclosures included)
amount to about 19 p.c., which is at least 10 p.c. too
much, and is one of the weak spots in this office’s posi-
tion—a weak spot which the present safe and conserva-
tive management probably regrets more than anybody
else.

. These figures show a remarkably steady and consistent
. increase of business, accompanied, on the whole, by a
substantial decrease of expenses. As to the latter point,
these are the figures for 10 years :
Expenses Compared with Total Income.
1896 .. £25 10 0 per cent. I 1901 .. £25 16 2 per cent.
1897 .. 22 18 9 „ |1902.. 23 3 2
1898 .. 22 10 11 „ 11903.. 22 3 9 „
1899 .. 21 18 4 „ 1904 .. 21 13 7 „
1900 .. 29 311 „ |1905.. 22 4 7 „
With the increase of funds, producing comparatively
cheaply-earned interest, the expense-rate on this basis
should, of course, automatically fall; just in the same
way that, on this basis, a large stagnant office should
show a small rate compared with a progressive office
searching after new business—which is the thing that
costs the money. One real test of efficiency is, what is
new business costing? Following an arbitrary rule (be-
cause there is no other to follow), and charging renewal
premiums with afixedsper cent., and debiting the balance
of the expenses to new premiums, it is found that £10,538
of new premiums cost £13,353 to get in, or 127 p.c. This
is by no means unreasonably high for a small office ; on
the same basis, the A.M.P.’s figures show 112 p.c., and
the National Mutual’s 121 p.c. ; while among smaller
offices the figures stretch out to 250 p.c. In 1895 this
society issued policies giving an annual premium revenue
of £6908, and the renewal premiums were £23,642 ; and,
though the form of the accounts has since been altered,
making an exact comparison impossible, apparently the
cost of the new business at that time, reckoned in the
abovementioned way, was 131 p.c. of first year’s pre-
miums. Though the difference between that and the
present rate is not large, still there has thus been some
cheapening of a rate which, all things considered, was
already low in 1895.
But whether the City Mutual’s business sticks is
another matter ; and this seems the weak point. The re-
port is, unfortunately, silent on the matter of lapses and
surrenders. As stated just now, the form of the accounts
has been somewhat altered, and some of what were for-
merly reckoned as renewal premiums are now’ included as
new ; but going back to 1904, the renewal premiums were
£46,835, while in 1902 they were £45,260 ; so that the two
years’ increase was only £1575. The new’ business written
in 1902 and 1903 should have brought in during the two
following years £16,477 ; and the loss in premiums through
maturity of policies could hardly have amounted to half
that amount. It looks, therefore, as though the lapse rate
must be high. Last year’s account contains the item—
“ Surrender values exhausted, policies now lapsed,
£5792,” which equals 21 per cent, of total claims ; in the
A.M.P. the corresponding item represents only 10 per
cent., while in the National Mutual “ surrenders to repay
loans and premiums advanced ” stood last year for only 6
per cent, of total claims. But this not-very-satisfactory
experience of the City Mutual is mixed up with another
—the disposition of the funds. The City Mutual
lets out its funds largely in small loans, to some
extent upon the security of policies, and this almost
inevitably means a high surrender and lapse rate—the
advantages attract that sort of policy-holder. On the
other hand, it undoubtedly means high interest earnings;
the rate on mean funds earned last year was £5 3s. 7d.
per cent., as against an average Australian rate of about
£4 9s. per cent. The difference betw’een this office’s rate
and the average means an extra £2lOO on to the revenue,
available either for wu-iting down assets or for the advan-
tage of policy-holders in some other way. But the
lapses ought to be shown, since they, as well as the
extent of the new business, have a most important
bearing on.the expenses; for obviously 121 per cent, spent
on business that sticks is less of a burden than 121 percent,
spent on business much of which fades away after a year
or two. It makes all the difference, in fact, between a
remarkably satisfactory rate and a moderate one.

The directors “regret that the financial result is not
more comfnensurate with the heavy risks all shipping
undertakings must necessarily incur.” Of course the
directors are looking at the matter on the basis of a
£lso,ooocapital. Still the £75,000 of capitalised reserves
were as much the shareholders’ money as capital itself,
and ought to be earning just the same profits. Certainly
it is quite possible that some of this £75,000 should not
have been capitalised, but should have been written off in
spite of the fact that £47,067 was written off at the same
time. There is a popular idea that inter-State shipping
co.’s are stuffing their profits away where the public can’t
see them. The Melbourne Steamship Co. probably
wishes it was one of those co.’s. The simplest plan is, of
course, to write down shipping property’, But this co.
keeps its shipping property in its balance sheet at cost
(to the new co., that is, formed 18 months ago), and only
£3OOO a half-year has been put aside for depreciation,
which means less than 5 p.c. p.a. ; and either some co.’s
greatly over-estimate what is a fair allowance for depreci-
ation of ships, lighters, &c., or this co. greatlv under-
estimates it. This depreciation fund, now £9OOO, is in-
cluded in reserves above. The true reserves are, therefore,
£9oooless, and probably they are much more than that less!
In the past 18 months gross profits have been £32,908. if 71
p.c. p.a. were charged for depreciation, which seems
to be a fairer average, the net profits would be reduced to
£18,733, and the present true reserves would be £7483
assuming that the assets were sufficiently written down
when the new co. took possession, which isn’t at all
certain. Apart from floating assets, the co. has very
little ; after meeting current debts, all forms of liquid
assets (including stores and debts due) amount to
£44,333; and when the accounts were made up only
£3431 had been paid on account of the new steamer just
about to be handed over in England. Altogether the
position isn’t by any means what might be expected of a
co. which but lately puffed out its chest and doubled its
capital out of reserves; while the profits have fallen so
seriously that it- would seem to be a very fair thing for
the co. to publish a profit and loss account by way of
explaining how it has happened. Is it loss of revenue,
increased expenditure, or what ?

This looks better ; it is almost the first streak of hope the
shareholders have had since the prospectus flashed out in
1897, baited with the names of the most successful Broken
Hill and Lyell men, and dangling - with the promises of
another rich Silverton Tramway monopoly. But the
streak is such a faint one that it is probably not visible to
holders of ordinary shares, some of whom, it is almost
cruel to recall, “ got in ” at a heavy premium a few daj's
after the share lists closed, and have hardly seen their
shares within coo-ee of par since. This, briefly, is the
order of things :
(1) The co. rents a few miles of line from the Emu Bay
and Mt. Bischoff Railway Co. ; and the first £7500 over
working expenses must go to it.
(2) The co. owes £200,000 on debentures. The second
£7500 must go to the debenture-holders as interest.
(3) The rent of the line is £10,370 a year, and the inte-
rest is £lO,OOO. The balance between £7500 and those
amounts has to be made up next; if profits won’t run it,
then debentures must be issued for the deficit.
(4) There are £lOO,OOO 8 per cent, preference shares,
and 4 per cent, of this 8 per cent, is cumulative. The
accumulations amount to £23,206. That is to say, already
future profits are mortgaged and ear-marked to that
extent.
(5) And then the ordinary shares come in ; they stand
for £260,000.
The profit last year was £3828 ; even if it jumped up to
£BOOO, and nothing were written off that profit to wipe
out preliminary expenses, &c.,it would take five years’
profits to overtake the accumulating preference dividend.
Even then, as long as the profit only stood at £BOOO, the
preference shareholders would take it all. But the co.
has a paper asset of £37,125, representing “ registration,
expenses on issue of debentures, &c.” ; and, as the direc-
tors see the only honest thing is to wipe that out as soon
as possible, there is another sponge waiting to suck up
profits.

Some of the figures in the last line are approximate. A
distribution of profits was made as at July last, when
3s. 7d. per share was paid, or at the rate of about 80 per
cent., shares being then, it is understood, paid to about
9s. per share. This absorbed £4885 ; and a trade bonus
of 7s. 6d. per hogshead absorbed £4923. Now, on an
increased capital, and with the old shares split into two,
Is. lid. per share is paid (£5295 =76 percent, per annum),
and 6s. bonus per hogshead. What this bonus will
amount to there is no way of saying definitely, but
it has been estimated in the foregoing at £4077,
though The Bulletin doesn’t know any better reason why
it should be £4077 than £6900. Anyhow, the success o
this co-operative beer factory has been amazing—the re
suit (1) of dispensing with a vast amoun t of the unnecessary
expenses to which those brewers are put who have to go-
out and buy business, and (2) of not having to carry a num-
ber of unnecessary pubs on its back. Assuming that, apart
from the bonus, the customers got their money’s worth in
beer, the first half-year’s profit, after providing for de-
preciation, was at the rate of over 125 per cent, per
annum. During the past year the co.’s capital—which
was formerly £15,000 in 30,000 10s. shares, of which 22,525
were issued and 2s. 6d. paid upon them in cash—was
altered to £15,000 in 60,000 ss. shares, of which 55,259
were issued and ss. paid upon them. Besides this money,
£6094 has been received in premiums on new shares. Even
assuming that the whole of this capital and premium has-
been available all the year, the net profit upon it was at
the glittering rate of 120 per cent. Yet, in spite of this,
and even before the payment of the half-yearly dividend
and bonus, the co. had managed to acquire an overdraft
of £7250 ; while sundry debtors have increased during the
year from £6783 to £11,396. Certainly one can’t judge;
the size of this last item until one knows the size of the
turnover ; but it is an item through which a lot of leakage-
is liable to occur, and it seems to be growing very
rapidly, considering that this is a co-operative business.
Further, the showy figures have led to a demand that the
price of beer be brought down ; and that has been done—
to £2 17s. 6d. per hogshead. It was a start of dazzling
magnificence that the co. made ; and, even allowing that
it drew pointed attention to the advantages of co-opera-
tion, and also to some of the glaring absurdities and
extravagances of the ordinary brewing and wholesale
beer-selling business, it is a question whether it wouldn’t
have done the co. itself more good in the end if it had
had to struggle a bit.

Considering the price of copper (though the best of the
market has been only towards the end of the year), the
year’s results are not what might have been expected.
But, then, no less than £66,256 has been paid out of
profits on account of the fire which occurred at the mine
two years ago. This fire provided a handy pretext
for a badly-needed reconstruction of mine methods.
Therefore, the whole £66,256 can’t be put down as lost;
nevertheless the profits would certainly have shown up
to something ahead of £lOO,OOO but for the fire. And if
only £64,000 had been available for divs., the distribution
would have been equal to 20 p.c. As it is, Is. was paid
in January, 1906; another 2s. is to be paid at the end of this
month ; total £24,000, or 7£ p.c. for the first quarter of
the year. So the old mines pay 3s. per share in three
months ; and only 18 months ago the £2 stock was down
to 18s. The special expenditure through the fire is not yet
over ; but the costly part of it is. The amount charged
to profit and loss in 1904 was £78,368 ; last year, £66,256 ;
total £144,624. But, as said just now, it doesn’t follow
that it was all loss. Still, this amount does not include
the whole cost of the new machinery which has been
installed; thus on the power plant £19,418 has
been spent and charged to capital. The financial position
has been steadily improving ; at the end of 1901 there was
a surplus of liquid assets over current liabilities of
£58,679 ; next year the surplus stood at £65,309 ; at the
end of 1904 it was £79,247 ; and on December 31st last it
was £118,087.
Standard copper averaged £69 12s. during the
year ; in 1904 it was £59 os. 7d. The production at Moonta
and Wallaroo was 6501 tons fine and 15 tons blister cop-
per ; but an unknown quantity of this was produced from
purchased ore. But, though standard copper averaged
over £lO per ton better than in 1904, the profit (on smelt-
ing ore) was only £lllBs. 9d. per ton of metal as against
£4 ss. lOd. per ton in 1904. Part of the explanation lies
in higher costs. Following figures are per ton of copper :

The copper recovered by cementation (or salt-water leach-
ing) has to be smelted at a cost of £7 2s. Id. per ton.
Part of the striking increase in costs is due to the sliding
wage-scale ; wages rose 10 per cent, in January, 1905, and
another 5 per cent, in February, and kept at that all the
year.

Site of the principal works of the new Austra-
lian Smelting Corporation is now being settled ; there
was a gathering of the clans in Sydney latter end of last
week, and a search party went off down to the South
Coast, leaving Lionel Robinson in Sydney, where he put
in some of his time advising London to pick up the
Broken Hill Prop, shares that a panicky market had begun
to throw out at 595. This Australian Smelting Corpora-
tion followed on the heels of the Zinc Corporation, and,
like it, has a capital of £350,000, and, like it too, has
8., M., and Co. for managers. The Smelting Co., besides
taking over the works, &e., of the Smelting and Refining
Co. at Dapto, has the New Caledonian copper properties
of the Caledonian Copper Co. ; it has made a contract
with the Zinc Corporation to handle the latter’s output
of silver-lead concentrates ; and, besides, it has some sort
of agreement with the Broken Hill South as to that
mine’s silver-lead concentrates. Its Australian directors
are W. L. Baillieu, H. J. Daly, Hertzig (of 8., M. and Co.),
and F. C. Howard ; last named is managing director of
the B.H. South, and went on the board in view of the
just-mentioned agreement as to his mine’s output.
Baillieu and Daly are directors of the B.H. North ;
Hertzig and Howard are directors of the South Blocks ;
and the latter’s output, anyway, will be available —when
the co. gets to the output stage.
This smelting co., prima facie, deserves a warm
Australian welcome ; for it is after work which hitherto
has been done mainly in Germany. For a few years, in
the days of oxidised ores, B.H. South ran its own smelters
on the mine ; but since it got on to the sulphides, over 12
years ago, practically the whole of the output has been
shipped to Europe—sold on the mine mostly to German
buyers. Same thing has taken place with regard to Block
10, the British, Junction, Junction North, and North ; and
ever since T. J. Greenway left Block 14 that co. has also
sold its output to the foreign smelter. Thus the only
Broken Hill mines that have smelted in Australia for years
past have been the B.H. Prop, and the Sulphide Corpora-
tion ; and both these —the latter especially—have
occasionally sold concentrates for shipment. At the
lowest the smelting or “ returning” charges on sulphide
concentrates have been £4 10s. per ton ; and they have
been as much as £5 7s. 6d. On top of that have been
numerous deductions and penalties. But it has probably
cost the Australian mining co.’s much more than this;
for they found it impracticable to send their output to
custom works, and so they have almost always sold it
straight-out, either on a sliding scale or at a fixed
price for metals. The German Government, to stimulate
the highly profitable smelting- business, hassubsidised ship-
ping co.’s carrying this class of raw product ; so the Aus-
tralian workman has been paid, say, 11s. per ton for
mining the ore (and even that includes the cost of the
imported timber and the imported stores ; the Broken
Hill Prop., anyway, burns Rangoon candles), and another
4s. for dressing the ore ; another 2s. or 3s. probably re-
presents the Australian labor spent in freight arid hand-
ling- ; and the foreigner has got all the rest. The foreign
labor spent on smelting, refining, manufacture, and
freight amounts to at least 275. 6d. or 30s. per ton of ore,
against the Australian’s 16s. or 17s. The new Smelting
Co. isn’t a philanthropist, and its object isn’t to get most
of that extra 275. 6d. or 30s. for the Australian workman ;
but if it can do what it aims at, that will be the effect
of its operations, nevertheless.
Returns published by the B.H. South for last
half-year are affected by zinc dealings ; so those for pre-
vious half are best to figure on. In that term the
concentrates contained 11,896 tons- lead, and 382,4210 z.
silver, with 1245 tons zinc. Lead averaged £12135. 6d., and
silver a trifle under 2s. 5Jd. Total value, £198,574. But
the co. only got £137,490 ; and that was reckoned a most
favorable contract. In the same half-year the Broken
Hill Prop, spent on mining £150,524, and on concentrat-
ing £37,676 ; total, £188,200. On smelting and refining,
including slime-sintering-, and coal, coke, and fluxes, it
spent £220.497. That, roughly, is the proportion in
which Australian and foreign workmen now benefit from
all those Broken Hill mines which sell their concentrates
to foreign smelters —£188,200 (plus a little for extras) to
the Australian ; £220,497 to the foreigner,
* # * *
Sulphuric a«id and superphosphates are pro-
bable by-products from the Smelting Corporation’s works.
Production of sulphuric acid is necessary for zinc treat-
ment. The Carmichael-Bradford process was intro-
duced five or six years ago for desulphurising the
sulphides. At that time the B.H. Prop, had the
Huntingdon-Heberlein process under test, and the
big co. eventually adopted it. It or something
of the sort was indispensable during the last metal slump.
When the imported process had been adopted for desul-
phurising, the C.-B. people experimented with the pro-
cess to produce sulphuric acid, and succeeded. Cost is
£3 per ton at most ; under favorable circumstances it is
alleged, acid could be produced at the dirt-cheap price ot
£l. The B.H. Prop., meantime, acquired the sole right
to use the process for acid production ; but later on it
sold back part of this right, and allowed the co. to make
a contract with the Bewick-Moreing-Robinson people,
■who control the new zinc and smelting concerns. The
B.H. Prop, is apparently not wholly satisfied with the
Carmichael-Bradford process for acid manufacture ; and
it has lately erected works on another principle—and
these, by the way, are no great success either. But the
new zinc and smelting concerns are well enough satisfied
with the process to have got hold of a large interest in

There was a fine, healthy bear in the market
last week, and it squeezed down Broken Hill Props, to
595. using - the fire as a lever. The fall for the week
meant £800,000; and other mines, where there wasn t
anv fire, gave in proportion. It is possible the mine was
over-valued before ; but to knock off £SOO,OOO was absurd.
Like the other two fires, this one has been built in, and
must burn itself out. Probably that means a big fall of
ground, sooner or later; but it doesn’t seem likely to do
the mine any serious harm.
* * * *
Concerning mine fires. W. H. Patton, the first
of the American managers imported to Broken Hill,
owed his appointment largely to a reputation he made
at Comstock as the man who fought and beat the greatest
of the fires in that underground Oregon forest. Block 11,
where the present fire is, had the biggest bunch of
oxidised ore in the B.H. Prop., and probably in the
world ; it was taken out with square sets, stayed (after it
was found that square sets alone could not resist the
diagonal pressure) with solid bulks of the same soft,
resfnous timber. Just south of this the big creeps
occurred, and the ore that was left above 300 ft was
taken out by opemout—the work on the mine with
Which John Howell’s term as general manager is
associated. The work relieved the pressure and pre-
vented further creeps; but down to 400 ft., and
occasionally deeper, the ground is much disturbed,
and there is a vast quantity of splintered timber for fire
to feed on. Very little ore remains in the shallow levels
in that neighborhood. It is some comfort for the share-
holder that where there is most timber there is least ore ;
and the biggest practical danger is from creeps affecting
shafts. It has long been the policy of B.H. Prop, and
other Barrier mines, however, to make themselves inde-
pendent of shafts sunk in the lode or in country liable to
be disturbed. Ever since the previous big fire the co. has
had its own fire brigade and equipment, under the con-
trol of Hansen, formerly superintendent of the Broken
Hill town brigade. But a fire underground isn’t work for
an ordinary fire fighter. At the first outbreak, 12 years
ago, Deputy Superintendent O’Brien, of the Melbourne
Brigade, was whirled up in a special train, but a very
brief turn underground satisfied him that all he had
learned was no good to him in that case. Later
on, as the difficulty always is to locate the fire (it is im-
possible to get near it for smoke and gas), various smoke-
jacket contrivances were tried; and it was understood
that, though it would be impossible to work in them,
they would be kept on hand in the hope that they would
be some use for exploring purposes. But nothing has
been heard of them this time ; general manager Delprat
relies on the blowers to clear the drives more or less and
thus beat a way up to the supposed seat of the lire ;
but there are obvious disadvantages about making a
current of air underground when you don’t know where
there are embers waiting co be fanned. *
What appalling rubbish some of the dailies call
mining news ! This, for instance, alleged to have been
wired from Broken Hiil ’last week :—“ The fire at the
Proprietary mine continues very bad. Both the mine
and hill are now idle, and will probably remain so for
some time. It is impossible to ascertain the full extent
of the fire, Which has, no doubt, spread greatly in the
direction of the Junction. Arrangements are being made
to attack the fire from Junction North.” The Prop, fire
has spread greatly in the direction of the Junction, and
arrangements have been made to attack it from Junction
North ! Between Junction North and Block 11, where
the fire is, lie blocks 11 and 12, Block 14, the British (two
blocks), and the Junction.

Rolker has finished his work at Cobar, and if
that extra-cautious engineer’s report on the mine is
satisfactory, Australia’s biggest. copper-gold mine will
pass to J. Bull; for the necessary capital has been under-
written in London and Paris—mostly London ; Paris took
up the balance promptly when it got the chance. Kolker
inspected the mine some years ago (for Wehrner, Beit and
Co., the S’Africans, it was supposed), and then had an
idea that it was over-valued ; but copper at £BO and
copper at £4O odd are different animals.

Specimen Hill, Bendigo, was the hero of last
week in Melbourne, (hough chasing at its heels was the
New Argus. Co. is an example of the maxim that it is
hard to kill a good mine. New Hopeful, between New
Argus and Specimen Hill, failed to get the Argus shoot;
and Specimen Hill, too, was unable to show up tdl
Januarv, when'it got gold in the west leg at 1835 ft.
First crushing of 47 tons gave 37^0z., and since then a
total of 738 tons from the new formation has yielded
1020oZ. This has put the co. in funds, and allows of a
shilling div. to start with. So far the 1835 ft. level has
been driven 200 ft., and the yield stated above came from
there. South face of the level is all stone, but north, as
work gets lower on the leg, thestone is pinching sharply.
Co. has a big area of ground, and is opening up what is
really a new mine. Hence the rush after shares.
The shutting down of the pumping plant at the
Southern and Prentice shaft of the Prentice and Southern,
Rutherglen (Vic.), is bad news for the Creat Northern
Extended Consols. Prentice and Southern is thought to
have been draining the Murray River from its Southern
and Prentice shaft; but this is hard to believe, in view of
the reduction of water at the Great Southern, the
Extended Consols, and at its own North Prentice shaft.
How long will the co. be able to keep water under at the
last-named shaft after the pumps stop? Even if it can
keep the water down sufficiently to allow of shaft-sinking
and reef-driving, much more money will be needed to get
the water out of the gutter, when once it has again to
be entered, than if pumping had been kept on. Beggars
cannot choose, though ; and with money going out at
every stroke of the pumps, the directors have no alterna-
tive except to stop. *
Allan H. P. Stoneham, of the Australian Gold-
fields, London, who tried to get into the British
Parliament on a semi-Australian ticket, ■ has apparently
got some funds for the Junction Deep Leads mine. This
claim was one of the old Bull floats at Moolort (Vie.).
Junction Deep owned a quarter interest in the povyer
station which supplies electricity to the Charlotte Plains
and the Victorian Deep Leads, and it also is
the proud possessor of a shaft and a drive
which was reported in the London annuals to be going
ahead in 1604, but which certainly was stopped long
before that. Plant has already been erected, so that all
that has to be done is to go on with the drive, get under
the lead, and take the water out of the gutter. But there
isn’t much cash for this work, as all the capital
subscribed and guaranteed amounts to £14,000. This is a
mere drop in the ocean compared with what is wanted to
float a deep lead co. into a haven of security. Berry
United to reach the same stage has put up about £40,0011,
and there isn’t a co. on the far end of the Moolort lead
that has made headway with an outgoing of less than
£50,000. Apparently what has been done is to get some
cash in the hope of raising more.
* * * •
New Loch Fyne, Matlock (Vic.), is hung up,
except for four men who are scraping - up what quartz was
left in the old stopes. It is about two years since the co.
took out a payable crushing ; there is plenty stone m Nos.
3 and 4 levels, but none is payable. No. 5 shows very little
stone, and that little is irregular. From below that bot-
tom level they lately took out a trial crushing from an
underhand stope ; but 100 tons only gave 19Joz. It was
folly, anyhow, to drive the No. 5 level 2000 ft. double
width in order to obtain 80ft. of backs. A shaft from the
surface would have been of much more use, as it would
have proved the dyke to a greater depth. Co. is now
trying to get money enough to rise some 270 ft. to connect
the lower level with the surface, where the dyke outcrops,
then work the dyke by open cut, sending* all leaders and
quartz seams to the battery. It is a large undertaking,
but only the battery can tell whether it will pay.
* * * *
Callide Railway and Coal Syndicate (Q.) dies
quietly by forfeiting £2500 deposit put up as a guarantee
under the provisions of the Act granted by the Queens-
land legislature. The syndicate was originally formed to
exploit the undoubted coal deposits at Callide, about 70
miles south-west of Gladstone. Its scheme was no
wild-cat in the accepted sense. Coal is there, and of good,
clean quality. No less than. 50,000,000 tons of it
are certified to by State Geologist Rands, and the Ad-
miralty’s test in ‘H.M.S. Torch was favorable, although
the 50 tons used indicated that the steaming capaoity was
30 per cent, below that of Newcastle coal. The pi opo-
sition necessitated 70 miles of railway—ss mu es
easy grades, and 15 full of engineering difficulties.
This and the reduced capacity of the coal tested
killed the business. In 1902 the syndicate was
given an amending Act, providing an extension of time
until 31/12/’O5 to construct the line, and permitting grades
of lin 18. John Bull did not “part up,” so the line was
not constructed, and the State resumed the leases, and
£2500 goes into the Treasury. Despite all this the day
may come when Callide will be a profitable field. Just at
present Queensland has no reasonable market for the coal
already opened up ; but G. H. Reid and his followers do
not seem to care much about that, so long as hats and
calico come regularly from foreign parts.

Since 1890, when the present co. got to work, 78,379
tons have been crushed for 8170 tons tin, which
has been sold for £428,240, or £52 Bs. 3d. per ton
of black tin; while the average realisation for the
past six months has been £74 os. sd. per ton.
Thus on the half-year’s output, the difference between
the average price realised by the co. and the price
realised in the past six months has been, worth £10,500
clear profit to the Vulcan. After paying dividend duty
the profit was £25,198 ; so that the recent high market
has been responsible for pretty well half of the profit.
In other words, given the average tin-price of the past
14 years, the co.’s profit would have been £14,698 instead
of £25,198. Even in the previous half-year (to August,
1905) the average realised was only £59 11s. Sd. per ton ;
in the half-year before that (to February, 1905), it was
£39 17s. per ton. The outlook for tin seems, no doubt,
remarkably good ; hut, all the same, it is a tiicky niaiket,
and it is just as well to keep posted up on the bedpost
the fact that for very nearly half of its profit the Vulcan
is depending on the present excess of the market value of
its product over the average value of the past 14 years.
It is still worked in a primitive, or rather per-
haps patriarchal, fashion, this Vulcan, even though it is
the most permanent-looking tin line in Australia and
the deepest lode-tin mine in the Southern Hemisphere.
In some respects no doubt the shareholders are the
gainers For instance, “Directors’ and audit fees,
£BO Bs. ” I and the half year before that it was £4O 4s. Think
of it, London Stock Exchange ! On the other hand, con-
sider this item—“ Tin stone cartage, £562.” The co.
hasn’t even got its own mill; the Irvinebanlc Co. crushes
for it and the Vulcan hires horse-teams to laboriously
dra o- ’ the stone to the battery. Last half-year,
besides cartage, &c., crushing charges amounted to
£4830, or about 17s. 6d. per ton. Possibly it couldn’t he
done for less than that, but. after carting coal and fuel 300
odd miles by rail, and paying from 3s. 6d. to ss. per 1000
gallons for water, the Broken Hill mines can do mill work
for 3s. 9d. per ton ; and 17s. 6d. is a longdistance from 3s.
9d At one time it probably didn’t matter much. John
Moffatt owned the big slice of the Vulcan and the biggest
slice of the dressing works, which he manages (it is prac-
tically his) ; but now that shares have run up to £5 ss.
they have been getting into many hands in the south,
and the call for theco. to own a battery, and to modernise
the whole system of work, will soon be heard.
* * * *
Unless something wholly unexpected happens
to the tin market, Irvinebanlc is in for a brisk time.
Vulcan has over four years’ work in sight of 10 per cent,
grade on a crushing basis of 1500 tons a month ; and,
despite archaic methods of transport to mill and high
crushing costs, with tin where it is, it will pay 9d. a month
on the split shares-four for one. The Governor Norman,
a Moffat-owned mine, now being equipped with its own
mill has an immense tonnage of high-grade ore
■Droved • and the Vulcan Consols, which lias up to now
been pig-rooted for 8 and 10 per cent, stuff, has a big
tonnage of 5 per cent. ore. And there are others.
Reid’s Creek tin shows (N.Q.) wait the tardy
erection of John Moffatt’s mill. Dalziel’s Tin Co. has a
lot of 8 per cent, ore in sight ; and the Adelaide, another
Moffat-owned property, and the Denford and the Titama
(owned by E. B. Torpy) are opening up big payable lodes.
Torpv’s properties, especially, are developing well enough
for Torpy to propose erecting a reduction plant. In the
Titania there is proved a big lode of 6 per cent, ore ; and in
the Denford there is a shoot of 30 per cent, ore 50ft. from
surface driven on for 15ft., and still in the face, besides a
great deposit of 3 per cent, stuff proved over a length of
130 ft. * * *
Croydon (N.Q.) has been struck with another
patch of bad luck in the shape of a cyclone ; and for the
time being, the local miner has knocked off looking for
the gold beyond the granite and is prospecting for his
house and the lost, fled, vanished frying-pan. Mean whi e,
in case he cannot again pick up the domestic lode, the
philanthropic public of Queensland is building up a solid
little fund to help him. # , "
Latest move in Cloneurry (N.Q.) properties is
the reported purchase, by a Knox-Schlapp syndicate, of
Kennedy’s Duchess mine for £15,000 cash. When one
considers “the mine” and the cash said to have been
paid for it, and the people said to have given it, one is at
first inclined to merely say “Rats. But perhaps they
“ know something.” Melbourne is taking very kindly to
Oloncurry just now; similarly Knox-Schlapp are not
averse to Cloneurry. Duchess is, anyhow, a good mine
to gamble with. But as to Cloneurry in general it has to
be always remembered that the railway has to be built,
and either a genuine sulphide zone has to he opened up,
or the capacity of the carbonates has to he proved.

Although Brilliant Central, Charters Towers,
keeps a bold front, the workings are not too gay.
Recently at a vertical depth of 1420 ft. in the straight
shaft, a start was made to lookforthcreefwirlcedby

Many Westralian gold shows have been found
by natives; only the other day a gm strolled into a

* * *
The Fenian, about the best show so far worked
at Meekatharra, which is Westralia’s richest spot at
present, belongs to six men, two of whom own half of the
mine between them. Stone has yielded, so * ar » &ioz per ton,
and although the reef is a little less than 2ft., it is we
defined, and at water level (150 ft.) looks like goiiig down
rich. Spite its name, there is only one Irishman in the
party, and three of the others are Scots. Lease*contains
17 acres, and £30,000 has already been refused for the
show. The country north and south is being worked for
a total length of a couple of miles, and several of the
shows are getting rich stone. There is a Government
battery, but water is scarce and stone is accumulating.
Winter rains will soon set in, but no attempt is being
made to conserve any of the supply.
* * * *
Perhaps the oldest mining company m
Australia was wound up a few days since at Perth by
Justice M'Millan. The West Australian Mining Company
was formed in 1840, and was one of the first in the State.
It was worked until 1857, and sunk shafts near Armadale
in search of some mineral or other —name of mineral
almost forgotten. At. one time nearly everyone in the
State, was a .shareholder. The capital was £J),000 in
10 000 shares of 40s. each. The co. has not done any
dieting for 49 vears, all the directors are dead, there is
no office, and the assets consist of a few patches of land,
which are to be sold.
* * * *
As the result of the Westralian Goldfields water
scheme, the Kalgoorlie co.’s save on the average 3s. or 4s.
per ton in costs ; and yet they successfully resist a pay-
ment for the service which will allow the State to wipe
off anv of the debt. The co.’s themselves write off an
annual 10 per cent, or more from their plants ; but they
have had the spineless Governments of Westralia so com-
pletely under their thumb that the State can t
write off even £ per cent, out of profits. Before
the scheme was completed, fresh water (condensed)
cost about. 655. per 1000 gallons; now it costs ss.
On the average, water costs the mines Is. per ton of nre
treated ; it runs from 7.86 d. per ton on the South KaL
o-urli to 18.84 d. per ton on the Associated. Although
both these mines use practically the same process, and
treat almost identical tonnage monthly, the former uses
only £240 worth of water against the latter s £590, a
difference of £4200 a year. But the South K.algurli gets
its power from the Kalgoorlie Electric Power Go., and
that accounts for most of the difference.
* * ■» *
Associated Northern is doing well for a mine
that was nearly worked out years ago present reserves,
by the way, will last two years on the conservative esti-
mate of G. M. Roberts. Co. is earning a profit of £ll,OOO
monthly, has in hand £132,000, and has written down
equipment and development to £36,000. The western
lode which has been tested in shallow ground is to be
exploited from below by a crosscut right through to the
Kalgurli boundary. This western lode is low grade in the
oxidised zone, hut may be better at a depth. As costs
are only 28s. a ton, what would not have been looked at a
short time ago is good enough now.
* * * *
Lancefield, Laverton (W. A.), which closed down
at the end of November, will not move again for some
time yet. After testing 25 tons at the South Kalgurli
mill, and obtaining an extraction of 83 percent., a similar
plant with slight modifications, has been ordered ; but it
.will take time to get. The ore at 300 ft. contains arsenical
pyrites, and is valued at 48s. per ton. With this g?rade of
ore, and treating 10,000 tons monthly, a profit of nearly
£lO,OOO is looked for. Well, it won’t do anybody any
harm to look for it; but finding’s another matter.
« * * *
A prospectus issued in Adelaide ought to have
the attention of the Stock Exchange there and of the S.
Australian Mines Department. The thing is called the
Golden Gate G.M.00. ; property situate near Angaston.
Capital is £21,600, in 10,800 shares of £2 each. Of these,
1200 fullv paid go to C. H. Angas and H. Johnston (ap-
parently they own the freehold) ; 7600 fully paid go to the
Angaston G.M. Syndicate ; and a whole 2000 are offered
to the public, the £2 being payable on application. Public
has the privilege of putting up all the capital and getting
less than one-fifth of the alleged mine; further Angas
and Johnston take £250 of the money that the public puts
up There is no plant on the property ; there are only
three shallow shafts, from which a few tons of the richest
stone have been gouged ; and the public’s £3OOO or so is to
provide machinery. There is a big irregular clayey forma-
tion with leaders through it. The prospectus says, on the
authority of “ the present mine manager,” that the lode
matter is 175 ft. in width, with neither wall showing, and
gold bearing and highly payable for the whole width.
Prospectus also says —“ The directors ” (of the syndicate,
apparently) “ have sent to the Mt. Torrens Government
battery a parcel representing an average bulk sample
from the mine for treatment ”; and the result is 640 z.
from under 41 tons. And the manager says the mine
consists of two bodies, one of which is over 175 ft. wide.
These statements are put forward as a basis on which the
public is asked to put up its money. If they are true,
what does the syndicate want to raise a paltry £3OOO or
£4OOO for, and what is late millionaire Angas’ son and heir
doing to hand over an interest like this ? Two practic-
ally unknown men report on the property ; apparently
neither of them sampled it; and two shafts that were
120 ft. apart when one “expert” reported had come
together to 75ft. within three days, when the other
expert reported. But this is a side issue. Here the pub-
lic is told that “ an average bulk sample” from the mine
of 41 tons showed 640 z. ; and as the mine consists of
“lode matter 175 ft. in width and highly payable all
through,” it is up to the S. Aus. Mines Inspector to
report whetherjiis State has about the biggest gold mine
in Australia, or whether Adelaide has just had put before
it something extra-outrageous in the way of prospectuses.
It ought to be the duty of the Adelaide Stock
Exchange to urge the Government, in the interests of
mining "and legitimate Exchange business, to find out
which°of the two it is.

day that 'you could buy pure gold from the Mint for
£3 17s. lOd. per ounce. But, alas for this writer, s hopes of

informed itself as usual. It was in an article on gold-
minino' on the coast ; the Mint, they said, was giving
pi -ig 0 per ounce for this product, therefore it must
contain “other valuable minerals, probably iridium
or platinum.” But, in the first place, the -Mint doesn t
buV iridium or platinum at all; in the second, the tact
of the Mint giving this good price denoted that it was
buying gold, and not an impure, alloy ; and,. lastly,
brethren £3 17s. lOd. per ounce is the standard price for
22-carat gold, which isl-12th dross.

have to contend with sometimes develop into a scare which
tends to knock shares out of the market. The A. S.
Cameron Steam or Air Pumps have saved many
precarious situations by their ability to handle water,
even gritty or aciduous without fear of the pumps getting-
out of order. Broken Hill Mines, the Mt. Lyell, and other
leading coys, have recognised the great value of Cameron
pumps in their simplicity, strength and durability under

steam. W. and J. Lempriere, of Melbourne—the Sole

Important Notice to Mine Managers and
Users of Concentrators.— In the recent action for In-
fringement Wiifley v Card the decision of the Supreme
Court of Victoria did not merely refer to a Card top
which was altered without authority, but it didrefertoa
Card Concentrator manufactured and worked at the New
Chum Consolidated Mine, Bendigo under the supervision
and control of N. Guthridge Limited, and to a Caid Con-
centrator exhibited at Long’s works, .Poot#^ which
was officially pointed out as the trim Card construction
during the trial, and which defendant used for exhibi
to customers and making tesus, and on which they gave
exhibitions to witnesses for the purposes of this tual. Ine
Judo-e specifically decided that the substance of the
Footscray table and of the No. 38 (New Chum Consolidated
Table) was the same as that of the plaintiff Wiifley and
4ere infringements. N. Guthridge Limited were ordered
to pay damages and costs, the costs were taxed and the
Sheriff seized the goods of N. Guthridge Limited in Mel-
bourne and Sydney, but as they were all claimed by the
receiver for the debenture holders the execution wa s re-
turned unsatisfied. Bankruptcy proceedings have been
instituted in the Supreme Court of N.S. Wales to wind up
N. Guthridge Limited. As N. Guthridge Limited are still
offering to indemnify purchasers, take notice that all
users o*f Card tables which.infringe the Wiifley patent will
'•be proceeded against: The user is liable to an action as
well as the manufacturer.* # „

„ ® an : It always hurts me when the
shadow of doubt is cast on an “ Abo.” par. Some
weeks ago,'a writer stated that he had charmed a
snake by whistling to it across a waterhole, and
the snake waited there at the bank until the said
writer had found a big stone and bashed it on the
head. Next week, another “Abo.” man wrote
slightingly of the snake’s intelligence, and, I took
it, cast insulting doubts on the story altogether.
The question was : Has a snake the sense of hear-
ing ? Well, last Saturday, I was called in to
prosecute a large black snake that had been found
coiled up on my neighbor’s verandah. The snake
did not wait for the ceremony, but, before my
arrival, was seen to crawl under the veran-
dah floor through a hole. For half- .
an-hour I waited with a nice
straight bit of tree. ' The
waiting was tedious, and
soon I found myself softly
whistling. In a minute, a
the small devilish-
looking head ap-
peared in the hole,
and—stayed there.
Calmly the near-
side eye met
mine and held
my gaze. For
half-an-hour
longer I kept
up the spirit-
ed airs, only
pausing for
wind, but
not the
eighth of
an inch
would the
reptile
budge.
Bull ants
were run-
ning up
to his
nose con-
tinually
and clear-
ing out
again in
a hurry,
but he
took no
notice. Then N
I took a
saucer of milk,
which was handy,
and put it beside
the hole, thinking that
thirst might induce him to come out. No good ;
he was offended, and not even the “Bull and’
Bush would fetch him from his seclusion. Only
he cursed me with his eye all the time. There is
nothing else on earth which has such a cold
bitter curse in its eye as a snake.

“ Uckle ” writes :
A.S. (B. 15/2/’O6) asks has any Aboriginally person
reared a kookaburra. My mother reared three (not
including us). We kids had to feed them. Having had
practical expenence in that line, am of the opinion that
A.b. has not. Our kookas fairly cackled with delight
at the sight of raw beef. They didn’t bolt their food
before we suspected their intentions, like the birds of
A-f: They used to belt the beef on the ground about
99£ times before eating it. “A.S.’s” kooka bolted a
lizard alive, leaving its hind legs kicking. That jackass
was either a thundering idiot, or “A.S.” is a thundering
(missing word). Whilst we had the jackasses, we never
saw a worm in the garden. The kookas met a sad end.
Our nearest neighbor killed the three one day. He had
the d.t.’s, and got a loan of the birds to killhis snakes.
He got angry because they didn’t kill his snakes. My
mother told him afterwards that as the snakes were
imaginary he should have borrowed imaginary kooka-
burras to kill them, and he admitted that she was right.

FIRST AID FOR THE DEAD.
MOTOR FIEND (cheerily): “ It’s all right, old
hoy. Don’t he at all alarmed. I always carry
a medical kit with me.”
W.P.B. ’: The suction power of greasy
wool is extraordinary. It is only necessary to
place a bale of the “great natural resource’’ over a
bucket of water and this will be illustrated. In a
few hours the water will have entirely disap-
peared from the bucket. This has nothing to do
with any vacuum, and the experiment may be made
equally effective by putting a bale near a puddle.
Also wool, after being shorn, grows. Why it
grows, and whence it draws its nourishment, I am
unaware. Hair and wool draw nourishment from
the scalp and skin of animals. Can anyone
account for the fact that the hair on dead persons
still persists in growing ?
* * * *
Though so far there isn’t much to show for
it, in a commercial way of speaking, the S. Aus-
tralian Forest Department has, up to date, given
away something like 0,200,000 trees: This free
distribution began on a small scale 23 years ago,
and nowadays about a quarter-million a
year go out—gums, Remarkable pine (for
box-making), N.S.W. swamp oak, and
American ash principally. It is the
same department that has for years been
nursing the date-palms along the over-
flow waters of the far northern bores.
The biggest plantation, containing close
upon 3000 palms, is at Lake Harry ; but
the ultimate idea is to scatter palms in
small clumps at intervals where they
will do the rare travellers in those part?
most good—to serve much the same pur-
pose as the melons and pumpkins
scattered by early explorers on far-in
sandbanks, and still, after these 40 or
50 years, bearing the squashy vegetable
in season. So far, however, the date-
palm hasn’t taken as well to its new
home as the transported pumpkin
did ; especially the cool changes that
have a habit of dropping-in during
February and March affect the ripening
of the fruit. It didn’t seem possible that a cool
change could drop in too often during February
and March ; but it seems that, till it gets acclima-
tised, anyway, the stranger from mid-Africa has
its own views on that subject.

“Bricky”: Joe was watching a spider
climbing up the post. That started a conversa-
tion about different sorts and sizes of insects •
finally it came round to fleas. Said the man with
a cap on the back of his head “ Yer can’t beat the
West fer fleas. Biguns? Why yer can stand ten
paces off and fling ’em at the wall and hear ’em
drop on the floor. Sleep? No yer can’t get no
sleep, they’ll sit on yer chest and pick their teeth
and fairly howl at yer. But strange ter say after
two or three nights they leave yer f’r a spell
Discovered the cause. When a whistle sounded
and announced the arrival of a fresh boat, they’d
be off ter meet it.” Then a gust of wind lifted the
roof off the smithy and we had to flee.
‘ 1 O’Fipp ” : I was present at the breaking
of all long-distance and sprint records for bullock-
driving profanity in a Gippsland (Vie.) township
the other day. Two men, driving a waggon with
18 bullocks, came to the township for a load of
goods. After getting their load and spending
some hours on a lovely jag, they decided that it
was time to leave. When they reached the
railway gates they found them shut, as a train
was due. But, full of Gippsland tanglefoot,
Bill and Jim declared that they wouldn’t wait no
’arf-hour for no train, so they opened the gates
and started the bullocks through. Just as the
leaders got fairly on to the line the whistle of the
train was heard. Bill rapidly fled down off the
load. Strenuous swearing got the bullocks half-
way across the line, when the nave of a hind
wheel struck the gate-post, and stuck fast.
Then began a simultaneous cussing mission
such as would have moved a team of
hippopotami, let alone bullocks. An accompani-
ment was rendered by two old dames, standing at
the door of the store, who had their hands over
their eyes, and squealed continuously—“ Don’t
look ! Don’t look ! Oh, the poor men will be
killed ! ” The train was pulled up just in time,
and, the bullocks hauling solidly, the wheel
skidded off. Bill and Jim let the bullocks go
and each rushed for a gate. They met half-way,’
and used another beautiful batch of profanity in
an argument as to whose gate was to shut first
while the indignant engine-driver threatened to
run the train over them if they didn’t get off the
line quick and lively. When I left, the bullocks
were tangled in the railway fence lower down,
and Bill and .Jim were going back to the township
to try to bring their shattered nerves together
with another of the same.
v « * *
The sort of farmer who, as an instructor
of others, ought to be worth £lOOO a year to any
State, strolled into the Vic. Agricultural Depart-
ment lately with a sample of maize, 7ft. Gin. high,
luscious and green, grown near Dandenong. This
is poor country, and the season has been dry ; yet
the crop had thus matured without a drop of
water artificially applied. It was planted in drills
3ft. apart, with 9in. between each plant, and lewt.
of superphosphate per acre was run in the drills
with the seed. A cultivator was run through the
rows every three weeks. If that man’s experience
holds good elsewhere, it seems as if this country
could do with a lot less irrigation than it is getting
—with less rains and more brains, in fact.

“Jim. Pan.”: T'other day I watched
fourteen camels come to the Darling to drink.
It was my hour for Nature study. I timed the
performance. ■ It lasted just fifteen minutes.
Abdul Jam Turn marshalled the camels side by
side on the sandy bank. The ungainly beasts
drank very deliberately, lifting up their heads
high m the air (fowl-fashion) every now and then,
as though to give the water a better chance to
settle down into their storage tanks. When
finished with absorption, they marched in stately
fashion up the bank and over the red sandhill,
with A. J Tinn hanging on to the tail of the
last. An hour later the local ferryman gravely
told me that “the river had fell somethink
terrible since mornin’.”

: Will the naturalists who
afnrm the impossibility of the cat-rabbit cross
kindly assign to its place in zoology the animal
with a cat s body and rabbit’s legs, that eats both
meat and grass, and sits in an upright position ?
It has been found in most rabbit-infested dis-
tricts, and writer will land one in Sydney for any
enterprising person who will deposit £5. Here is
a chance for some budding naturalist.
THE BOMBAZINE ARTILLERY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
For full dress occasions it was decided to allow each
corps to retain, if it so desired, its old pre-Federation
traditional uniform, or to adopt the gorgeous dress
■ordeied by General Hutton. The various corps could, in
short, please themselves on full dress occasions. . . .
splendid artillery uniforms . . . costly gold and silver
chevrons—-sometimes costing £l6 or more each.— The
JfxGE, 14/2/ O.
The papers talk a lot of rot;
They say our army’s gone to pot!
Which simply proves there’s quite a lot of things they
don’t get on to—
For instance, ever since the war
They’ve been a-cuttin’-up the corps
For things they might have known before—but then they
didn’t .want to.
They used to say “the country’s proud
Of you ” ; and used to say it loud ;
Eut now we’re just a useless crowd that “ lacks organisa-
tion ”;
And everythin’ is in a mess—
We “ don’t know' even how to dress ! ”
Oh, blow your “ free, unfettered Press it’s ruinin’ the
nation.
It’s—“ One for the Infantry!” “ One for the Horse! ”
We look for it now as a matter of course ;
We all get put in the pillory.
They rate us, and slate us, and disintegrate us
Without any shame or remorse—
All, exceptin’ the Pride of the Force—
The Bombazine Artillery !
Yes, that’s where they have to stop ;
Where.we get ’em on the hop ;
Where the Force comes out on top when they start
a-skitin';
That's Australia’s starry hope,
1 hat s what makes the papers mope,
That’s the corps ’ll make ’em slope when it comes to
fightin’!
You should see each golden gun,
Glitterin’ like the flamin’ sun,
When they swing to “ Action-fron’! ’’-though they
seldom makes ’em ;
You should see the limbers, too,
White and silver, picked with blue,
An’ the harness bright an’ new—that’s the thing- that
takes ’em !
There s nothin about ’em that’s vulgar or coarse,
They re alwa3 7 s looked up to as men of resource—
Fit to defend a Distillery !
They chide us, deride us, an’ some can’t abide us—
A feelin’ the papers endorse—
But nobody flouts the Pride of the Force—
The Bombazine Artillery!
You should see ’em on parade—
That magnificent Brigade !
Officers in gold brocade, trimmed with silk an’ laces ;
Aiguillettes, and all the rest,
Gleamin’ on each dazzlin’ breast,
And an ostrich-feather crest settin’ off their faces !
Pale-blue trousers, striped with gold,
Perfect cut, an’ crease, an’ fold—
Beautiful, as well as Bold ; lovely and alarmin’.
And the han’some rank an’ file,
All arrayed in similar style,
With the Band in rose-pink voile, lookin’simply charmin’
It’s hard for the Infantry, Hell for the Horse,
To rough it in uniforms hairy an’ coarse,
But they’re unworthy of frillery ;
They jeer us, an’ fleer us, an’ never will cheer us,
Stuck dowm in the scrub an’ the gorse ;
But, hear ’em yell for the Pride of the Force—
The Bombazine Artillery!
Then their service-dress is neat:
Satin, with a double pleat—
What the Staff calls “ re-al sweet ’’—frills of bright blue
“lustre”;
Picture hats, an’ brown, tan bools ;
Gloves just what their fancy suits ;
When the “ Stables ” trumpet toots you should see the
muster 1
Nice, enamelled mops and pails,
Gold paint on the manger-rails,
Ribands in the horses tails, manes done up artistic ;
While for years the rule has been
That no gunner shall be seen
Without pants of crepe-de-chine— cut idealistic.
So, rail at the ,Infan try, pooh-pooh the Horse,
We look for that now 7, as a matter of course—-
WE are quite used to the pillory !
Yes, flout us, an’ scout us, an’ say things about us
Without any shame or remorse.
But don’t “ sling-off ” at the Pride of the Force—
The Bombazine Artillery!
vie.
O. C. CABOT.

“Hamer”: Be drastic methods with re-
fractory horses (B. 8/2/’O6). A man who was
proud of his horsemanship told me that his
procedure with buck-jumpers was to spring
into the saddle with a very thin glass bottle full
of water in his hand, which, at the first symptoms
of trouble, he brought down weightily between
the horse’s ears. Then he looked out for a soft
place to fall. The water trickling over the horse s
head after the blow gave him the impression that
he was killed or something, and if he didn t drop
he would stand trembling, and would never after-
wards run the risk of having the operation re-
peated.

“ Homo ” sends this as a true bill: —
It was race-day at Fillemup. “ Slogger ” Samson had
come down from the shearing with a cheque as big as a
blanket, and was “ shaking it up.” Things were, m fact,
prett\ r willin* all round, and the wooden box that seivecl
as a lockup was bulging'. The race-night dance was
coming on in the hall next the Travellers Rest, and the
social pulse of Fillemup was quickened by the dread that
Slogger would violently disturb the evening’s harmony,
as he had done many times before. So a council of war
was held, and Slogger was chained to the log that held
the overflow from the lockup during festive periods.
Then Fillemup gave itself to the delights of the dance,
and nothing occurred to mar the proceedings till close on
midnight. Then, above the revelry, arose the noise of
an escaped menagerie. When Fillemup was sufficiently
recovered to inquire the cause, it found that the row was
merely Slogger, with his prison on his back, demanding a
drink.
And another true bill from “ P.X.” to match it
When “Ginger” Clarke and Francis Abigail were
Ministers they were sent out to make things a bit more
solid for their boss (Parkes) in the Far West. Broken
Hill was then a mining camp ; lookup, a couple of corru-
gated-iron shanties, with the iron nailed on not many
times. Saunders, then sergeant there, now superinten-
dent in the same district, had run in two diggers (more
or less diggers) in the morning. Broken Hill knocked
off digging to have a drink—even Abigail is excuse
enough for a drink in a Broken Hill dust-storm. Later
on, when the drink began to talk and make strange
noises, the prisoners couldn’t resist it any longer—they
put their shoulders to the side of the prison, and walked
through it. Then they joined the crowd that gave what
the Sydney papers said was an “ enthusiastic welcome ”
to Parkes’ Ministers. Within a few months, by the way,
the same Broken Hill turned out to follow its one hearse
carrying Abigail’s effigy to a stake, where the effigy was
burned with the same sort of liquid enthusiasm.

THE NOMADIC BARD AND HIS FREE BEER.
WOMBAT WALKER: “ Yus, you give it ter ’im, but yer won't give it to me!”
MR. PUBLICHOUSE: “ Well, you see, I believe in encouragin’ Australian literachoor.”
A peculiar trouble has struck a number of
towns in N.W. Victoria. Some time ago there
was a plague of mice there, to cope with which
cats were in very great request. The farmers
begged, borrowed, or stole all the cats and kittens
they could get in the towns or elsewhere, and the
mice were at length got under. Then the trouble
was to keep the cats down, and now every farmer
that visits a town brings with him a bag contmn-
ing several or more pussies of all sorts and sizes.
These he lets go, and the towns-people can
scarcely stir out of doors without falling over six
cats. The Mallee people will probably have to
import tigers to get rid of the new pest.
“ Brolga ” : During the Northern Terri-
tory wet season—November to March —the
lagoons and swamps swarm with black geese,
and the niggers collect the eggs in thousands,
stack them in a huge, common heap, build a
skeleton pyre over the stack, and set fire to it.
Most of the outside eggs are broken, and others
charred to a cinder, but those nearer the middle
of the stack are baked hard, and the remainder
are fairly well cooked. Then commences the
feast; and the number of eggs each nigger can
stow away is marvellous. Very few whites have
witnessed these feasts, as no bushman w'ith any
sort of a nerve ever gets into the sw 7 amp-country
in the egg-season. The “ smell ”of a wild black-
fellow is bad at any time ; but, after a couple of
feasts of goose-eggs—phew !
* * * *
“Flabby Paul”: Be smells. The worst
smell I ever struck is dead carpet snake. When
lie’s alive and has had a gorge a fortnight before
he’s not exactly lavender. But when he has been
dead a fortnight, the air around him has a faint
green tinge, and it’s possible to cut pieces out of
it. Dead native cats don’t come within miles of
it. There is also a tree, appropriately named
“stink wood,” that grow'S in the scrub. It can
hold its Own against amateurs when freshly cut.
Wild banana—that has got water in it—is a pro-
fessional, and can even knock the “stink wood
endways. But the worst vegetable smell comes
from a fruit (like a custard apple) that grows on
our seashores up North. When ripe this fruit
falls from the tree and bursts on striking the
ground. Then every living thing within a hun-
dred yards shifts quickly. The bushmen here
have a name for that tree, only it wouldn’t look
well in print. But a fortnight-dead carpet snake
holds the belt!
* * * *
“D.S.M.”: Be the aboriginal appetite.
About a year ago I missed three yards of Ruberoid
from the store, and never could make out what
had become of it, until, one day, I stopped at a
blacks’ camp for a drink. Noticed one of the
lubras chewing something with evident enjoyment.
I asked her what she was eating, and she replied,
“ Dunno, but him blurry nice.” It was Ruberoid!
You can bet I was astonished. I inquired if she
had eaten much of it, and she informed me that
she and her buck had eaten the whole three yards.
It had evidently agreed with them, as she wanted
more. I was too flabbergasted to try to find out
how they had managed to get hold of it, and I
climbed on my horse and rode away, thinking out
the problem : If it fakes two blacks to eat three
yards of Ruberoid in a month, how long would it
take them to get through 2\ sheets of galvanised
iron ?
“Curlew”: The Victoria and Grampian
Ranges (Vic.) still offer partially inaccessible
haunts for the native fauna, and are well stocked
with emus, wallaby, and a few “ great reds ” in
spite of the exertions of the rabbit scalper and the
station hand to exterminate them. In the same
district, on top of the Victorias, at the North
Western, is a plateau consisting mainly of swamp
—an ideal refuge for wild fowl, and so sequestered
that few, if any, of the powder-shot-and-trap
maniacs have even seen it. But on the plains or
in the lower scrub a native bird or animal is very
rarely seen, and, at the present rate, extinction
of certain species is only a question of a few years.

“ Hassan ” : lie those “ fishing notes ” of
the Sydney newspapers. The cream of the joke
does not appear in your correspondent’s par
(l/3/’O6). One finds in these columns that “ Mr.
Jones informs us that the fish are biting greedily
at worms or prawns,” or toasted cheese, or what-
ever fish do bite at. And “Mr. ‘ Bill ’ Smith, the
popular and well-known sport, reports from Cowan
that a party from Sydney, fishing half-an-hour be-
fore slack water, bagged 18 dozen schnapper, all
sizable fish.” The true inwardness of these things
appears when the guileless tourist is told, upon
visiting the places named, that he can get boats
from Mr. Jones at ss. a day, and that Mr. ‘Bill’
Smith runs the boarding-house.

Me an’ me mate wuz blasting a rock on the Cromwell

Rover (the dorg) wuz standin’ by me side ; ’e must ’ave

an’ bits o’ dorg.

“ Quandong ” writes :
“Jimmie Pannikin’s” par (B. 22/2/’O6) re the once
magnificent cedar forests of the Tweed and Richmond
rivers being almost a memory of the past, is not at all
exaggerated. Apropos, many years ago the younger son
of an upper-class English family settled on the Tweed
and engaged in the cedar industry. Once he had several
logs on the creek bank out of flood reach. He refused to
sell these to the timber-buyers at the current rate, ex-
pecting a rise in the price of cedar. Before the rise even-
tuated, he received a letter—the contents of which were
never disclosed —which called him to England in such
haste that he made no arrangements to dispose of his
timber. Though years have since elapsed he has not
returned. The logs lie in the same place, almost rotten ;
and last time I saw them a swaggie was cutting chips out
of the soundest of them to boil his midday billy. They
represent most of what remains of the cedar industry.

“ Wyoming ” : Am just now slogging-in as
assistant to the secretary of an Agricultural Show.
It is one of the greatest games on earth. The
secretary (who has to cultivate a genial manner
towards even the greatest bores in existence) lives
a joyous life in being interviewed by every con
ceivable kind of crank, from the man who want-
to know why there is no prize for white ants or s
locusts, to the woman who insists on entering a
knitted quilt in the darned sock class. Each hour
of the day assorts itself, and finds a different species
of men arguing and wrangling in the office. The
horse men come early, and it would raise your
scalp to hear their language. Those owning
trotters are the worst. The dog men are amusing.
I had an idea that a dog was a small, hairy animal
of some intelligence. I now find that this earth
was created for dogs in general, and for
each individual’s dog in particular. If
Brown’s brindle pug terrier doesn’t receive
every attention and a bushel of blue ribbons,
well, the whole scheme of Creation is a failure.
The dog men are not as many as the horse men, but
they make up for this by staying longer and talk-
ing louder. But, for a curiosity, give me the hen
men. Here I want to air a grievance. The only
hen that has any value for me is the bird that lays
a large, frequent egg, and is edible when properly
decapitated and cooked. I don’t care twopenn’orth
of gin for the animal’s color or shape, or the
number of feathers in its liver-wing. But the
bird that attends to eggs seems no class at all, and
as much out of place as a society woman with a
baby. At least, that is the impression hen-
fanciers’ talk gives me. The cattle men are fat
and jolly, and reasonable. The canary men don’t
gather in the office to rhapsodise about the length
of a bird’s toe-nail, but, if they commence, I
intend to fall upon them and brain them with the
entry-register. After hours of this sort of thing,
the suicidal ferocity of the infuriated printer, who
demands “copy ” for the catalogue, and threatens
to expire on your doormat unless it is forthcoming
instantly, is a mere circumstance. Besides, I
don’t mind printers; I’ve met them before.
They’ll wipe any insult out in beer.

“ Nym ” : Next to the brown snake give
me the gohanna in the bush-beauty ballot. There
is no more graceful reptile in the bush, and why
Aboriginaliars hurl their inkpots at the creature
licks me. The average “go” is a splendid insect
exterminator, and gets no credit for it But if he
licks a frying-pan placed outside a tent, the Abo-
liar writes to The B. about it, and earns beer
money. Whaffor ?

“Orthos”: Recently surprised a “go.”
which took to the river, where the water was
about 18in. deep, and so clear that I could dis-
tinctly see the reptile crawling about on the sandy
bottom. I kept it back from the bank, and it
stayed under water for an hour before I let it
crawl out.
A DISCOVERY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He stood upon the quaking kerb,
And watched the lights go up and down,
His peace no discord could disturb,
He watched the world with smile superb,
As though ’twas something: he did own,
And he had built it stone by stone.
“ I had been told that it was true,”
He said to me with look profound,
“ But disbelieved it as did you,
No doubt, but yet the fact I view,
The world is really going round ! ”
And then he fell upon the ground.
N.S.W.
