PERSONAL ITEMS.
Another record ? Wm. Boardman, having
got 12 months’ “hard” from a Bench of Vic,
honorariums, lately interviewed Justice Hodges
and Judge Chomley on the one day, and accepted
a further instalment of two years from the former
and 18 months from the latter.
Thbmas Fitzpatrick, ex-M.L.A., goes on a
world tour. He is a pioneer squatter, represented
Murrumbidgqe in N.S.W. Parliament for ten
years, and was one of the most consistent of the
little band of Protectionists who fought many
hopeless battles against Calico.
Interviewed by the Pall Mall Magazine,
John Burns, M.P., says his attention was first
turned to politics by finding a torn and soiled old
copy of Smith’s “ Wealth of Nations,” buried in
the sand at Akassa, West Africa, when he was
digging to get a foundation for a piece of ma-
chinery.
The Dowie slump is complete, a cable
announcing that the Zion deacons have taken over
control' of the city. Something of this sort was
looked for when recently Profit Howie’s chief
elder preached before GOOO Howie communicants,
in the huge Shiloh Tabernacle, on the failure of
Howie as a leader, and created a sensation by
declaring that the long-haired fakir hadn’t “mea-
sured up to the fullness of the great vision
vouchsafed him.” “Let no man attempt to
trouble me. Mispah,” cabled Howie, from the
West Indies, next day; but the elders merely
scribbled “Ichabod” across the paper and pro-
ceeded to business.
The late F. W. Haddon, quondam Argus
editor, had a claim to notoriety as chief instigator
of Fitcliett’s “Bleeds that Won the Empire.”
Fitchett’s fancy had not lightly turned to thoughts
of gore till Haddon suggested in 1896 that he
should write an occasional anniversary article on
British battles. Both were rather surprised when
numerous anaemic curates and schoolboys who
were prohibited from reading Headwood Hick
yarns started to call for more of the red, raw stuff.
However, Fitcbett continued gallantly to seek the
human entrails at the cannon’s mouth, and his di-
gests were made a weekly feature. They ran for
16 months, and ever since then the Aegus has
shown a fondness for the “penny dreadful” type
of serial. As for Fitehett, the trouble has become
chronic with him.
THE LATE GEORGE COPPIN , Comedian,
Theatrical Manager, Politician, Philan-
thropist, dc.
The daily paragraphs about Colonel
Ricardo in the Melbourne papers begin to grow
tiresome. One could manage to exist without
lumps of information concerning his youthful
experiences.at Rugby school, or even without the
knowledge that he got out of bed early a day or
two ago to test a drawing-slate notion obtained
from an American magazine. • Some people
wouldn’t care if the gallant colonel stayed out
of bed all night.
Nicholas "Willis appears to have got the
loan of the Natal press, and the impression that
Nicholas owns and runs Australia is growing very
strong. Already, the Natal press is pretty solid
on these five points : (1) That Nicholas appeared
before the Lands Commission before his departure
from Sydney, and cleared his character till it was
whiter than the unblemished snowball. (2) That
he came away by direct permission of the Judge
who ran the Commission. (3) That he is being
persecuted chiefly on account of his political
importance. (4) That he personally, and off his
own bat, supplied GOO Australian troops during
the Boer unpleasantness. (5) That he is prepared
to find 1000 more Australian troops if the South
African natives continue to create trouble, as they
have been doing of late.
C. B. Stephen, the latest N.S.W. King’s
Council, who lingered for 36 years at the junior
bar before taking the plunge into “silk,” and who
is said to expect the first vacant puisne Judge-
ship, was once offered the Chief Justiceship of
N.S.W. It was at the time when, upon the death
of Martin, Salomons was appointed, but resigned
a few days after acceptance. Darley, the present
C.J., had declined the office before Salomons
accepted it, deeming the sacrifice too great.
Want, Attorney-General, would have appointed
Owen, then at the bar, but Owen was travelling
in Europe and could not be found. After fruitless
endeavors to find Owen, Want offered the position
to C. B. Stephen, who declined it, on what ground
is unknown. Now Stephen would probably be
well content with a puisne Judgeship, but Sly
seems to have the inner running.
An ancient settler, Mrs. Creswick, of
Hawthorn (Victoria), on the 13th celebrated the
70th anniversary of her appearance in Port
Phillip. She arrived when eight years old (in 183 G)
with her father, Dr. Alexander Thomson, from
Van Diemen’s Land. The population of Mel-
bourne was then fifty souls. Her father at the
first land sale bought the half-acre at the S. W.
corner of Elizabeth and Flinders streets for £46.
It was valued in 1888 at £428,000. In the first
Parliament of New South Wales, Dr. Thomson
represented Port Phillip, his colleagues being C.
H. Ebden, Thomas Walker, Charles Nicholson,
and John Dunmore Lang. Henry Condell was
the sole representative of Melbourne. In the
first Parliament of Victoria, consisting of one
House, partly elective, the doctor represented
Geelong, and in the first elective Assembly he
represented the same unobtrusive locality.
Charles Smith M‘Phillamy, of Orton
Park, near Bathurst (N.S.W.), has joined the big
camp of squatters at the fair age of 85 years.
There were three brothers —Robert, John, and
Charles —large, stalwart Hawkesbury natives,
who crossed the Blue Mountains in 1833. Some
years before that Mr. Robert Smith was managing a
property in the West for Parson Marsden, the great
flogger and indifferent preacher. He afterwards
took upland for himself, and when he died he left
all he possessed to the M'Phillamys. They added
to the original estate until the M'Phillarny family
became about the biggest land-grabbers and the
worst obstacle in the way of settlement at
Bathurst and further out. John M’Phillamy,
one of the three, at one time represented West
Macquarie in the Legislative Assembly, and was
succeeded in the seat by his son-in-law, C. E.
Pilcher, K.C. A daughter of the recently
deceased married a brother of the K.C., solicitor
Pilcher, of Orange.
A man who knew him writes :
Old George Lansell, the gold-mining boss of Bendigo,
passed out last Sunday, after 83 strenuous years. At the
age of six years he had to climb out of his bed at Margate
(England; to get to work at 6 a.m. in a soap and candle
factory. He got sick of the labor conditions in the un-
speakable “mother country,” and came out to this land of
milk and honey, yet he always maintained a hostile
attitude towards labor unions. Perhaps it was because •
he became rich and a large employer of labor. Old George
was a model of energy and pertinacity, but, like a good
many more of his kind, lost sight of the fact that
a great portion of his wealth was due to the
accidental luck that put him on a good wicket.
About 12 years ago, when a labor dispute was on, the
conference secretary wrote to Lansell, inviting his
attendance. The Quartz King replied : “ From my youth
up I have always avoided all such organisations as that
to which you belong, and 1 trust you will take this as a
sufficient answer to your letter.” Lansell was remarkable
for his pluck in the struggling days. If he put his money
into a mine he would hang on even if he had to sacrifice
his household goods to keep the game going. Only once
did he ever get an attack of “ funk,” and drop his shares.
Almost immediately after they went up with a bound,
and Lansell took it as a reproof, for he never afterwards
lost his nerve. It was a depressing drive out to
his home, “Fortuna,” among the red sandstone
hills, but when the visitor had passed through a gate in a
brick wall, he found himself in a Garden of Eden. The
choicest fruits and shrubberies surrounding a fine
bungalow gave him an impression that he had
stepped out of the desert into an oasis. There were
underground cave-like rooms where, in the hottest
weather, an enjoyable chat could be had over
choice claret that the Quartz King had stored in
his cellars for over 20 years. The old man looked an
impressive warrior amidst his great machinery, with the
stampers beating out fortunes for him, and in liis home,
with his beautiful wife to assist him, he was a splendid
host. Last time I was there he said: “If I’d
only cut the painter years ago, I would have been a
millionaire. It costs too much to get the gold now.”
Anyway, his perseverance has been a good thing for
Bendigo district.
Thomas Bent, Victoria’s quaint Premier,
has been reminiscing some more. This time
it was to a church crowd for whom he was
opening a fete at Elsternwick. It was a
wild Saturday afternoon. The thunder
growled, muttered and roared. The
rain fell in masses the size of a boot-
trunk, and Tommy, inspired by
the elements, told, in a voice like
that of a banshee, this tale of
the dim, dead, dark, dread-
ful past. “Not far from
here, it was dangerous
to travel in those
days. I myself have
been shot at when
passing from here to
St. Kilda by the
bushrangers. I was
one of 40 men who
were taken from the
main road to Sar-
good’s paddocks and
robbed by the bush-
rangers. You ill savw
we were not very brave
men to allow them to rob
the lot of us ; but, you see,
they had pistols ; we had not.
So we had to let them do it.” Accord-
ing to Thomas, there never was a
hold-up, or a cricket match, or any
sort of unusual thing anywhere in the
State, that he was not connected with
in some formidable manner. This is
the secret of Tommy’s power. He
knew when to be on the spot. He
still knows when to be on the spot, but
he can be on a larger spot now than he
could 40 years ago.
Peter Brennan, a trades-union
stalwart, who died the other day, was
the first Labor candidate mentioned in
N.S.W. It was intended to run him
for West Sydney, but the late J. D.
Young “jumped” the claim. Bren-
nan was also a member of the first
Arbitration Court —under Barton’s volun-
tary Act. He was always a sturdy Protec-
tionist and a practical man.
N.S.W. Blue Book shows 13 William
Smiths in the civil service.
About the late George Coppin as a poli-
tician :
George Coppin, comedian, was before the public in
many other guises than that of theatrical entrepreneur,
though stage business was the only thing he ever made
money out of. He was an ardent politician and was
always years ahead of his time. In 1858 he entered the
Richmond Municipal Council, and organised a committee
to urge on local government. It was this committee
that induced Parliament to pass a Bill giving municipali-
ties the right to tax unoccupied private lands, a measure
that caused the prompt breaking up of big city and
suburban estates that had been acquired for almost
nothing in the first instance. In addition to putting in
a spell in the Assembly Coppin was a member of
the Legislative Council for five years, and saw clearly
what a cumbrous, useless business was the House of Rich
Landlords. He advocated a series of reforms tending to
lessen its Toryism, and if they had been carried out
at the time Victorian legislation would have been 25 years
further advanced than it is. He brought in a Transfer of
Land Bill, which was rejected, but three years later vir-
tually the same Bill was passed into law, and still forms
the basis of the Victorian land transfer system. Coppin
advocated the establishment of Post Office Savings Banks,
and it was through his efforts that they were ultimately
created. It was Coppin who broke down the monopoly
of the Melbourne Gas Co., by the formation .of its first
suburban rival, the Collingwood Gas Co. He" instituted
the Royal Humane Society out here, and the Old
Colonists’ Association. He was the founder of Sorrento,
and the originator of Bay excursions. In everything that
is good—mechanics’ institutes, fire brigades, citizen
soldiers, benefit societies, and all manner of other things
—Coppin’s hand was at the helm in their beginnings.
The people of Victoria owe him a huge debt of gratitude.

HER UNCLE: “Did you enjoy your birthdayl”
LITTLE GIRL: “Not too much, unsle. That rude
boy, Charley Thomas, he runned after me all
round and round the lawn. He wanted to
kiss me. But he didn’t catch me.”
HER UNCLE : “ Then why worry.”
LITTLE GIRL : “ But, uncle, I’m sorry I runned
so hard.”
Someone proposes to erect a memorial to
Henry Parkes. There is already one. in Sydney
Arcade. It is a marble bust, ordered by the late
Parkes from sculptor Sani, and it represents the
equally late Critchett Walker. It is net paid for.
It stands there a mark for the admiration, or
otherwise, of the passers-by, and that is Sani’s
share of it. He was “done in” even for the
material.
Schoolmaster Chas. Hedgelong, for 43
winters at Brisbane, will winter there no more;
aged 84. As a boy he belonged to the choir of the
famous St. Mary’s Church, which stands in three
parishes of the Cold Country—France, Tun-
bridge, and Spendhurst. A good tenor voice
secured him some prominence in later years. His
education was provided by the Dukeof Wellington,
by whom his father was employed. He voyaged,
to Queensland during the days of Herbert’s
premiership, in the Soldana. When the ship
reached Moreton Bay the yellow flag was hoisted
by the shore authorities, but the captain pulled it
down, sent the pilot about his business, and sailed
for Rockhampton. Hedgelong head-mastered the
C. of E. Giammar School at Brisbane for 14 years,
but when Griffith’s Education Act came he joined
the State service, and put in 29 years pouring
erudition into the young Bananalander.
Everybody knows that the late Geo.
Lansell, the Bendigo Quartz King, was by
trade a soap-boiler, but only a few remem-
ber George’s original boiling-down establishment,
which, in the absence of noxious trades’ restriction
laws, was situated right in the centre of what is
now Bendigo city. In those days Lansell was a
man of small pretensions, and pushed his own
barrow about the field collecting scrap-fat from
thrifty housewives. When he’d collected enough
fat he would work, and convert it into soap,
filling the atmosphere with perfume. Presently,
as Bendigo grew, there arose a fierce popular aver-
sion to George, and efforts were made to shift his
odoriferous mill, but Lansell fought hard and
clung to his site. Then compensation was offered,
and the soap-miller said that made all the differ-
ence. He took his rank fat and his boilers and
planted them further out, and the compensation
went into a mining venture and laid the founda-
tion of a exeat fortune.
TO BILLY SQUIRES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We’re living in an Educated Age,
We can order Culture daily at the door,
The cheers that round the ring-side nightly rage,
Are tilings right-thinking persons must deplore.
For the fascination of your fighting face
And the elemental passions that it fires
Are a civilised community’s disgrace ;
Still—l’d give a bit to be you, Billy Squires.
Your gory tale of battle makes one sick ;
You shouldn’t for a moment be endured ;
And we blush before the lift-boy’s rhetoric,
When we go to get our fingers manicured.
We nerve ourselves to see a brutal bout,
That all the finer human instincts kill,
And find that we’re in frenzy yelling out,
“ You’re a blooming, blessed, blazing boshter, Bill.”
And so you are. And merchants fat and staid
Who pass along the plate in sanctity,
Read up your fights, and, were they not afraid^
Would own they’re thinking just the same as me;
While literary blokes, who loudly quote
To show how superfine their point of view,
Would give up all the poems that they wrote
If they could only swing a left like you.
Vic.
C.N.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
“ Spring ” : I have witnessed some gory battles
in the ring, one railway smash, and a biograph picture of
a Spanish bull-fight, but for thrill give me a big old
smash on a bicycle track. In the ring the thrilling
sight of gore is tempered by the haze of par-
tizanship. The railway smash comes unexpectedly.
But a bike smash 1 Ah ! Under the ghostly glare on the
other side of the track 13 forms sit waiting on frail-look-
ing wheels, some near each other in front, two well back.
The track is wet, greasy, and unsafe. A shrill whistle,
and the muscles tighten. A pistol-shot, and they’re off.
All lights but those on the track vanish, and 13 silent
spectres appear to be fleeing under the moonlight from
fiends of darkness. As the wheels race over the wet track
muttering sounds come up from it. Suddenly one back
man sits up as if shot, and glides into the inner pit of
darkness. A fiend has him—the fiend of fat and
want of condition. The other shoots along in terror, and
gradually overhauls the forward lot, now dangerously
bunched and timid. The mob staggers, opens, falters,
and the onlookers experience a thrill of expectancy.
Riders at the back seem to clamor for room —room. Those
in front turn their heads anxiously from side to side,
waiting for something. At the back of the
track the troop struggles .and leaps like sheep
taking a shadow. A collision? No! They’re safe. The
bell rings for the final lap. The pace increases. Riders
call forth their hidden sprints ; and the ghastly wet
track grins in front. Twelve athletes throw caution to
the wind, and the crowd gasps. Then it happens. There
is the thud of falling flesh, the rattle of twisted machines.
Four weird spectres speed silently on as if fleeing from
the scene of their crime—the back-marker (and favorite)
last. With a splendid sprint the favorite smothers the
three and wins amidst applause. The silent figures on
the track are dragged off to make way for the next race.

Stated that Joe Darling intends forsaking Aus-
tralian cricket altogether to try his hand at sheep-breed-
ing, and bought a big estate at Mt. Barker (S.A.),
t'other day, as the first step. But Darling once retired
to a station in the wilds of Tasmania, and, a few years
later, had a place again in representative cricket —a place
that younger players had eagerly sought.
# * * *
A sensible suggestion has been made to the
N.S.W. League of Wheelmen that bike riders should be
compelled to wear skull caps for protection against
accident to the head. Such caps have proved very
helpful to jockeys in hurdle races. Of course, it may
interfere a little with cycling “ gates ” if extra provision
is made against fatalities.

“ Outsider” Writes : One of the most promising
cricketers west of the Blue Mountains is Clarry Edwards,
who has just Won the averages in the Oarcoar District
Union competition. His batting average was 45‘33, and
his bowling figures 2'82. In one match he secured 10
wickets for 15 runs, taking six wickets with consecutive
balls ; and he is only 18 years of age.
* * * *
Quite a crowd of new craft, some of them freaks,
will make their appearance at the Sydney Motor Boat
Club’s regatta on April 7th. Although the boats are
being built locally, the huge majority of the engines, sad
to say, are imported* If we had a few local foundries all
doing well, the inventive genius of our people would, in
all probability, produce a boat that would break the
world’s record, as we have broken it in other things, but
under present conditions we must always lag at the tail
of Europe and America. The Canadian Government,
which keeps armed cutters on the great lakes to prevent
Yankee fishermen poaching, recently replaced compara-
tively new coal-driven boats with motor craft of tremen-
dous'speed. The Department had received word that the
Yankees were fitting out poachers of considerable swift-
ness. The result was an exciting chase. The Canadian
armed boats poured a fire of Jib. shells round the fleeing
poachers from a Vickers-Maxim gun, worked while the
craft was running full speed. The Yankees replied with
a Colt automatic gun, and strove to keep the revenue
boats off till the dividing line of British and American
water was reached. The Canadian boats, however, were
too fast, and the poachers were overhauled just on the
line, where a Yankee cruiser was waiting to protect them
once they got across. The engines of the Canadian boats
were of local manufacture, and the firms that made them
are now doing a booming trade,

They have set to work to deal it out to the jug-
germote in Germany. All cars have to be numbered on
sides and back, and when the scorcher runs over and kills
a bread-winner, he has to support the widow and
all dependents in the state to which they have
been accustomed —the widow for life, the children
till they are able to take care of themselves.
This is equivalent to saying that the single motorist shall
always marry the widow of his first victim. If he only
injures the breadwinner, he has not only to pay the
victim’s expenses, but to also maintain wife and chil-
dren till the b. w. is able to get to work again. This sort of
thing is calculated to make running over people beastly
unpopular.
* * * *
The petrol cars were hopelessly beaten at the
great speed meeting on Florida Beach (U.S. A.). Marriot,
as before mentioned, covered the mile in his Stanley
steam car in a little more than 28sec., or at the rate of
over 127 miles an hour. The highest speed a petrol car
could make over the same distance was 30 2-ssec., ora
little over 118 miles an hour. A curious feature of the
business was that the same drivers often failed to get the
same speed out of the same cars over the same track, and
Marriot was several times unable to make up to his own
record. The Englishman, Clifford Earp, covered 100
miles in 75min. 40 2-ssec., equal to 80 miles at hour.
This shows how the speed drops when it comes to
travelling the long distances that are so glibly worked
out to show the enormous speed of these short dashes.
And the question that the steam car has to settle now is
whether it is as reliable for the long run as the petrol car
it has beaten for speed. Earp, in a Napier petrol car,
also won the five miles championship, getting over the
distance in 2min. 68sec., or at the rate of less than 108 J
miles per hour. With such high running gears the
secret of serial navigation is practically solved ; the only
question is how to apply the power.
• * • *
E. H. Flack, the Victorian cross-country
runner, who competed in the Olympian games in Greece
10 years ago, lost the big 23 miles race from Marathon to
Athens principally owing to the tactics of a patriotic
horseman who met him on the road, a mile or two from
home, and told him that the winner was already getting
rubbed down. Flack eased off, and was passed by
several Grecian peds. previously behind him, one of
whom, he subsequently found, won the race, q The
present Australian runners have been carefully instructed
not to take advice from kind gentlemen on horseback
who speak to the footsore and weary and take them in.
* * » *_
Defeated pugilist Fitzsimmons is still in the
limelight. When he was reconciled to Mrs. Fitz. (really
it was the other way about, but the male supremacy must
be upheld) he went to a neighboring smithy to turn a
horseshoe for luck. He was accompanied by 250 people,
and the floor of the smithy gave way. The anvil and red
hot iron went down first. Then came Fitz. and on top of
Fitz. 250 people. When they hauled him out he was
badlj cut, and a good deal singed. Now he has struck
more trouble —the landlady of the premises which
collapsed wants 500 dollars for damages to her floor.
* * * *
One thing for which the Sydney Motor Boat
Club deserves well at the hands of the community is its
effort to instruct its members in a knowledge of the
engines by which their boats are driven. At present the
slightest mishap on the water me£CKs a helpless boat
drifting about till some Samaritan tows it home. In the
majority of cases a very little knowledge of the engine
would enable the driver to rectify the trouble, and thereby
avoid being a sort of general nuisance,'to say nothing of any
mischief he may do by unintentionally kedping a lady out
long after her husband has arrived home. These lectures
on the carburetter, electric ignition, high and low tension,
and all the rest of it, will do a lot to popularise the sportc
A little risk gives a spice—too much burns the mouth.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
My Dear Arini,—
Sydney, March 20, 1906.
To every evil there’s a recoil of good. Mrs.
Allen Taylor, Sydney’s Mayoress, has gastric
influenza, and was so saved from attending the
first Flag Flap of the year given by the British
Empire League women. The meeting was further
. prostrated by the absence of Bruce Smith, but
non. sec. Mrs. Farrell rendered first aid to the
sorrowing with the news that, next Thursday, at
Randwick, Bruce, all dressed up in a light new
Autumn overcoat, and sprayed with bay rum, and
rubbed up with brilliantme, will give them vibra-
tions on duty to the Empire and Flag-flapping. So
we are warned. On the night of the lecture we
shall be crabs. One luminous hole in the meeting
was that knocked by Julian Ashton’s paper on
“Artin Australia.”
“Who is Mrs. Norman Shelley?” half this
State has been asking. The other half probably
knew already that she was a Miss Pell (sister of a
once well-known man-about-town, Reggie Pell
and daughter of Professor Pell, a great mathe-
matician, who drafted the A.M.P. Society’s
first tables of rates and things), and that
she married a wine and spirit merchant,
Norman Shelley. Her case against a Baronet
Charles Ross, who rented her flat in Park-lane at
12 guineas a week, and then broke into a sealed
room and destroyed her lovely gowns by bad
packing, has advertised her (amongst women at
least) better than if she had solved the riddle
of the Sphinx. The flat had seven bedrooms
and two reception rooms and the secret chamber.
Sir Chawles saw, presumably through the key
orifice, what looked like bodies suspended from
brass rods that stretched across the apartment.
The bold baronet forced open the door and dis-
covered that the dangling bodies were calico bags.
In each bag was a gown masterpiece including :
A white chiffon and lace evening frock that cost £520
at Callot Soeurs ;
A black chiffon frock, beautifully embroidered with
gold, on which Paquin had lavished his skill;
A white accordeon-pleated evening frock ;
Masterpieces from the Maison Rose.
And heavenly hats. All these the baronet
crammed into boxes and sent to some old address
to await Mrs. Shelley’s return from the Conti-
nent. Packers and male modistes from great
London houses gave evidence in court, and the
reports say that Mrs. Shelley gave the retort
brilliant several times to a hostile Bar. She
was awarded somewhere about £3OO damages.
Mrs. Shelley, when here, was a very smart
dresser, dark, of the buxom type, and lived for
some years at Edgecliff-road, Woollahra.
Miss Zaida Ben-Yusuf is the Arabian-sounding
name of a young lady now visiting Sydney.
American press notices mention her as a contribu-
tor to well-known magazines. She lived for eight
months in Japan, as much as possible after the
style of a native woman, studying the people,
their domestic life and their art and education,
and after returning to America, she lectured to
various audiences on “Unfamiliar Japan.” When
Sydney absentees return to town from mount and
stream and sea. Miss Ben-Yusuf. may give them
drawing-room samples of her chrysanthemum-
ming, illustrated with lantern slides.
“Am considering the flotation of a company
for the manufacture of ‘ Silver Photo Frames for
Wedding Presents’ right here on the spot where
the raw material comes from.” That from a cor-
respondent at Broken Hill. It seems that the
friends of a fashionable pair who married lately in
Melbourne gave them 24 silver photo, frames.
Captains Balmain and Fitzpatrick were amongst
those who sent silver frames to the honey-bun
pair. So doubtless the Frame is the thing,
Although the givers don’t enshrine their own
faces in those frames, they are secretly hopeful
that the bride will do so, and when later they call
and see Julius Knight or .Eugenie Duggan looking
out from the silver of the hollow square they are
secretly mad.
Dr. and Mrs. Chisholm Ross left for a trip to
Japan and Canada by the Prinz Sigismund on
Saturday. Other Monday departers were Mr.
and Mrs. Ebenezer Vickery, who go by the
American boat on a belated honeymoon. A lot of
brides and grooms seem to like to spend the first
weeks of their life sentence in the neighborhood
of the place- where they were sentenced; and,
later, when a bit tired of looking love to eyes that
speak, again, they go.planet-worrying and scuffling
over the masterpieces of Nature.
Another treatise (the first chapter is appended)
which I shall write in the days when I get a nib
to suit me, will be one which I shall call
The Unwrinkled Beast.
Mrs. Flores, a very pretty woman, whose fair fame and
taste in dress are unassailable. I do not describe her
“ beast ”in the gross sense of the word. I might substi-
tute “animal” for “beast”—the Unwrinkled Animal;
but that would pre-suppose for Mrs. Flores the possession
of a soul. Humans who have souls are still animals.
Humans who have no souls are beasts. Mrs. Flores has
no soul. ...
The first time I heard Mrs. Flores’ voice it was giving
directions to a waiter about “ the caviare sandwiches . . .
and soup in a jelly . . . mind the salad dressing . . . the
cream . . . the hamper . . . the carriage . . . my fur
cloak. . . . We can have a late dinner when we return.”
She was speaking behind me at a luncheon-table at a
grand hotel, and her voice was clear, rather thin and
very staccato. It was not ail unpleasant voice, yet it
made me think of parched peas dropping on a tin plate,
and I turned in my chair to look at its maker. O, but she
was a cunning, cast in clay ! Large grey-blue eyes with
long brown lashes looked out of the cat-shaped oval of her
face, and the cheek had almost the pure outline of girl-
hood. Light-brown hair beautifully coiffed on a head that
left no room for brains, a small straight nose, and a narrow
forehead, with which the big eyes were nearly level. Al-
most they gave the impression of not being socketed. Her
small ears—in each was a three-hundred-guinea pearl—
seemed to encroach on her pink dimpled cheeks, and her
mouth seemed fashioned to say “ cream,” “ fur cloak ” and
“ caviare sandwiches.” When the moist red lips were in
repose they took an expression of material satisfaction as
if the thinking apparatus of the Beast was signifying that
“ the salad dressing was just to my liking.” Usually Mrs.
Flores kept the short fingers of her well-manicured hands
extended, being mindful of their jewelled condition. But
when she closed them into the palms and laid the hands
on her silk and lace lap, they suggested kittens curling
themselves up for sleep and seeking the softest bed. Her
figure was very good aiid, though full, was without a
suggestion of grossness, and her neck was a piece of
columnar perfection.
At first sight I took Mrs. Flores to be about 26 years of
age, with perhaps one infant. After-knowledge proved
y ears and five children, the eldest a boy of
16. All five were at boarding-schools in Europe. Their
parents principal home was in London, but Mrs. Flores
was Australian-born, and to this continent her husband
and his beautiful Bedst paid prolonged visits. Mr. Flores
was a fleshy, sallow man of about 50, who used to boast,
in a North of Ireland voice, that his wife never had an
hour of anxiety, worry, or sickness in her life, and that
. f fiad gratified her every whim. His children he
idolised, but regretted that his sons should show no apti-
tude for enny wark that brings in the monny.” Most
of their studies, he said, were useless, and he was filled
with exasperation that he could find no school of note
where the masters did not place money-mailing on a
lower plane than mind-making. When Mr. Flores was
introduced to a woman not really middle-aged or old, he
looked her over slowly, his scrutiny starting from
her knees, then travelling upwards, and ending
with her hair. It never got as far as her hat. She knew
it was not her dress but her figure he was scanning. He
was making pertain.“that his article was better than the
one before him.. When he joined his wife in the corridor
of the hotel for lunch or dinner he never failed to eye her
closely. She would be talking—she talked incessantly
after the manner of empty vessels—either to him or to a
neighbor, but he would be deaf until he assured himself
that the Beast showed no sign of deterioration in condi-
tion or gowning since he had left her three hours pre-
viously. . One noon when he came in, his wife, in a 30-
guinea pink silk muslin gown, was sitting' under a stained
glass window, through which the light came greenly. Mr.
Flores saw the greenish pallor, and mistaking the cause,
stood horrified. He declared that she. had been over-
tiring herself. She took no notice, but continued in her
parched-pea-and-tin-plate voice an account of a difference
she had had at a glove counter with a saleswoman. A
magazine was lying open near the pink silk elbow. Flores
pounced upon the book and read aloud in his own Doric
the name of the article, “The Stellar YVurruld.”
“So this is what ye have been knocking yourself up
with,” he exclaimed. “Madge, d’ye mean to tell me ye
have been reading this 1 Who set ve on ? Haven’t you
all you want in this wurruld ?”
“ I never even saw that book before,” replied the lady,
and continued her glove-contest recital.
When she rose to go in to luncheon he saw the cause of
her discoloration, and, in his relief, kissed her rapturous]y.
Once someone spoke in Flores’ presence of a certain
man as being broken-hearted through the death of his
wife. Flores seemed interested, and asked if she had
been a “ comely woman.” When it transpired that she
was 50 years of age he smiled unpleasantly and began to
talk of something else. \
The Unwrinkled Beast was accurately mated.
Mrs. Flores filled, her life to overflowing with shopping,
appointments with dressmakers, and the theatres. She
delighted to drive through crowded streets, to dine at
the table d’hote in evening dress, and in card-playing.
Like many otherwise stupid persons, she was a capital
Bridge and whist player. She craved noise, light, move-
ment, and luxurious environment. The preservation of
her physical condition by' means of baths, massage,
and hair-shampoos took up considerable time.
She never read a book, or, in fact, anything
but personal journalism. She rarely spoke of
her children, and if she did it was in such remarks as
“Bertie has blue eyes like mine,” or “ Juliette’s hair is
the most beautiful copper shade—like mine was at her
age.” She hated to entertain, for that would mean some
self-sacrifice, or loss of ease, or the practice of tact.
Entertaining requires some brains. And she cared little
for entertainment that was not directly bought and paid
for. She would sit out a Wagnerian concert with
pleasure, and without a yawn, if there was a crowded
and fashionable audience, and if she were accompanied by
several friends with whom she would, later, have an
expensive supper.
Every man would glance admiringly at Mrs. Flores.
Many sought her acquaintance, looking as if they would
like to make the relations friendly. She received
them brightly, and lavished attention on them and
chit-chatted them blind, but presently they drifted away
not to return. Perhaps her husband was too much with
her to suit them ; perhaps she talked too much. Pos-
sibly she lacked the emphasis of sex. But she would
frequently glance around the crowded dining-hall, com-
ment on the best-looking men present, and remark, with
a certain complacency, that all the fortune-tellers had
prophesied for her early widowhood. She, baffled study
of her for the reason that “ into the depths of emptiness
no one can see far.” Now, whenever I see a sleek cat
sitting on agate-post and intently watching the traffic in
a busy street lam reminded of Mrs. Flores. The outlook
on life of woman and cat is of about the same depth and
breadth.
A correspondent writes me from Queensland:—
, Brisbane was probably the greenest city in Australia
last week. The 17th of March is a great day in that capi-
tal. Shop windows were specially dressed in grass and
emerald hues. Some were in tableau style with maps of
Ireland hanging about, while one place displayed a square
foot of brown-looking stuff, labelled as peat from the bogs.
The night (Friday) before St. Patrick’s Day, the mem-
bers of the local Irish Association banquetted on
fish and cakes and a certain amount of whisky.
The day of the procession Queensland left off work, and
for the most part Brisbane went forth to see the sight.
An array of vehicles, with horses and bullocks hitched on,
and a great procession in green hangings and decorations,
made up a good but ordinary kind of show. But there
was one startling feature. A trolly representing the
Garden of Eden carried a youth and maiden attired in
flesh-colored tights and fig-leaf aprons, and between them
stood an apple-tree laden with rosy fruit. There ought
to have been a stuffed python trailing behind to repre-
sent the Serpent, but that lengthy deceiver was absent.
After all, what had Adam and Eve to do with St. Pat-
rick’s Day in Brisbane? And they didn’t even tuibble off
the waggon once to represent the Fall.
A recent Leichhardt wedding notice, Ramsay-
de Mestre, recalls a couple of ancient Australians.
The grandfather of the bride, Prosper de Mestre,
was amongst the wealthiest of Sydney merchants
and ship owners as far back as the twenties and up
to the late forties, when he retired to his estate at
Shoalhaven. The father of the bride was well-
known on the turf for many years, as breeder,
owner and trainer of some of the best race horses
that have competed for big prizes hereabouts.
Ex-army Chaplain Brindle, now R.C. Bishop of
Nottingham, who stage-managed Princess Ena’s
’version, shows in his pictures as areal “Hand-
some Harry,” with seven decorations on his
chest and an irresistible peaked cap above his
aquiline features. If the R.C. Church could lure
a confessor of this bijou type out here many
society women would ’vert, and a lot of Bridge
tables be left as permanent stands for the potted
palm.

vious to the Rawsons, and gave lavish parties,
and wore rare jewels and hurrah clothes, is stay-
ing at a hotel in Johannesburg, a friend writes
me.
One of the surprises of Sydney Society last week
was the re-marriage of a Randwick lady to the hus-
band whom she divorced a year or so ago.
Miss Newton, of kindergarten fame, left by the
Sonoma on Monday for what, in journalese, is
known as a well-earned holiday. With her went
two Sydney girls—Miss Carlyle Thomas and Miss
McPhee—to study kindergartening at Chicago.
The Maori is still evoluting. A certain borough
in a M.L. province has been memorialised by a
Maori church committee for its patronage for a
combined Maori and pakeha concert: —
Having exhausted all efforts, besides giving up large
portions of our lands, in the maintenance of our houses
of worship, we are finally compelled to practise and adopt
the ways and means of our pakeha friends.
Henceforth the prettiest wahines will be called
upon to sell button-holes whilst their fathers and
brothel's look to the raffling of sweet-potatoes,
pigs and pumpkins. It’s a pathetic thing to see
the noble Maori compelled by poverty to work for
his halo along these lines.
Artist Souter has done the design for a post-
card to advertise “The Glass of Fashion,” to be
played at the Criterion by the Histrionic Society
on April 7, in aid of the Thirlmere Home for
Consumptive Women. There are said to be 500
poor women in Sydney suffering from phthisis,
and no room for them in any < ire place. Thirl-
mere Home committee has £2OOO in hand. If it
can get another £4OOO, it will enlarge its premises,
and accommodate most of those unhappy five
hundred.
The “Light of the World” picture, now in Syd-
ney Art Gallery, is drawing bigger crowds than did
the original Messiah in the days before Imperial
Caesar was turned to clay or Pontius Pilate asked
jesting questions. Parsons come in groups and
gaze bareheaded, working men and women,
typists in bunches, parties of ladies from the
Piper, Potts, or Darling Points, mortgage sharks,
clubmen, office boys—they come as only people
come to a free show. Mr. Charles Booth, Jhe
owner of the picture, is certainly an altruist, for
he has sent the canvas on its travels for the good
of humanity with a staff of two—the redoubtable
Mr. Mack Jost and another. Great majority of
the gazers are quite sure to see in the picture what
Ruskin has told them is there. But the minority
just side-track Ruskin, and criticise the brilliant
and deep-toned emotion-and-moral canvas, and
the criticisms are not all the essence of admira-
tion.
Milliners would learn a hat or two by see-
ing West’s pictures at the Palace. The coy
hat, the skittish hat, the hat to which one would
lose one’s heart and money ; hats of many lands
and eras, shading oi displaying the most start-
lingly life-like girls’ faces. The Martenengo
girls, who. are part of the Brescian troupe,
are much alike—regular-featured, plump, star-
eyed and black-haired, with very pleasant voices.
At the Victor Daley fund matinee, Margaret
Thomas will sing the poet’s “ Love is a Fadeless
Flower,” which has been set to music by May
Summerbelle. Lauri, who will give his “Gar-
dener’s Dream,” is working like a beaver for the
success of the programme in conjunction with
Edwin Geach, who is working like three beavers.
Mrs. F. Aronson has, up to date, sold about half
the circle’s worth of tickets.

out here.
Baritone Roberts, whom Sydney rather liked in
musical comedies, will return to Sydney and work,

At the bazaar in aid of the Lewisham Hospital,
to be opened on the 28th, Miss Asche, whose re-
putation as a Fine Artist in cookery equals Hol-
man Hunt’s standing in his art, will organise, cook

ments and sweets that will rear its bulk among

Miss Asche.
Sydney society has a grievance against Mrs.
Willie Mitcliell-Godwyn, It says she doesn’t look
younger than when she went away, and that her
gowns are no longer highly specialised and daring,
and that she has ceased to say smart things.
Ethel Turner’s eldest Little Australian took
part in a schools’ swimming contest at the Spit
aths last Saturday. Two little (Dr.) Arthurs
also swam like fishes. The child is, I suppose,
nearer the tadpole than its parents, for the
readiness with which youngsters take to the
water amazes the full-grown woman, who,
after fooling in the briny deep for six years,
spends a summer (with an instructor’s exclu-
sive services) trying to get the right stroke.
Little Marjorie Williamson, after three lessons at
Manly, can swim over-arm or breast-stroke, float
and dive. Which reminds me that the last-named
small girl’s picture (from Longstaff’s painting)
forms the Christmas supplement to London
Graphic.

Victoria’s new Supreme Court Judge, Leo
Finn Bernard Cussen, is a comparatively young
Australian, born at Portland (Vic.) 47 years ago ;
and into those 47 years he has crowded a bit.
Finishing off at Melbourne University, he passed
in Civil Engineering in ’79, and gained an exhibi-
tion in engineering and a scholarship in mathe-
tics. For the next four years he surveyed for the
Railway Department, between times studying for
his 8.A., which he took in’B4, and then for his
LL.B. and M. A. degrees, gained in ’B6, in which
year he was admitted to the Bar. Since then he
has built up a valuable and well-founded practice,
besides lecturing at the University on the law of
obligations and international lay. His salary is
£5OO less than that of the other puisne Judges,
viz., £2500, with pension rights. The Cabinet’s
choice finally lay between him—the best available
man for the position—Mitchell, K.C., and under-
study-Minister Mackey.
The movement in the lowa (U.S.A.)
Legislature to legalise the painless killing of hope-
lessly incurable patients at their own instance is
not the novelty some of its critics suppose. A law
authorising that much, and a good deal more, was
in operation in Greece before the days of Chris-
tianity. Not only were persons afflicted with
disease permitted to plead for extinction,
but persons who felt themselves unequal
to the battle of life, or who regarded them-
selves as irretrievably ruined, could go before the
Areopagitica, or High Court of the time, and on
satisfying the Judges that the case was as repre-
sented, they received a certificate sanctioning
self-destruction. The certificate had a use.
Ordinarily the property of a person committing
suicide was confiscated, and the body buried
without funeral rites; the certificate excused the
remains from those penalties.
“ Mahlsfcick ” writes :
The droves and hordes of gaping people are under an
illusion that Holman Hunt painted his first “ Light of the
World” in Palestine. Not so. Hunt was born in 1827
and got hung in the Royal Academy at 19, but for some
years his pictures did not sell well, and he thought of
emigrating to Australia as a farmer. However he tackled
the “ Light of the World” in 1852 and 1853. It was ex-
hibited in 1854 and made its furore. Then he went to
Palestine. I think his finest thing is the face of the young
Jesus in the “Finding in the Temple.” The Jews who posed
as his models were threatened with excommunication.
His first replica of the “ Light of the World ” was smaller
than the original; the second, now in Australia, is much
larger than the original. The three are exactly alike, so
far as he could make them, and yet the original is the
most beloved. The eyes are said to possess a magnetism
not in the others. There is considerable variation in his
two paintings of the “ Triumph of the Innocents.” The .
first, painted on linen in Palestine, was supposed to be
spoilt by the material, but a restorer made it all right.
The second was done on canvas when the first was thought
to be doomed.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, March 20, 1906.
My Dear Yarrie,—
The “off to Europe” craze is raging so furi-
ously that, till the extreme end of the cold season,
every sort of outward-bound ship is booked from
hatchway to forecastle, and wealthy passengers
are camped on the rigging or hanging on to the
anchor. Even the hot season at the terminus of
’O6 is heavily marked off by voyagers. On the
cheap route the hooking continues right up to the
Easter of 1907. Some of the outgoershave booked
their pet dogs. One madame has marked off deck
and barking-space for .two terriers. The shipping
clerk has some queer yarns to tell of the travelling
craze. Every voyaging female demands informa-
tion of her cabin mates. “ Don’t put me in with
a cat,” moans the skirted one. And the official
solemnly promises to personally study the amia-
bility of her cabin mate. At least he does that
if he is at all sensible. It saves discussion, and
makes no difference in the end.
A batch of beauty ships to the Cold Country by
the Mooltan this week. Mrs. Turnbull and a
spring chick, Mrs. Niall and a similar consign-
ment, and all the banker Urquhart’s flock get
aboard this Tuesday. Toorak will turn out in
full force to wave its lace hanky at the pilgrims.
Rupert Clarke, his brother Ernest Clarke, and
Robert Clarke, the veteran stock-breeder, were
humming along in a motor-car, a few days ago,
between Kangaroo Ground and Eltham, when the
wheels skidded and the whole party was thrown at
the ground at a fearful number of miles per hour.
The only one badly hurt was Robert Clarke, who
broke his arm and had to be taken to a
mending establishment. Rupert the gay was
shaken up, and billed next day as suffering from
shock. Ernest, the silent naval man, escaped
without a scratch, The motor was brought back
to Melbourne in a number of bags.
Justice Barton, with a pocket edition of himself
in the form of a curly-pated son, and the eldest
son of Justice O’Connor, held one box against a
full house at the frolicsome Princess farce on
Saturday. In the box opposite was perched
Rupert Clarke, convalescing after his motor spill.
With the sport was a honey-tressed Miss in
gorgeous raiment. In a third pen a very decollete
dazzler made eyes. A fourth pen had for chief
star Miss Margot Gavan Duffy.
A great sensation was created at one of the
race meetings by the sight of a long-severed
husband and mate terracing it together. The
surprise sent a thrill from one end of the gaping
stand to the other. It reminds me of the day
when a much-detached couple made a joint
appearance in Menzies’ eating-room. The spoons
fell into the soup, the knives rattled to the table,
and an attack of acute lockjaw seemed to have
seized the whole assemblage as the temporarily
reunited couple passed together down the room.
New Justice Leo Cussen is no social light.
Despite a fat income and large popularity, the
Chamber lawyer hugged the hearth in winter and
limited his outlook to a garden hedge in the hot
season. He looked the while like a thoughtful
farmer, in town on a matter of the transference of
some grazing earth or in search of a new harrow.
He drives a ramshackle phaeton, in which he
packs as many of the young Cussens as possible.
There is an octave of sons and one ewe-lamb.
Music is worshipped in the Cussen household, and
the new Judge has a brace of nieces who are great
in song and somewhat addicted to the violin.
Justice Box, the other recently elected, is a Mel-
bourne Club lion, but he has the early-Benedict
horror of the frou-frou sex and avoids mixed com-
pany. Society has gained nothing by the election
of either Unsociable except such facility as the
extra judicial strength will give for the hearing of
its divorce cases.
At a suburban kindergarten, young White Aus-
tralia occupies by day the benches and desks
graced by a school of Chows at night. The marms
who run the establishment hold evening classes
for the cabbage John who is training to make
love in nicee Engleesh and to break the Ten Com-
mandments like a Clistian.
The “Voice That Breathed” suddenly broke
the Lenten reserve on Wednesday last and accom-
panied five or six mating-pairs to the starting-
rails. The couple of most stylish interest were
Miss Annie Bullivant and Mr. F. W. Baily, who
were mated for better or worse by the parson
with the unpropitiousname, Rev. C.*Drought. St.
John’s Church, Toorak, was given a festive air by
a great scheme of arch and bell decorations.
Ivory satin and leagues of Honiton lace formed
the bridal array. A trio of maids, Misses Flor-
ence Bullivant, Kitty and Gerty Austin, added a
touch of splendor to the scene by novel turn-outs
of foamy silk and chiffon and 18-carat gold coatees
in lustrous galon. Crowning their three thatches
were gold wreaths. The festivities wound up to
“Bull and Bush” strains at “ Larundel,” the
snug Toorak home of the Bullivants.
At another Wednesday knot-tying—that of Miss
Kathleen Husband and Mr. Charles Kirby-
four groomsmen were on picket duty. All
last season one groom sufficed. But the old order
changeth. By the way, Miss Husband was the
beauty bride of the day. She’s a piquant
brunette.
One local man of the Bar has been “ best man ”
nine times, but at last comes the turning of the
worm. He refuses to stand again. “I’m sick to
death,” he explains, “ of ‘ seeing fellows through,’
as they facetiously call it. And I hate the very
thought of weddings. I’m dog-tired of best-
manning it, of kissing the blushing bride, and
making a fool of myself when the speeches start.
I’m tired of looking after the ring, of fastening
the nervous groom’s collar-stud, of finding his hat
and his watch and his courage for him. I’m dead
sick of getting drunk the night before, and of.
wiring love and kisses for the idiots every hour of
the fatal morning. I’m nauseated with the
ecstasies of the maniacs over the commonplace
bride-elect. I’m full up of propitiating the
relatives, and practising wiles and tricks and
telling lies for both parties. If another so-called
Eal asks me to ‘see him through,’ 111 punch his
ead.”
The gown worn by the bride at the Oliver-
Murray wedding boasted four skirts. The nether-
most was made of pale-pink glace, above that
came white chiffon, over that again white crepc-
de-chine, and on top of all a gauze woven of spun
glass. The ivory satin train had a lining of blush
color.
Some of the she sex take their joy curiously. A
charming dame went into raiment of great glad-
ness as soon as her spouse was out towards the
Heads on his lonesome to the Cold Country. This
was probably to disguise her sorrow. But he re-
turned a month or two ago, and ever since the
lady has sported habiliments of woe. Now,
can it be to dissemble her joy?
Mummer Gerrard takes his sombre brow and
boot-trees, his gay vests, his stays, and his other
adventitious aids to beauty to a sheep-station for
a few months, to recuperate. Meanwhile, the
worship-fever drops 40 degrees. There was so
much beautiful promise about the gorgeous
Gerrard and so little performance. He complains
he never had a fair chance. It’s no use making
eyes at Douglas. He talks shop or horse or
pantomime with gusts, and has no tender theme
for the sighing Eve. This came as a great blow
to the first feminine swarm that crowded round.
Now Douglas is allowed elbow-room, and the
sigping of photographs has fallen off to two or
three a day.
All the Scotch and a considerable swarm of the
Emerald element attended Miss Maggie M‘Gann’s
off to-Yankeeland benefit concert. She is the
best burr balladist the State owns, and by per-
sisting exclusively in Highland ditties has estab-
lished herself as the local Jessie Maclachlan. The
benefit resulted in a fat sum that should take the
cheerful lassie well on her way. She now sheds a
smile and a tear on the rural audiences that know
her best through this Bent State, and goes a-war-
bling through Maoriland to further swell the
travelling fund. In Uncle Sam’s Land Miss
M‘Cann thinks to make dollars by the lorry-load.
The Caledonian Societies offer a welcome —hence
the trip.
The dog cause (I speak particularly and with no
reference to the Dry Dog) suffers considerably by
the death of Argus Editor Haddon. He started
exhibiting nearly forty years ago and persisted in
dog-fancying till he died. The Kennel Club was
F. W. Haddon. A wife and daughter are left to
help the dog cause along. The fad for fancy-
doggmg runs through the family.
Captain Crichton—the new Talbot caddy—
landed by the Marmora the other day, and
entrained for the Pivot to start to heel in the
Governor’s wake. He is a younger son of Lord
Erne, a not too wealthy Irish Earl who has a
reputation as an ancedotist and is as keen as your
Rev. Dill Macky or our Rev. Fierce Canary about
the Walls of Derry. Mr. Crichton himself is an
officer of one of the Foot-guards regiments; his
clubs are the “Marlboro’ ” and “The Bachelors” ;
and his intention in coming to Australia is pro-
bably the strictly honorable one of economising or
marrying.
Miss Gertrude Alger, who fiddles more than
cleverly, gets a trip-to-Europe benefit presently.
Her supporters draw dazzling pictures of plat-
form fame in store for the young lady, who up to
date is an artist only as far as her violin is con-
cerned. Some calm, cool, well-balanced friend
might quietly pass a-slab of advice to the effect
that she, like every other gifted musician, should
cultivate a charming address and gather some
brain and soul together as well as some additional
skill at music-making. That ad vice was ladled
out to Miss Una Bourne —the most brilliant of all
Melbourne pianistes who have gone Europewards.
What’s the use of technique without a savor of
personality—the secret magnetism that should
flow out from a performer and wrap itself round
the feelings of an audience? One wants the vital
spark every time. And the aspirant who goes off
to further cultivate a musical gift has no hope of
ultimate success unless she can also develop the
temperament and give it a chance.
An Australasian scribe has announced that an
extremely stylish politician is at present in London
with his wife. The particular husband is still in
Australia attending to business, and he will pro-
bably send along inquiries as to the cause of the
trouble. That’s how the forward, anxious scribe
makes trouble. A few years ago a Justice from
another State was interviewed at a shade beyond
the peep of dawn, and, the scribe seeing a bright,
youthful person in the room at a matutinal egg,
there resulted a touching story in a weekly of the
elderly Justice and his young wife, and the bloom
of health and happiness on her cheek, and her up-
to-date clothes, and her obvious devotion. The
sorrow that followed that story when the elderly
authentic wife read it is not ended yet. It was a
disastrous case of forward journalism.
Mrs. Reggie Morrison looms at present as a
most attractive frivoller. Her brunette coloring
and general sparkle, joined to a pretty wit, place
her easily top of a sadly depleted list of local
charmers.
The death of artist Ramsay, at the age of 28, is
a loss to the brotherhood. At 18 he did some
promising things as a Gallery student, and later
sailed for London. Work and the frightful fogs
affected bis health. Two or three years ago he
come back invalided, with a great collection of
portraits and life studies, which were shown at
“Myoora,” Toorak, during Melba’s season in
Australia —shown there through the kindly in-
terest of the Diva, whose life-size portrait was at
that time about three-quarters finished. A few
more years and young Ramsay might have made
a stir with his skill at portraits. As it is, some of
his best have the true line, the flesh-tint, and
actual expression.
Magistrate Panton passed an impressive bless-
ing on the picture show of Isaac Cohen, the
winner of the 1905 National Gallery Scholarship,
the other day. All along, the autocrat of the City
Court has espoused the cause of that artist. The
canvas that secured for Isaac the travelling ticket
has for its subject, “Reconciliation.” It deals
with the return of a beautiful sinner of the
“Magda” order to the arms of a careworn,
poverty-stricken mommer. The radiantly-decked
Magda is on her knees in a sea of frou-frou at the
feet of the old lady, who casts a downward bene-
diction of love on the coppery head of the repen-
tant daughter. A small-slcirted innocent stands
at the foot of a hastily-disturbed tea-table, contem-
plating the reconciliation with stupidly wide,
child-like eyes. The story is cleverly told in well-
applied paint.
The Cohen Show has several interesting ex-
hibits in pastel, oil, crayon, and in color-sugges-
tions. There’s one strenuously-drawn figure of a
Sandow student, the muscles like wharf ropes
drawn taut along the upper arm and chest. One
portrait, that of Herr Louis Hattenbach, suffices
to demonstrate the Hebrew youth’s skill at repro-
ducing a face and an expression to life, two half-
nude studies, showing the back of a girl, are
good in their truth to Nature in line and color.
In one, the girl of the 'back has her face reflected
in a mirror in the Velasquez “Venus” manner.
Mr. Cohen trips off to Paris in a few weeks. By
the way, he might give the picture-fancier some-
thing other than his Hebraic brethren in oil and
chalk. Nearly all his present portraits represent
the Chosen. Of course the Chosen have the
money to buy pictures with.
Director Bernard Hall is not always in full
accord with the decisions of the National
Gallery Board. The present Cohen case is an
instance. The Director thought he saw traces of
lack of originality in the young man’s interpreta-
tion of “Reconciliation,” and said so. But the
Board thought otherwise. Prom this forth com-
petitors at the Gallery are to be allowed to select
their own stories for illustration. This causes
satisfaction. The limitations of a set subject
narrowed the students woefully. “Reconcilia-
tion,” for instance, suggests only two or three
situations, and these didn’t, perhaps, appeal to
two of the whole team of starters.
Mrs. Seton Williams and Mrs. Tom Staughton
feasted a great shoal of Toorakites last Friday at
St. Neots, the domicile of the elder Staughtons.
A gay programme of fancy dancing (with graceful
Miss Green directing things at the pianoforte), of
palmists, bridge, tinned strum, canned song
and brass band made the time fly gaily. It was a
novel bill for a private show, and went down like
a spiced scandal. Mrs. Williams appeared to ad-
vantage, as the dramatic critics say, in ivory satin
with saucy decorations of gold (to match her
sovereign hair) and dawn-pink touches. A dawn-
pink posy crowned the picture garb. Mrs. Tom
Staughton’s rig ran to a demure black chiffon
velvet, and she sported a chaste white bouquet as
a foil.
Miss Florence Lloyd has lots of big strong
teeth, a frank “ What-ho ” sort of smile that
seems a permanent decoration, and a long sweep
of festive limb to carry her through the part of a
costly siren in “ There and Back.” The chiffons
are of no account. They’re no aid to the Lloyd
looks. Skirts seem to hang awkwardly on the
form that rollicks and shapes so well in tubular
male costume.
Bendigo rushed to congratulate its Dolly
Castles when she returned the other day as a
leading member of the Gilbert and Sullivan Co.
There was a mayoral reception, and the officials
of the Austral Society, at whose competitions
the Castles sisters first achieved local fame,
turned out to do honor to the fair one with the
golden locks. The company put in six nights at
Bendigo, producing a fresh opera every night.
And the ’Gonians, whose little theatre seldom
accommodates this dainty kind of show, felt com-
forted with Gilbert, and soothed their savage
breasts with Sullivan.
Saturday saw the first inter-State ladies’ cricket
match in Australia played at Victoria Park, Mel-
bourne, between Tas. and Vic. The latter boasts
a score of girls’ cricket clubs, the former eight—
mostly around Devonport—and the players were
selected from these. Asa matter of fact, women’s
cricket soon palls on a spectator —it isn’t cricket,
neither is it a figure show, while as a joke it grows
wearisome. Both sides wore dark blue, ankle-
deep skirts; theTassies had white blouses and sailor
hats, the Vies, white jerseys and blue caps. The
Victoriennes had dispensed with corsets while the
Islanders had not, thus keeping themselves less
supple and limber than their opponents. The
home team possessed a couple of comparatively
decent over-arm bowlers, a girl who could throw
in as far and as straight as a lad of 16,
and the only batswoman who had mastered
that pretty stroke, the cut. The Tasmaniennes
fielded smartly, batted daintily, and possessed a
little, long-haired, 15-year-old wicket-keeper who
took the balls bowled and those thrown in cleverly,
without gloves; but altogether they were out-
classed, scoring but 49 in their first innings to the
Vies.’ 60 for four. Pleasing diversions were
afforded at times by the advent of copious draughts
of lemonade and other intoxicating fluids; the
concussion of a ball on a fieldswoman’s nose,
causing that delicate organ to weep copiously;
and the coming undone of a batswoman’s shoe-
lace, whereby the game was stopped for a few
minutes. But it was a calm, placid afternoon’s
amusement. Women formed the majority of the
500 or 600 spectators.
Rita Hope writes of the first pianoforte lesson
that she got from the great Leschetitzki, at
Vienna: “I am glad I did not go before I was
ready. There is an American girl staying here,
and she went a few weeks ago. Frau Bree, who
is Leschetitzki’s vorbereiter , said she should not
have gone for another two months, but I suppose
her mother made her hurry up. Well, Leschetitzki
told this girl she had no talent, and said
he was tired of listening to the Nocturne (I guess
he is) and would not let her finish it.... Frau
Bree arranged the first lesson for Miss Spaeth and
myself on January 20th. We felt rather shaky
from what we had heard. We went into a very
large room with two grand pianos and were intro-,
duced to the ‘ dragon,’ who is a little white-haired
old man. He smoked a cigar sorneof the time and
spoke with it between histeeth, which did not make
understanding any easier. We were fortunate
to find him on one of his good-tempered days.
He even came out with a word or two of English
—all he knew, I suppose. He sits at one piano
and the pupil sits at the other, and he plays little
bits to keep time. We have to play from
memory, though the music is there if we need it.
Miss Spaeth was the first to take her lesson, and
she got on very well. I played a Czerny
study, then the Chopin Nocturne and a
Chopin study. He showed me a number of
finger exercises, said I had a good hand
and would in the future have a good tone.
I believe he was pleased. When Miss Spaeth
said she was surprised he was so nice, Frau Bree
said, ‘He is not always so. Oh, no ! ’ The only
thing that spoils my pleasure at learning from
Leschetitzki is the price—25 gulden (£2 Is. Bd.)
for each lesson. This year he raised it from
20 gulden because he did not want any more
pupils. I wonder there are so many people
(myself included) who pay his price. Still, it is a
great thing to say ‘I am a Leschetitzki pupil.’”
Unlike most actors, the late George Coppin
preferred to sometimes dodge the limelight. He
always said he wanted to be buried on the quiet,
that Death was merely a transition—nothing to
make a fuss about. The old man meant what he
said, and impressed his wishes so emphatically
on his family that, when the end came, they
stood firm and declined public obsequies, though
Mayor, Councillors, Freemasons, and others were
most pressing in their offers of a co-operative
funeral pageant.
Yours Affectionately,
TRYPHENA.

They breed poetical police in Melbourne
nowadays. Spake one Constable Cartin, in an
obscene language case : “ The stream of foul lan-
guage uttered by Lacey floated in the air like the
dense smoke from a bush tire.” Lacey got “£7 or
six weeks.”
“Dick” Sly, K.C., the newly-appointed
N.S.W. Acting Supreme Court Judge, is a keen
and learned, though not a very fluent lawyer, and
was a painstaking and reliable Judge when acting
some years ago. He is a mild-mannered little
man, but a good fighter, and rams home his points
with tireless persistency. Judges know that when
he raises a point of law he is probably right, and
juries (so satirical brother sharks allege) say to
themselves—- I 'There’s poor Dr. Sly. We know
he must be right, though he can’t quite explain
how. We’d better give him the verdict.” When
he gets his verdict he generally manages to hold it
in the higher Courts. The Privy Council has
more than once justified this belief on points
raised by “Dick.”
The siege of Sana, little as was the atten-
tion it attracted at this distance, seems to have
been one of the prize horrors of the age. Sana is
in that long western strip of Arabia which Turkey
swallowed some time ago; Mukhtar Pasha —
whom the London newsboy always alluded to in a
friendly way as “Murky” Pasha when he was be-
fore the public in the war of 1877-78 —did a lot to
enlarge the Turkish dominions thereabouts. But
the Arabs rose, as they have been doing ever since
Abraham’s time, and wanted their own again. The
Turkish forces were few, and there was no
Mukhtar Pasha to lead them. Sultan Abdul
Shylock, the terrified miser with the long nose,
sent little assistance, and Sana became an
Arabian city once more ! But the Turkish garrison
lived on the flesh of dead Arabs before it gave up
the defence and broke through and fled from the
premises, and the famished Arab inhabitants ate
dead Turkish soldier surreptitiously, and out of
the 70,000 people who were in Sana when the
siege commenced only 20,000 survived to the end.
The Turk isn’t too nice an Asiatic, taking him by
and large—still he is an Asiatic, and Bruce Smith
doubtless loves him for it.

“ N.” : A boss printer tells me the propri-
etors of the Encyclopedia Britannica can only
sell in America such copies as are printed there.
So the new English edition is to be printed there,
too. A double-shotted anti-Freetrade moral.
Last year, saj r s Melbourne Age re the
famine in Japan, the total Japanese rice-crop was
17 per cent, below the average. Well, in 1903 the
wheat crop in N.S.W. and Victoria was 80 per
cent, below the average. That was something
worth calling a fail ure.
If, as is stated, the N.S. W. Agent-General
is seeking toinduceEnglish shop employees to come
out here as immigrants with the promise of higher
wages, that ought to be stopped. There are far
too many of the shop employee type in the com-
munity already. Any addition to the ranks of
wan ribbon-rollers would be a calamity.
It is reported that the Yictorian Labor
party will make a great effort in
December to capture the seats now
held by Senators Zeal, Fraser,
and Styles. The necessity of
getting rid of Zeal and Simian
Fhraser is obvious enough,
but Styles has always been
a good Protectionist and
as good a Democrat as
any Labor man, and to
attack him is sheer folly.
If Styles and three Labor
men four in all are
standing for three places,
it can only result in a
splitting of the Democratic
vote, for a great many
Labor men will not support
the party while it sinks the
fiscal issue, so long as there is
a good man with definite fiscal
views available. The Democratic
vote might win all three seats (per-
haps by narrow majorities) if no
suffrages were wasted. The same vote, split
up among four candidates, might just lose the
whole three seats by narrow majorities.
The new British Labor party has given
notice that it will accept, for one session only, the
explanation that financial difficulties stand in the
way of payment of members and old age pensions.
In other words, it will give the Bannerman
Ministry one year in which to find a way out of
these alleged financial difficulties. After that
breathing space, if the Bannerman Government,
which is a painfully ordinary old Government,
hasn’t got over the difficulties, the Labor party
will begin to shake things up.
The Major Haw-hawker inquiry drags
long at Queenscliff (Vic.). Later evidence was
largely concerned with the borrowing of coal by
officers from the regimental stores —it is said to
have been an established practice with the re-
splendent officahs to have the run of the Govern-
ment coal. At all events, the testimony as to the
borrowing was much more distinct than the evi-
dence about the prompt return of the coal. In
the latter days of the inquiry, the millinery chair-
man of the Board (Col. Waddell) developed a
habit of sitting rather heavily on barrister George
Maxwell, who was bringing out the unpleasant
alleged facts of the case. Mr. Waddell’s conduct
of the inquiry has been such as to relieve him of
some of the hostility with which the Vic. military
clique regarded him when he accepted the ap-
pointment. The report of the Board is now being
awaited without much interest.
George Reid’s anti-Socialism campaign
recalls M‘II wraith’s in Queensland in ’93. Mac’s
battle cry then was : ‘ ‘ Return the Labor party
and the banks will close up.” The election
returns were barely in, giving M‘IIwraith a
majority and the Labor party a bad defeat, when
the banks shut up with a bang which was smelt
all over Australia. Heavens, what a narrow
escape Labor had that time !

published some figures which must make the
mouths of Treasurers water. “ From the tobacco
monopoly Fiance, in 1902, obtained a net
revenue of £13,297,464; Italy, 1902, £6,419,237;
Austria, 1901, £6,107,000. Japan last year made
its tobacco monopoly the security for war loans to
the extent of £60,000,000.”

There is talk of running Charles Atkins,
i President of the Chamber of Manufactures, as a
Victorian candidate for the Senate at this year’s
election. He should get the A.N.A. vote in con-
sideration of his persistent barracking for local
manufactures, and his good work in spreading the
gospel of Australian Goods for Australian People,
and in trying to persuade the manufacturers that
Labor legislation has come to stay, and must be
met by them in a friendly commercial spirit. The
Freetrade Argus will be bound to snort at Atkins
if he candidates, and the curse of the Argus
availeth much towards a man’s election.
The sugar districts of Bananaland look
vastly more prosperous since the clays of the bonus
on white cane than during the full blast of the
kanaka curse. At Mackay the cookies who lived
in humpies a few years ago are now domiciled in
neat cottages, and begin to be blessed with
furniture, and the growers of nigger cane are
manifesting a desire to break their agreements
■with Tommy Tanna and cast Thomas into outer
blackness. It is quite a while now since anything
very large and loud was heard about “ the ruin of
the cane industry.”
“Eros ” : A great deal has been said about
the decline of George Reid, who proposes to de-
bate Socialism with W. Holman in order to keep
himself before the public eye. But the man who
has really declined is Bruce Smith. He has got
into that condition that he would travel 30 miles
to address an infant school on the advantages of
nigger labor. Ho is constantly hastening long
distances to hold forth to women at bun-struggles
(or struggles where there is no bun) on the horrors
of White Australia. He would almost rather orate
to a caterpillar about the blessings of Chinamen
than not orate at all. I believe if B. Smith were
coming down the fire escape from a burning house,
with nothmg on but his night-shirt and his curl-
papers (if he wears curl-papers), he would stop
half-way down to address the excited crowd in the
anti-Socialist interest.
