One State Ministry had a great cleaning-
up in a department the other day, and sailed over
the heads of numerous droning seniors in order to
find a new chief. Whereupon three of the seniors
woke up sufficiently to write a joint letter, point-
ing out their long experience, and protesting that
they ought to be consulted before definite action
was taken. To which, so runs the yarn around
the corridors of that department, the Government
replied in two brief sentences :
Yours of received. When a man is draining a
ditch he doesn’t consult the frogs.
Yic. Railway Commissioner Tait to the
Shipping Services Commission :
Having been 20 years with a privately-owned company,
and three years with State-owned railways, my ex-
perience is that the public receive fully as great accom-
modation and services as they would from private com-
panies under similar circumstances, if not greater. There
is no need of competition to keep the railway manage-
ment and staff up to the mark, for the critic is always
with us, day and night, in all parts of the State.
Which is a tough bit for those to chew who
always say—“ But, cf course, Private Enterprise
could do better.”
THE VISIT OF THE DEPOSITORS.
Dr. George Brown , veteran missionary, to
N.S.W. Methodist Conference on the progress of
Christianity in the South Sea Islands : —
He asked his audience what they thought of a Fijian
taking out an insurance policy on the lives of his children
for £1000? Gould they imagine, also, an ex-cannibal
having a bank account?
Sydney Morning Herald, alarmed at the
near prospect of a series of strikes coming to
blight the returning prosperity of the State,
practically urges (13/3/’O6) an amendment of the
N.S.W. Arbitration Act to make it more effec-
tive :
The Government is, no doubt, to blame for playing with
the question of amending the Arbitration Act. It would
and it would not —talking boldly enough, and then acting
like Mark Twain’s pilgrim when the Bedouins were
announced —and disgust has been added to disgust. But
no good purpose can be served by rushing to strike
weapons at a moment’s notice.
The evidence of Queensland Trade Com-
missioner J*>nes before the Tariff Commission was
a hard blow to the Foreign-trade advocates.
Fortified with Japanese official blue-books, Jones
gave exact rates of wages and hours of labor
(which had been much in dispute), also conditions
of life ; incidentally he proved that the present
industrial position of Japan was largely due to
heavy Protective duties. Senator Pulsford was
aghast, and wrote to Brisbane Courier com-
plaining that Jones’ appearance was “ very inex-
plicable,” “ deeply regrettable,” “destructive of
the best instincts of civilisation,” with other wild,
raving, hair-clawing remarks. Pulsford’s love of
the foreigner who smothers Australian industries
amounts almost to a mania.
The “ Hello Central ” bureau still refuses
to budge from its intention of installing the toll
system of telephones, despite the frenzied opposi-
tion of the Sydney and Melbourne business people
and some of the angry suburban population that
wants to ring up its best friend and talk for half-
an-hour about its new hat. It is wildly declared
that “had private enterprise been running the
system, the change would not have been made ”
(which is true, for the generous ad lib. system
would never have been started). The proposed
charge of £9 a year represents a daily rate of 6d.
If a business subscriber demanded the right of
posting as many letters a day as he liked, because
he had paid for six, he would be laughed at. Yet
that has been the position with the telephone
service.
The late William M‘Quade, of Bomera,
Potts Point, Sydney, vested his property in the
Permanent Trustee Company of N.S.W. on
behalf of his heirs. Four years ago the N.S.W.
Government wished to resume part of the pro-
perty for wharf extension ; but the resumption
was objected to unless the whole property was
taken, and the Equity Judge, in September, 1902,
made a decree to that effect—that the State must
resume the entire property or none of it. The
Ministry of the day appealed to the Full Court
two months later, and the Full Court confirmed
the decree of the Judge in Equity. The Govern-
ment decided to appeal (at great expense) to the
Privy Council. Last week the Privy Council
decided the appeal against the Government, so,
after four years of expensive litigation, the per-
sistent “Crown” finds itself exactly where it
was. If the N.S.W. Parliament ever opens again
—and as the much-needed unification of Australia
seems a little distance off yet, that public misfor-
tune presumably will re-open—somebody ought to
ask just how much of the people’s money has been
spent in this one little scrimmage.
Sydney Telegraph writes glibly enough
of “ recovering lost public property” when it is a
question of getting back the harbor foreshores for
the pleasure of the city. But it shrieks “ confisca-
tion ” when there is talk of getting back from
monopoly ownership the big agricultural areas
necessary for the very life of the State.
Australian naval expert Cresswell, in his
latest report on Australian maritime
defence, suggests a mosquito fleet
of torpedoers and destroyers
to defend the ports. As an
instillment, that would be
well enough. But for any
sound system there must be
a number of swift cruisers,
and there ought to be
two or three of the
best battleships that
money can supply. The
mosquito boats would
be tied to their harbors
by, say 100-mile long
ropes. They couldn’t
circumnavi-
gate the
continent,
nor even
safely
run from
port to
port in
all weath-
ers. There-
fore, they
could not
be mobilised at a
crisis. The Aus-
tralian Navy should
have at least half-
a-dozen vessels that
can be anywhere
at any time, and
which can, by
giving them es-
cort, extend the
effective range
of the mosquito
fleet.
The new Canadian
national paper, Canada, is
beginning to agitate for the total
exclusion of the alien immigrant. One writer
records :
In the course of a summer’s tour I found that the
tiny historic towns of the eastern provinces, which I
remembered as “ calm and clean with the calmness and
cleanness of England,” have been invaded by a pestilence
of squalor and meanness and vulgarity as a result of the
appearance in force of the low-grade foreign immigrant.
. . . “ The commonest London loafer has more of decency
in him, of suavity, of inherent reverence for order, of
love of fair play, of humor, than any of the alien immi-
grants who are'now flocking into the Dominion.”

S.A. Education Department has a curious
quarrel with that of Tasmania. The Director of
Education in the island was till lately an inspector
in Adelaide, and he is now writing to the best
juniors he left behind him, offering them increased
salaries if they will follow. So says Premier
Price, who considers it “ very unfair.” Six have
gone already, and the Tasmanian teachers, who
don’t get billets accordingly, are not likely to
approve of the plan either. S.A.’s particular
grievance is that several of the departed teachers
have received a high-class education at Adelaide
’Varsity, the fees being made nominal with the idea
that they were going to devote themselves to the
service of the State. It seems a queer grievance,
however. Fancy Bent (for example) moaning
publicly because Melbourne men were getting all
the fattest billets in some N.S.W. department!
Sydney Telegraph is engaged in the
manufacture of another Ma State grievance. It
notices that N.S.W. has a land-tax. It doesn t
seem to know that S.A. has a heavier land-tax,
and that Victoria and Tasmania also have land-
taxes, both of the State and Local Government
variety ; and it argues that if J. C. Watson’s pro-
posed Federal Land-Tax comes into operation a
terrible injury will be done to the Ma State :
In this way the Federal tax would fall with such special
severity on New South Wales as to amount in a substan-
tial degree to discrimination between States.
Even if the statement were true, instead of
being the paper’s usual parochial lie, there is
a simple remedy. When the Federal land-tax
comes in force, the State one can be abolished. If
there is any difficulty about that, Carruthers can
be abolished. Also it wouldn’t do much harm if
the Telegraph were abolished.
“Robert Hap ” writes :
On a recent Monday a withering branch of the ring-
barked Liberal League hired an excursion boat and nearly
filled it with George Reid and other people who are sup-
posed to ’ave the cause at ’eart. They had a tame little
outing down Sj’dney harbor. There was nobody of much
importance present, or the daily press would have men-
tioned it. Also there was a lamentable scarcity of
refreshments, and as the day waned many of the merry-
makers roamed about the boat jabbing their gamps at
tool boxes, patent life buoys, and at any enclosed space
that looked like a larder. Just as the murmurings
were beginning to make the organisers look haggard,
George Reid got up and delivered a speech off Clark
Island. When he said that he believed “in the great
Socialism whose law was, sacrifice yourself for others,”
every eye was turned on the brief bag at his side; but
when he pointed out that the other Socialism meant sac-
rificing others for yourself, there was a general exodus in
the direction of the suspended water-bag. Altogether,
it was a dreary turn out, and if George w'ants to keep
before the public whatever political name he has left,
he will have to devise a better scheme than delivering an
address to Clark Island from a moored pleasure boat. Let
him try a few remarks from the Post Office tower, or a
stirring address from the top of a tram car.

kidney diseases.*
THE MAN WITH THE PUNCH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Williams has the skill. Squires has the punch.”—
Notice posted before the fight.
To the cheerful Oyclorama, up the crowded Eastern Hill,
All the population hurried to the Squires and Williams
mill.
There was biff, bash—holy thunder, the catastrophe was
o’er,
And Ed. Williams lay all huddled in a heap upon the floor.
So, ’tis not the clever “stopping” that removes the hair
and skin ;
Nay, ’tis not the fancy dancing, but the push-power that
can win.
When Bill paused and Edward rested at that half-round’s
sudden crunch,
Sober Science tossed the towel up to the man who owned
the Punch.
Now, Australia needs some strong man more pugnacious
than polite,
Some Gehenna of a cyclone with a death-dispensing right.
It requires no verbal buckets to be sprinkled on its hopes,
But rude blokes who’ll bash importers into wreck across
the ropes.
Neither clowns, nor dancing Johnnies, learned pens nor
canting choirs,
Only pugs who’ll deal out Shcol and disaster like Bill
Sauires.
For of coves who’re merely clever we’ve an overloaded
bunch,
Yes, there’s space for politicians with a paralysing
punch.
VIC.
LOUIS ESSON.

Caklyle.
Australia imports enormous quantities of
dairy machinery which could easily be manufac-
tured locally. If the duty was robust enough, the
manufacturers would come here with their patents,
since Australia is one of the largest users of their
goods.
A remarkable and most amusing poster, adver-
tising that well-known and popular hair tonic “ Capilla,
is at the present time appearing on the hoardings of
Sydney. The arguments and controversies heard on the
trams in reference to the possibilities of “ Capilla hair
growing properties must prove a very profitable advertis-
ment for the preparation which is now in its sixth year,
wjth ever increasing sales.*
Conventional Lies.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Twas at dusk that a figure sped past me,
All wretched, unhappy, and wan;
One look of mute suffering he cast me,
Then fled down the street and was gone !
’Twas Humanity, stripped of pretences,
All naked beneath the cold skies;
Aye, reduced to his primary senses,
He scurried with woe in his eyes,
And demanded, with anguish immense, his
Conventional Lies !
“I am naked !” the figure howled, faintly;
“ Pray, fetch me my ancient beliefs.
Deck me out like a hypocrite saintly
With baggy-kneed morals and griefs.
Let me walk in the paths I am used to,
Dress me in my customed disguise;
An you heed not my prayer, or refuse to,
Poor Humanity sickens and dies !
In my weakness and woe I’m reduced to
Conventional Lies !
“ Through the ages I’ve covered my carcase
With paltry pretences and shams ;
Now I howl in despair through the dark, as
The door of Pretended Things slams !
I’m forlorn; and I stumble and falter,
For I hear me no priestly replies;
No clerical finger-posts palter,
No sycophant’s sermon denies.
Let me shelter myself at thy altar—
Conventional Lies !
“I was nurtured on wiles and evasions,
And I yearn for my natural food ;
I was guarded on mental occasions,
So my brain was a dank solitude.
Now sceptic suspicions assail me—
I grope through a vast maze of whys ;
Dread doubts and perplexities flail me,
And none heed my moans and my sighs.
Will the Ark of my Covenant fail me—
Conventional Lies ? ”
Lo, the howls of the wretch checked my laughter,
There was death and despair in his voice ;
So I sped on my motor-star after,
And I bade the poor insect rejoice !
With a pitchfork I reached him his raiment,
Which he donned in the eagerest wise ;
Aye, he clutched his drab creed as if payment
Could never obtain that pale prize.
And I knew in that hour what thy sway meant,
Conventional Lies !
’Twas at night that a figure sped past me,
Complacency’s tallow-faced son ;
One look of thanksgiving he cast me,
Then fled up the street and was gone !
’Twas Humanity, clad in pretences,
Quite at home now beneath the cold skies;
He had strangled his primary senses,
And scurried, with joy in his eyes,
Clutching tightly, with gladness immense, his
Conventional Lies !
VIC.

BOWLING- ST., E3DFERN, SYDNEY.
The postponed Skutliorp-Martim contest took
place in Martini’s ground last Saturday night, and the
grounds were not large enough to hold the people
who surged up, so hundreds were turned away.
The terms of the contest were that Willie Waite should
ride three of Skuthorp’s horses, including the famous
Yellow Bay. Skuthorp was to ride three of Martini’s
horses, including, of course, the terrible Bobs. Waite,
looking about list., strutted round the ring in a white
riding suit and green sash. Skuthorp was a sketch.
He wore tight riding pants with the ends inside his
socks, a sac coat and vest, and on his head, above
all things, a motoring cap. Mr. Tom Watson
having declined to act as judge, three others were
selected on the spot, and they tossed to see who
should ride first. Skuthorp won, but when he produced
his saddle, Martini objected to it because its knee-pads
were backed with strips of steel. The Professor put on his
coat and vest and declared that he would ride in that saddle
or none. The crowd took Skuthorp’s side, and threatened
sudden death. A long wrangle took place. Sku-
thorp endeavored to tell his family history, holding
one hand behind his ear. Martini tried to argue
with the crowd, but was called objectionable names.
He retired with his men to a corner where they could
fight with their backs to the wall if it was necessary,
Then Skuthorp’s manager dispatched a cab for another
saddle, and when it arrived Martini announced that
Waite would ride Skuthorp’s horses right off. Yellow
Bay is a beautiful animal. But he appeared cowed by
the crowd and the artificial light, and worn out
by a 35min. struggle to get a bridle on him and
another 20min. fight about saddling’. There was not
much bucking left in him when Waite mounted him.
Then Dargin’s Urey was brought in, and as there was
apparently only one bridle in Martini’s show, there was
another half-hour’s delay getting it and the saddle off Yel-
low Bay. The saddle was finally removed by cutting the
crupper. Waite was as big as the grey horse, and
after one round rode the poor little animal full
tilt into the fence, cutting its head and dazing it. It was
a shocking piece of horsemanship Skuthorp’s third horse
was a failure from a bucking point of view. Then the
shout was “ Bring in Bobs.”
All this time Skuthorp had been taking off and
putting on his coat, as he alternately made up his mind
to ride and not to ride. He was apparently in a rage,
and the crowd said things. Some of these things roused
the Professor. He fastened on his spurs, and strode over
to where Bobs was being held by the ear till bridled and
saddled. Hardly was the Professor in the saddle before
they let go. Bobs went off bang. He swung round, re-
volved on his own axis, propped suddenly, reversed,
sprang into the air, stood on his hind legs, and raged
generally. Then he stood up on his front feet and
tried to kick the stars, and bounded in a series
of savage pig-jumps from one side of the ring
to the other. Then he got his head down
between his knees and gave an elegant exhibition
of all-wool buckjumping. The professor used the spurs
on him, and the animal settled down to buck all creation.
Up above, the man in the motor-cap never moved, just
sat as though he grew out of Bobs’ backbone ; and at last
the exhausted horse slowed down, and hung his head in
token of defeat. Then Skuthorp dismounted. How
fierce a struggle it had been was evidenced by the fact
that he was hopelessly sea-sick, right there in the ring.
There are few pluckier riders anywhere
than William Waite, but for graceful, elegant riding he is
not in the same street as the older man. There had been
a long’, undignified, hideous wrangle ; and the show that
should have started a little after eight did not commence
till after ten. Many people lost the star part of the
exhibition on Saturday night because the last boats and
trams left while Bobs was still unridden.
* * * *
At Richmond (Vic.) unregistered meeting the
4.year-old 12-3 pony Little Jack won easily the 3j furlong
handicap, carrying 12st., or nearly 4st. more than any
other horse in the race. The little wonder went out at
even money in a field of seven, and humped his burden
over the distance in 48J seconds.
■* * * •
If the V.R.C. stipendiary stewards are not too
proud to learn from Jack Wren, they might do worse
than follow that gentleman’s officials’example in “deal-
ing with ” blackmailing owners. At Richmond (Vic.)
pony races last Thursday a steward, strolling casually
round, overheard a pony-owner say to a fairly-heavy
punter, “ A fiver's no good to me ; you’ve got to make it
a tenner.” The pair were promptly invited to the
stewards’ room, and a searching cross-examination re-
sulted in the owner being warned off for life ! It was all
over in 10 minutes, and was sudden enough to be stagger-
ing.
* • * *
When N.S.W. Pioneer Motor Cycle Club affili-
ated with the N.S.W. League of Wheelmen, it was under-
stood that the League would do its best to see that the
club got a fair showing at whatever carnivals were run
under League auspices. Admirers of the whizzers
were pleased, and the first night the Summer Nights
Syndicate put on a motor-bike race at Sydney Cricket
Ground there was tremendous enthusiasm. Then the
motor-cyclists were apparently forgotten, and they got
angry and talked secession. When the League heard
this it rushed in and arranged a race for last Monday
night, but by the time the handicaps were published the
Pioneer Club had seceded. So far the League, like
the Law, had proved itself a hass, but confronted
with this situation it showed amazing energy. Right on the
ground, out under the stars, it formed a new motor cycle
club, assumed sole control of motor-biking in the State,
and managed to seduce some of the best racers from the
parent body, and brought off its contest. Said that it will
now proceed to declare the older club “ illegal.” This
prompt action may command admiration, but it would
have been more to the League’s credit if the position had
never arisen, and the old club deserves sympathy. If the
league triumphs it will be the victory of a bully who wins
by brute strength, and without a shadow of right.

How is this for form ? In the third heat of the
semi-final of Sydney Thousand on March 10, A. J. Clark
had 25yds. from Rutt. At the bell Rutt was well in front,
having caught and passed Clark and the field. At the
shilling-stand Rutt sprinted away and led by yards to the
first members’ stand. At the ladies’ reserve Clark had
closed on the German after being passed badly, and on
the line he beat Rutt by inches. Time, lmin. 59sec. In
the special semi-final the following Wednesday Rutt won
easily in lmin. 55 l-ssec. In the Kent Plate final, Mon-
day night, a scratch race, Clark was leading Rutt at the
ladies’ reserve and racing on the outside of Pye, when
Mayer bumped them and upset the applecart somewhat.
Rutt finished second and Clark third, owing to the bump
(Maver was disqualified) ; time, 2min. 15sec.
* * * *
A letter from Africa :
The League of Sahara Wheelmen recently promoted a
cycle meeting at Timbuctoo, but it looks as though the
predatory instincts of the natives will kill the sport. The
night the mail leaves here the big’ race will be held, and
there is considerable excitement over some remarkable
developments. As in most other places, except Aber-
deen, the Arabian bookmakers laid odds in a disjointed,
haphazard manner, and it was left to an amateur sport to
come along and practically get the whole field in his bag.
He said he had his own fancies, and would lay the odds
against any combination that did not coincide with his
own. He invited the Timbuctootans to back men not
only to win, but to get a place, or to finish in any posi-
tion up to 11th, and the Timbuctootans responded gaily
till it was whispered that he owned the field and it
would finish in whatever order suited him best. It
was also discovered that the state of the market
suggested that a middle distance man was the one
he had decided to make winner, and then operations
slumped somewhat. About a week before the race, how-
ever, Timbuctoo was thrown into a state of wild excite-
ment by the news that two of the riders had got out of
the bag, and one had backed himself to win, staking EOO
shekels of his own good coin in a3to 1 wager. The other
rider who escaped from the bag with him also backed
him to the extent of 200 shekels. But as the amateur
speculator is still laying the odds cheerfully against them
it looks as though he had something up his sleeve. Every-
body in the desert expects that there will be a great
smash on the night of the race, for if the man who pro-
poses to win when he isn’t wanted to is really going to try
and break up the ring, there is sure to be a tremendous
tussle. General opinion here among camel-drivers and
others is that the swift man who says he is going to win
when he isn’t designed to do so will go down somehow.
* * » *
After all, there is something very appropriate
about the present degradation of the bike. The pedal
mode of propulsion was the invention of a French
mechanic who had given up toil for burglary, and used
the machine in connection with the plundering of coun-
try mansions. It is still a predatory machine, now much
used by the Jews who have given up hod-carrying to
burgle the pockets of the Gentiles who follow the sport
of bike racing.

* * * *
The “Polite Hundred” of Melbourne have been
simmering in a state of suppressed excitement lately over
some blushful escapades in motor-cars. In spite of the
difficulty oi identifying automobiles, a number of the
ungodly” manage to make out to whom the cars belonged,
and the news that one, the property of an irreproachable
citizen, had been seen at midnight whisking along the
road with a carload of shrieking, high-kicking girls of joy
shook society to its foundations. However, it has now
leaked out that the expert drivers have been making a
practice of rushing round on their own in the pale moon-
light. Madame Toorak would find when she required her
car for a certain evening that the carburetter was work-
ing spasmodically, or the high tension was twisted, or
something of that sort, and, after working all the after-
noon on the trouble, the car at the time of requirement
was still obstinate. When Madame had departed per cab
the driver would get the thing going spasmodically,
and take the car out for a good run to put it in working
order again. Either Melbourne society is afflicted with
shocking judgment in the choice of its drivers, or it has a
pretty faculty for wriggling out of a tight place.
* * * * *
W. E. Samuelson, the Yankee bilcist who came
to Sydney with quite a host of performances, left for
Uncle Sam’s land this week. Excepting in one event in
January, Samuelson wasn’t a howling success ; still his
winnings for the season totalled £52 for three firsts, five
seconds, and two thirds, which, together with £6 Es.
appearance fees per programme, more than paid expenses.
The fact that nothing has been heard from
Daytona Beach of Mr. Vanderbilt’s 250 h.p. car, which
was to do the mile at the rate of 150 miles to the hour,
indicates that Barney Oldfield’s prediction that the freak
would turn out a failure has been fulfilled. Oldfield also
prophesied that whoever drove the contraption would be
blown so high that he would never come back to the
United States ; but that part of the prophecy was appar-
entlv not inspired.
* * * »
South Australian League of Wheelmen will
expire in June next. The recent carnival was a financial
failure, and left the League in debt. To clear off the
liability the League will sell its club-house and all its
other assets, and then dissolve, after a 10-years’struggle
to make the game pay.
* • * *
The god of winter is beginning to show his
bright-colored jersey and knickerbockers in Sydney parks
on Saturday afternoons, and to practise kicking. The
Rugby Metropolitan Union does not start the season with
alluring prospects. Although it opened the year with a
credit of £3OO, and raked in another £1619 at the gates,
it contrived to spend the lot and finished up with an
overdraft of £39. On the other hand, the N.S.W. Union
owes it a sum of £372 95., and, if it can rake that in, it
will come out about square. In the meantime, the St.
George club, that wanted to be admitted to first grade, is
very wrath over the recommendation of the committee
that its application be refused. It has got the active sup-
port of Newtown club, and will put up a stiff fight for the
rejection of the committee’s recommendation at the next
meeting of the Union. In the event of the first grade
door being slammed in its face, the club, it has announced,
will hold a special meeting to decide whether it will play
second grade or borough Rugby, or cross over bodily to
the Australian game.
The British Association game is going to loom
very large this winter. Four clubs from the North Coast,
and the same number from the Southern littoral will
compete with the eight metropolitan teams for the
Gardiner Cup and premiership; so the Soccers will have
no less than 16 first-grade clubs in full blast this season.
For some years —since the start of district football, in
fact—the country clubs have been excluded from the
Gardiner Cup contest, but the ruling body has decided
that such exclusion is not in the best interests of
game. Last season the best football seen in Sydney, p-as
that played bv the Soccers.
* * * *
Two of the “ All Blacks” have arrived in Sydney-
on their way back to the awful amount'of public food that
awaits them where Seddon rules.” Ever since the tour
ended the footballers have b£eli dreading this wel-
come home, for they take football very seriously in Maori-
land, and the whole country has been known to go stark,
staring mad over one victory. What will happen to a
team returning with a record of 35 out of 36 games, and
1025 points against 72, is almost too terrible to think of.
And yet the Sydney footballers rushed those two unfor-
tunate Maoriianders and “entertained” them at the
Arcadia! Some people have no consideration. 11 ■'
* * * -»
Admirers of the All Blacks are enthusiastic over
the defeat of Wales by Ireland. The rushing Irishmen
scored a goal and two tries, while the prancing little
Taffies only secured a couple of tries. When the Maori-
ianders were in America they played a team from British
Columbia at San Francisco for the edification of the
Yankees. The U.S.A. sports all admitted that Rugby
was a pretty game, but a number put it down as too mild
and only for freshmen, cripples and invalids. It seems
that what the U.S. footballer loves is “ line-bucking,”
which is a sort of wild, mad scramble, in which anything
is legal, and after which the whistle is blown till the dead
and injured are removed. Still, it is said that the spec-
tators were worked up to ii much wilder pitch of en-
thusiasm by the passing charges of the Rugbyites than
has been known in the memory of even the oldest inhabi-
tant 1 at the Yankee game. Certain it is that there is a big
reaction setting in against the brutal inter-collegiate
game in the States, and as it undoubtedly has to give way
to something, that something will, in all probability, be
Rugby, or a modified form of it.
* * * *
Saturday saw a fresh round started in Sydney
first-grade cricket. It was a perfect autumn afternoon,
with hardly any wind, but the crack Waverley team came
within an ace of completely collapsing before Glebe. The
first wicket fell at 10, the second saw 17 added, the thiid
went when the score was at 49, and two more followed
without a single run being added to the score. Even?
tually the side was dismissed for 205, and that was a mar-
vellous recovery on the opening of the game. Carter
batted excellently, but was caught at 91. Two of Glebe’s
wickets were down for 39 when stumps were drawn.
Bowden was Glebe’s particularly deadly trundler, taking-
three for 27 runs. But on the afternoon’s play Newell, c.f
Waverley, had the best figures ; three overs, one maiden,
two runs, two wickets. Out at Burwood “Sunny Jim”
Mackay made 102 for the local team against North
Sydney, smacking 14 fours and two sixes. Balmain
astonished the Camperdown natives considerably and
itself more by scoring 360 for the loss of eight wickets
against University. It was an amazing innings—for Bal-
main. Whitting, one of the not-out men, carried his
willow for 117, and E. Jansen garnered 105 runs before
Stack caught him off Harvey. And yet there were no
reports of fires from Wilks* suburb on Saturday
night.
* * * »
Matters concerning the Board of Control for
Australian cricket are at a standstill. The Melbourne
Club has refused to nominate its representative to the
Board until S. Australia’s position is clearly defined.
The Board, on the other hand, has given the Melbourne
Cricket Club a month in which to make up such mind as
it possesses; after that date, presumably, the seat
reserved for the representative of the M.C.C. will be
otherwise disposed of. As a matter of fact, this repre-
sentation of the M.C.C. on the Board at all is a huge
blunder, and the Board is treating the club with absurd
leniency. The S. Australian Ass. has moderated its
demands, but to accede to them as they are defined at
present would be to ensure a huge squabble every
time a team was picked to go to England.
The S. Australian Association and the M.C.C. stand for
the perpetuation of the bad old system under which a
trip to England was a mere boodle hunt on the part of
the “active players” and there was more than a sus-
picion that Test matches were thrown away in the in-
terests of gate. And the Board of Control, being formed
to bring about a better state of affairs, should meet these
boodlers’ representatives with an axe and not an olive
branch. The position taken up by the S. Australian Ass.
and M.C.C. is only maintained by bluff, and sooner or
later must fall to pieces because of its innate rottenness.
There’s little danger of any team coming from England
under the auspices of the M.C.C., and it doesn’t matter if
one does. That threat of disqualification for life W'ill
make its tour a hideous failure and smite the M.C.C. hard
on the pocket.

The financial success of the Australian Eleven
inspires sonne of the members to exploit the idea in a new
direction. Intention is to select a team for a tour of
Queensland. Moving spirit, selector, and prospective
captain is Victor Trumper. The programme includes
visits to Charters Towers, Townsville, Mount Morgan,
Rockhampton, Bundaberg, Maryborough, Gympie, Bris-
bane, and Toowoomba. Tour will cover six W'eeks, and
exes, are reckoned at £lOOO.
* * * *
N.S.W. Cricket Association is changing its
constitution. Hitherto each component first grade club
has sent delegates to the council, which has elected a
small executive. The council was practically in the hands
of the executive, for although it met monthly to review
the executive decisions, it was found that the inner body
was too strong to be properly controlled. The new con-
stitution provides that each club shall send two delegates,
the senior of whom will represent his club on the execu-
tive. The council is to meet only four times a year. So
though the new proposal makes the executive absolutely
supreme, it democratises it, making it as representative
as the council. In fact, under the new proposal, there
seems but little need for the council at all.

* Hr *
S.A. Champion Eights, rowed on the Port
River in a gale of wind, saw three of the four boats
engaged fill with water. The Adelaide crew escaped and,
of course, won easily. The disaster to the Port men hap-
pened early and in shallow water, so they emptied out
their boat and went on again. As they were pegging
along, a bad third, they saw the University men turn and
row for the bank to avoid swamping ; so they made a
dash. The students got rid of the ballast just as the
Ports came level, and won an exciting struggle home for
second place. It is rarely that a crew has gone for a walk
on the bank during a race and then finished second.
* * * *
Of all the freaks of wanton wealth, that pro-
posal to hold an international University eight-oar race
on the Zambesi River, East Africa, somewhere above the’
Victoria Falls, is the wildest. The £lOOO prize of the
winners might certainly pay their expenses getting there,
but it would be rough on the losers, and among the slave-
stealers and coffee-planters of Nyassaland the “gate”
would only just about pay for native or banana beer.
There are no special points about the Zambesi as a rowing
water. Above the falls it is wide, and flows smoothly
with a gentle current, but there are hundreds of sheets
of better rowing water.

r * * * *
According to Sydney daily press, the platform
that the divers sprang from at East Sydney “ Olympic
water contests” was 70 feet high. The display is said to
have been the most attractive given this year. Once
more, this high-diving craze is dangerous, and should be
prohibited. It is only a question of time till some one
makes a mistake—and then there will be a shocking
exhibition. It might also be worth the while of those
who promote these shows to find how they stand legally
if anyone gets killed in one of their ridiculous diving
exhibitions.

lian swimmers to England after the Olympian Games are
concluded, is now known as the Healy-in-Grease Fund.

Just at present foot-racing is booming in Wes-
tralia, but the third “ Kalgoorlie Hnndred ” saw a regu-
lar debacle of the favorites, and, as the betting was the
heaviest on record, the skinner means that several book-
makers have made quite a haul. This sort of thing is a
mistake—the bookmakers ought to arrange for a few
favorites to win occasionally. They get it all back in the
end, as the fools who win aiways come again, and bring
their friends with them.

Champion professional ped. A. B. Postle brushed
aside any doubts as to his brilliancy at Kalgoorlie, t’other
day, in winning the 300yds. Australian championship. At
the start, Postle stumbled and almost fell, but, picking
himself together, won easily in 32£sec. Postle has
scooped in 70 races and over £3OO prize-money in six
months.
* * * *
Dr. Sharp, the ’Varsity tennis player, is the
proud posseesor of both the challenge cups presented for
competition by the N.S.W. Association. The Singles
trophy was inaugurated in 1893, while those for the
Doubles started in 1885. Conditions were that both were
to be w'on outright three times running - before being
finally the property of the winner. Sharp, singly, and
paired with Wright, won both competitions in 1903, ’O4
and ’O6. (There was no contest in ’05.) Sharp is sup-
posed not to be in Brooke’s “class,” and as is often the
case, is held up to disparagement in comparing him with
Ben Green (who won the doubles four times), Webb, and
other heroes on the shelf. Still the fact remains that
Sharp decisively beat all his opponents now’ in flannels,
and w’hat he w'ould have done had the has-beens been his
contemporaries is an idle thought.

A wrestling contest, in the catch-bold style,
between tw’o heavy-weight amateurs, Bob Cooper
(12st. Ill) and H. P. Phillips (13st. 4), took place at
Clarence Weber’s rooms (Flinders-street, Melbourne), on
Friday evening before a select number of enthusiasts of
the ’rastle. This struggle proved the most interesting
event of its kind seen for some time. The men got
straight to work. The first fall was scored by Phillips,
with a bar nelsoq and arm hold, applied from the front,
after 56 minutes’ strenuous struggle, with two short
spells. Resuming, the brawrny ones occupied the mat,
except for a single spell, exactly 38 minutes. Phillips
eventually securing his former hold levered “ Bob ” over
and won the match with two out of three falls. The
work displayed may be described as Graeo-Roman catch
hold, on account of the absence of leg work, w'hich is con-
spicuous for its absence in all local struggles. The
Y.A.A.A. has decided to initiate W'restling competitions
during the coming winter, and, provided amateur contests
of like calibre are presented, the pros, are in for a rocky
time.

Peall, ex-champion of billiards, who retired
when the spot stroke on which he chiefly relied was made
unlawful, played an exhibition match in London last
month, giving Cook, a promising junior, 1000 in 2000, and
beating him. The veteran made, in succession, 103 of the
Strqkes that are now not recogriised as “ match ” billiards
at all. It was John Roberts whose great influence got the
spot-stroke ruled out, and so ruined his chief rival, and
London Punch said one of its few bright things when it
suggested as a motto for Roberts, the passage from Mac-
beth, “ Out, d—d spot!"
• * * *
N.S.W. National Rifle Association proposes to
clip the wings of the rifle shots who wander about the
State in search of the big prizes, by grouping the State
into districts, allowing only residents in each district to
compete in the sectional rifle competitions. The big
Association meeting is an open affair, but even there the
secondary shots are to be as well looked after as the
cracks. The policy of giving encouragement to the poorer
shots is certain to help the defence scheme on which the
subsidising of rifle shooting depends.
* * * *
Another keen disappointment to Melbourne
Eatrons of boxing was Mallv Jackson, of Tassy, v. Bill
ang, the shapely Richmond footballer. There was a big
house, in spite of the recent Williams v. Squires fiasco, and
the mighty Mally got going like an energetic windmill,
evidently thinking to finish Lang as he finished Fennessey.
But Lang knew enough to evade his unscientific elbow-
out deliveries, and got under and around them five tries
out of six, leading himself with a straight left that
reached the target every time. Jackson was not nearly
such an energetic bullocker in the third round, but he
put an Ugly screw on Lang, and pitched him clean out of
the ring amidst howls of execration. The referee
couldn’t possibly do anything else, and Lang was given
first cut of the gate. This display of mimic warfare ex-
cited the local Women’s Christian Temperance Union past
bearing, and its meetings have been expressing “ horror
and indignation ” over the “unrighteous and disgusting
show.” This writer has seen a “berruther" of the Wesleyan
persuasion do himself more hurt rastling in prayer than
happened to either Lang or Jackson on the occasion in
question.

After Bill Squires knocked out Ed. Williams in
lmin. 25sec. (21sec. of which Williams spent on the floor),
a pressman followed the Newcastle earthquake to his
dressing-room. After much battling he was admitted,
and set to work to interview “Bill.” “Yes, it was a
quick sort of business,” Squires agreed, and waited for
the next question. “ How did you knock him out ? ” the
pressmah asked. Squires looked at him in a puzzled
fashion, then drawled, “I hit him on the jore with mb
’and.” The pressman’s questions stopped, and he retired
to “ fdke” his interview, while Bill proceeded to lace up
his boots.
* * i »
When Melbourne middle-weight Robert Fraser
Stepped into the ring at Sydney National Sporting Club
last week he found soldier John Thompson in good fettle.
Fraser was fairly hard-looking himself, but immediately
the scrap started it was seen that the Sydneyite had ail
the skill. He planted a shower of clean' quick blows on
mouth and ribs, and blocked all reprisals in a manner
that roused much enthusiasm among his backers. It was
not till well into the second that Fraser smote Thompson,
and then it was over the eye, and while Thompson tried
to make out the shape of the constellation, he caught
another across the forehead. The soldier looked out
through the fireworks and swung a heavy right across
Fraser’s kidney region, which made the unfortunate
Melbournian writhe with pain. But he managed to keep
on his feet till the bell saved him. Thompson’s obvious
superiority made him careless, and getting away slowly
he was smitten across the jaw, ripped in the vvind, and
forced to take seven seconds on the floor. This was
unexpected, and revealed the Melbournian as a
hard hitter. Fraser caught one where the chicken
caught the axe, and for some time afterwards seemed to
have trouble straightening his neck. Then Thompson
fell upon him, roughed him to the boards, punched him
when he clinched, and knocked him down when he tried
to get away. When he got up, Thompson landed him on
the jaw and sent him down again, and when he rose once
more he was belted across the ring till the bell rescued
him. It was a bad time, but he stood it, and four more
rounds of heavy battering found him still fightingabrave
but apparently hopeless battle. In the 11th round
Thompson seemed tired ; the battered object before him
came at him, smote him three times running on the ribs,
and then hooked his jaw. They clinched and went down
together. Staggering up, Thompson hurled himself at
the exhausted Frazer, who responded with a wild left
jab. It took Thompson in the throat, and rolled him
over. It was the random shot which takes science sud-
denly when science isn’t looking, and Thompson was out
—struck down in the very moment of victory after
winning all the way on points.
* * * *
Citizen John Wren has arranged a bruising
match between Tim Murphy, who killed McColl partially
dead for the championship and then went all the way to
S’Africa to serve as a chopping-block for Mike Williams,
and William Squires, who recently caused deterioration
in the carcase of Ed. Williams. Wren puts up a purse of
£3OO, and the men have each agreed to find a Side stake
of £2OO. The disturbance will take place in Melbourne
the first week in April, and the duration thereof shall not
exceed 20 rounds. Melbournian bruise lovers have had a
series of keen disappointments lately, but unless Michael
of Jew’burg broke the spirit of Timothy, they ought to
see enough gore to make up for recent milk and water.
* * * *
The tide of success still flows with the Mel-
bourne bruiser brigade in Sydney. On top of the vic-
tories of Fraser at the National and Moodie at the Gaiety
Club came Ted Whiting’s defeat of the sturdy Sydney
fighter Joe Walcott. Walcott is, this paper believes, a
West Indian native, and a man of beautiful chest develop-
ment. He is a slow, steady, reliable fighter, who takes
his punishment standing up, and batters away grimly at
whathe considers the weak spot. Whiting rushed him,
got in close, and hustling and pushing turned to account
the fact that the white man is tougher in the fibre than
his colored brother. But it was not until nine rounds of
roughing had left their mark on Walcott that his second
dropped the moist, clammy towel of defeat into the ring.
The present N.S.W. Commission of Inquiry
into the relations of the N.S.W. Railway Com-
missioners will cost a heap of money. The Fehon
Inquiry of 1889 cost the public £4BB, and the
Schey-Eddy Inquiry of 1892 ran into £2454.
Then there were a couple of intermediate Tram-
way Inquiries which piled up a bill of over £lOOO.
The daughter (and only child) of Fred
Archer, England’s leading jockey in the eighties,
has j ust come of age, and the Court of Chancery
has handed over to her all her father’s estate.
Archer was understood never to bet, but he was a
rider of the “superior” order, and the owners
who made a pet of him gave him enormous
presents, which he did not throw away in
foolishness. He was also canny enough to marry
the daughter of Mat Dawson, the famous trainer.
Sheer carelessness on the part of the attendants
while he was delirious with fever (incurred in
“wasting” to ride) led to his taking his own life
nearly 20 years ago, but his estate has been better
nursed than he was, and Miss Nellie Rose Archer
has the handling of £50,000.
The N.S.W. Statistical Department has
ceased the publication of the “ Seven Colonies ”
(more recently known as “Australia and Maori-
land: A Statistical Record”), the most valuable
work of its kind turned out in this country. Of
late years the Australian Government has paid
most of the cost of the work, but the Carruthers
Ministry is aggrieved as usual; it objects to
Federal postage stamps not being printed at the
Sydney Government Printing Office, and it
objects equally to a statistical work which deals
with the whole Commonwealth being printed
there. Under the circumstances, the Australian
Government proposes to consider the question of
appointing a Federal Statistician, who will advise.
the Government about the re-issue of the work in
question. But surely neither matter wants a
minute’s consideration. A Federal statistician
and a complete Federal volume of statistics,
published annually, are absolutely essential, and
the fact that the Australian Government has
lingered so long over the matter is a disgrace.
INTIMIDATION AND SENTIMENT.
GENTLE TRAMP : “Ah, good lady, ’ow like you
are to my pore dead wife wot I murdered 14
years ago!"
Henry Somes, late South Kensington
student, has painted in oils an excellent portrait
of the late Victor Daley. It is pronounced by
friends of the poet to be a speaking likeness, and
is on view at George Robertson’s Book Store in
Castlereagh-street, Sydney.
Captain George DorwaTd, who recently
died in Melbourne, was a Very early Victorian
navigator. In the early 50’s he and Captain
Cadell pervaded the Murray River in the Lady
Darling, the first steam vessel to reach Echuca.
He owned the Naverino estate near Moama
(N.S.W.).
The point of view ! When Archbishop
Redwood, of Maoriland, lately denounced So-
cialism, S.M. Herald called him a man of singular
force of character. “He is dispassionate, and
strikes a clear note,” says the Herald. But
when, a few years ago, he said that Protestant
marriages were not, in the sight of heaven, mar-
riages at all, the Herald pointed out that he was
passionate, and struck a very muddy note.
Mother thinks I am rather
thin, and ought to go in more for athletics. So
one night after toil I climbed a flight of stairs
in a Bourko-street building, and got into a place
all full of dull thuds and hopping men in singlets.
I suppose I looked a bit lost because, as I was
gazing round, a man with a face that suggested
the necessity of local option came up and asked
me what I was after. I said that mother had
sent me to learn boxing, and immediately his tone
softened, and he became the most calm and
obliging man in the world. He robbed me of a
hard-earned guinea and said “seeyafter,” and
went to a corner, and started to knock about
with his gloves a poor, thin fellow, nearly as thin
as me. The rapidity with which my turn came
round was cruel; but when I set to work on my
instructor I got some lovely hits on to him. I
didn’t know the technical name of the hits, but
they were good ones, and I thought I was getting
on splendidly considering that it was my first
“go.” I went home proud, very proud, and when
I told mother she was proud too. The next night
I tramped up those stairs with a firm, confident
tread, and strode into the gymnasium with that
“ air of possession ” we read about in ladies’ books.
I put the gloves on and shaped up to my in-
structor, who, considering the hiding I had
given him the night before, smiled with
a friendly, fatherly smile upon me. Then
we started. Was it earthquakes? Had all
the motor cars in Victoria plunged into me?
Where was the thunderstorm ? Whence came
the multitudinous stars? Where was the floor?
I would have given two bananas (or more) for a
little bit of something that I could stand on.
How dark everything got, and what beautiful
colors shot through the darkness! —but all the
time there was a fearful rumbling and a sys-
tematic piston-rod pounded at my frail form till I
knew no more. You should have seen mother’s
face when she saw mine ! I won’t be able to go
out now for three or four Sundays, and Lord only
knows how I am going to ever eat anything again.
Even if I had a tooth left, my mouth won’t open
wide enough to let a peanut in. My instructor
can keep that guinea ; it’s worth ten to keep
away from him. They say that boxing instructors
always do this. It saves them giving the full
complement of lessons. I don’t care so much
about the guinea ; but my face—do you think my
face will ever come straight ?
A man who says he has done six months
in gaol writes, suggesting that The Bulletin
should start a Children’s Column, and sends along
a tale with which to open the proceedings. Tale
herewith :
Marjory Blessing-ton and her brother Tom were very
fond of each other. They were left orphans when quite
children, and were very poor. Happily, the good vicar
of the parish was very kind to them, and sent Tom out to
sea with his friend, the captin. Marjory he treated as
his own child, and because of her pritty blue eyes she was
called “ Blue Peeps,” and the clergyman taut her to read
and sent her to skool. She was so clever and lerned so
much that when she was older she became a skoolmis-
tress- herself. She- grew into quite a charming young
lady, and she was very kind to childeren and to all
animuls. So thought Sir Charles Jones, the squire, to
whom she was married. Just at the wedding her brother
Tom came home from abroard, where he had made a large
fortchun. Marjory, who was now Lady Jones, was
delited to see her dear brother. She and her husband
lived happily together for many years, and were much
beloved by all in the parish.
As for Tom, he became an Admirull.
The End.
That tale has all the ear-marks of the Children’s
Column, which is an undermining institution, and
calculated to soften the brain of the rising genera-
tion.
Curious admission by “ General ” Booth at
a big Mansion House (London) meeting : “ Some
times, when they are drunk, we are able to sober
them, and get them saved. In fact, not in-
frequently we have no chance with them unless
they are in a state of intoxication, for there are
some people who are never religious except when
they are intoxicated.”

The inwardness of the Reid desire to
debate Socialism now becomes apparent. Extract
from a recent interview :
The Cardinal says I am tilting at a windmill, and Mr.
L.yne says that it is a bogey lam fighting. Well, I
think it more significant than anything else he may say
that the Political Labor League of New South Wales has
put up, in the person of Mr. Holman, a champion to
defend the Socialism of the Labor party’s State and
Federal objectives.
In short, Reid desperately argues that the fact of
the debate shows that he is still to be taken
seriously.
While N.S.W. Divorce Justice Simpson
was away on holiday, Justice Walker, acting in
his Court, made an excellent rule forbidding the
publication of nasty details in divorce suits. Most
of the newspapers objected. “In the public
interest ” they wanted to goon reaping the harvest
of coppers which a sensational divorce case brings.
Now Mr. Simpson is back and has been asked if
he intends to continue the rule. And, very
characteristically, Mr. Simpson says “he knows
nothing about the rule.” Probably Mr. Simpson
isn’t even aware that Mr. Walker acted in his
august place for some months, and has a vague
sort of notion that the Divorce Court was shut up
in his absence. At any rate, he couldn’t dream of
taking any cognisance of, much less following, the
action of a brother judge, for there’s no wisdom on
the Bench when our learned brother Simpson is
away.
A witness before the Tariff Commission
explained that local importers are indenting
American buggies (said to be prison-made) at £BO
per doz. (as if they were bananas) and retailing
them to unsuspecting Australians up to £25 each.
Freetrade Labor-man Fowler was aghast at the
idea that any importer of shoddy would be so base
as to allow two unsuspecting Australians to drive
round among the local unemployed coachsmiths
in a buggy which cost £6 10s. But he did not
express an opinion on the wisdom of a policy
which, while insisting on paying the local coach-
smith a living wage, takes every precaution to see
that, owing to cheap imports, there is no work
for him to do.
Up to the time this issue went to press
the latest outbreak of plague in Sydney had got
as far as six cases. They were all traceable to the
old plague region down by Darling Harbor, which,
despite all cleaning and fumigating, and boiling
and disinfecting, seems just about incurable. The
trouble began with Margaret Mair and Amelia
Jones. The other day, Divorce Judge Simpson
did a lamentation on the Bench because, as he
said, the good old plain names were dying out,
and the modern girl had a dreadful habit of being
named Gladys, or Marie, or Louise, or Victoria.
Surely when the plague, swiping blindly at a
population of 500,000, hits at the first attempt
Margaret Mair and Amelia Jones, it should bo
proof that the old simple names are not quite
extinct. But perhaps Mr. Simpson wants every
girl to be named Sarah Briggs.

The Bulletin, 2 2nd February, 1906, said: “ The increase in the holding of public securities—which
means safety Is notable. No other A-ustralian office of first or second rank has so large a proportion.’’
The Company’s Consulting' Actuary, Mr. R. P. HARDY,
F.1.A., F.F.A., in his Report on the 1904
Valuation, says:
Directors are fully entitled to claim that the Security they offer, bothTto the present
ssured and to the Public generally, for the redemption of their contracts is of the highest
possible character.
They should use every means to educate the public to appreciate, not merely the great
protection afforded by estimating the Liabilities upon the basis of only a 3 per cent. yield, but
such prudential course has the additional advantage of providing a Bonus fund that
vvt grow as the Company grows in age, and will enable compensation to be made to those longer
livers whose contributions will have helped to build up the Company.”
Actuarial Valuation.
Tfie valuation of the Liabilities of the Company, at 31st December, 1905, was again made
on a per cent, net premium basis. A gross surplus of £71,217 was revealed, of which £20,572
Was wr/tten off Securities; £45,019 was divided, and the balance, £5,626, carried forward. The
amount divided permitted the allocation of Bonuses in accordance with the following scale

“Drifter”: Nothing short of an earth-
quake will make some people start work before
the last stroke of the bell. Know of two Sydney
laborers who got a job, during harvesting time,
on a farm in the Quirindi district, a couple of
seasons ago. One morning, at daybreak, the
cook rushed excitedly into their humpy, yelling,
“ Turn out ! Turn out ! the haystack’s afire ! ”
“Haystack’s on fire, is it?” said one of them,
sleepily ; “ why, what's the time ?” “ Come on ;
buck up, men, and lend a hand! ” yelled the
cook. “It’s about half-past four.” “Ah, that’s
all right, then,” sighed the laborer, thankfully ;
“we don’t start work till half-past six, Jim and
me.” Then he turned in his blanket and snored
on.

“ 0.K.” writes :
Am struck by The Bulletin’s recent statement that
Mrs. Clem. Hill doesn’t want her husband to be a dis-
tinguished cricketer any longer. It sounds so very true.
Thq average girl is a hero-worshipper until she has got
her hero for a husband, and then she soon begins to pout
disdainfully at his bachelor friend Fame. Fame takes a
man out of his wife’s society a good deal, and doesn’t
bring her any compensating pride worth mentioning. It
requires him to remain absorbed in the work by which he
is distinguished, whereas she would prefer to be the
principal object of his attentions. The satisfaction of
being wooed by a popular identity is all very fine and
large, but the state of being wedded to him entails so
many drawbacks that the girl is apt to wish his popu-
larity to the dickens. The rule applies to all sorts of
notables. A young man who has made something of a
* name ” for himself in one line or another, talks to his
outwardly romantic, but inwardly practical, girl on the
subject of Himself and his precious personal triumphs
and the glory he is going to reflect upon her‘by making
her bis wife. And she listens—like a darling little hum-
bug—with an air of feeling greatly honored, by being
ask ed to partake of his Career. She is just pulling his
leg. She knows that as far as his Career is likely to unfit
him for commonplace domestic virtues she will blow cold
upon it —after marriage. And in many cases she blows
bitterly cold.

PRIZES FOR RIDERS AND HORSES.
Professor SKUTHORP offers the following: prizes :

Roug'hrider. Open to ail-comers.
£2o—£ls first, £5 second, for the best Buckjumper pro-
duced. Open to all comers.
CONDITIONS.—For Roughriding Contest are The first
round to be ridden in ordinary roughriding or stock-
man’s saddle ; second round iii a 4in. kneepad rough-
riding saddle ; third round, Sin. kneepads ; fourth
round, 2in. kneepads. All horses to be balloted for
by each rider each night. All horses to be ridden
with a bucking rope. This applies to both contests.
In the event of horse falling or tackle breaking, the
rider to be allowed another trial. None of Skuthorp’s
employees allowed to compete in either contest.
NOMINATIONS, £1 (riders producing own horses free)
to be made with the Secretary, Skuthorp’s Show, Hay-
market Hippodrome, on or before SATURDAY, 31st
March, at 4 p.m. Approved nominations genuine buck-
jumper’s fare will be paid to distance 150 miles ; also
stabling' accommodation and feed.
Two Judges and one Referee to be mutually agreed
upon.

“ A.P.8,.” : “A treat,” as used by local
“ blokes,” is saving many cubic feet of swearing.
In lieu of “ ’E roused on me like blanky blanky,”
he now says “’E roused on me a treat.” Said
one to me lately: “See that Tom over there?
Clobbered up a treat, ain’t she? By gum, it’s
hot; and ain’t the dust blowin’ up a treat? Makes
yer drink a treat, don’t it ? ”
“ D.J.K.” : Over this way (Gippsland,
Vic.) some of the well-to-do settlers employ
Indians as milkers and calf-feeders. One man
lias his milking altogether in the hands of the
aliens. An Indian took the contract at Id. a
gallon, and 2s. 6d. a year for each calf that sur-
vives. From 300 to 500 cows are milked daily, so
that a nice little penny passes into the black-
fellow’s hands, he employing only other Indians
at his own price. They cater for themselves, and
as they can live on part of nothing, save money
quickly. In other parts of E. Gippsland the
swarthy ones have bought farms and go in for
dairying or sheep-rearing.
“Bricky”: What strange places some
drunks get into (and, by the way, what strange
faces some drinks get into !). Self and three mates
were camped among the mountains of Gippsland.
Arthur was the beer-sparrer of the party, and
although the pub. was three miles away, he spent
his evenings there, drinking with the flies. On
one occasion, with the aid of the windlass-rope,
we hauled him out of a 30ft. shaft, where he’d
been standing up to his teeth in water. Another
time, he’d been drinking Jimmy Woodsers, and
didn’t come home. Next day we saw his under-
standings protruding out of a wombat’s hole, and
we hauled him out on his ear. Found him
smothered in a slimy substance that we couldn’t
identify. He blinked, opened his eyes, yawned,
sat up, and said, “Where’s the bottle?” Just
then a huge tiger snake wriggled out of the hole
and slithered away. Arthur had been sleeping
with it.

Eight Years’ Suffering
A PERMANENT CURE.
The Case of MRS. A. WEAVER.
(BY A SYDNEY REPORTER.)
No one can tell the self-denying work which good men have done in the early days of this
county, neither can we exaggerate the nobleness of spirit which animates present-day people in a
desire to ameliorate distress as much as lies in their power. Therefore, the public should be
informed of some of these efforts, one of which was made by Mrs. Alice Weaver, of No. 7 Louis-
street, Redfern, who replied to the reporter by saying:—
“ About this time last year I was living in Telopea-street, and it was just before I left there for
this place chat I was fortunate in casting aside a complaint that had worried me for no less than eight
years. What a fearful life of suffering mine had been all that time, to be sure, and to think that
nothing in tire world could benefit me made my spirits droop lower than they will ever be again,
I hope.”
“ What made you think that nothing could benefit you ? ” queried the writer.
“ I arrived at that conclusion because nothing had done me good so far, and I believe I tried
every medicine going. At the time my second child was born I was under a doctor who did everything
he could for me, but still my health remained as bad as it could be. Indeed at that period I was nearly
dead with indigestion, although I dieted myself and did all that could be done to keep my
torments in check. It was of no avail though, for when I ate anything I could hardly breathe
owing to a feeling which came like a lump in my chest, from which place a sharp
pain went right through to the centre of my shoulders. I then remained in the utmost agony until
the food was vomited up again, after which I felt so exhausted that I could not think of doing a thing
in the house until I had had a good rest. It would surprise you if you only knew how poor my
appetite was. You would wonder how anybody could keep alive with it, and as for my head —
oh ! my sufferings with it were awful. The most piercing pains at one time, and the
most depressing aches at another. Really, I think it was a marvel how I kept my
senses with the agony I endured in the head, and it’s strange that I didn’t meet with many a nasty
accident through the giddiness which used to overtake me. I was a misery to mySelf and everybody
around me, and, to tell you the honest truth, I never thought I would live through it all. My eyes
used to feel heavy enough to drop out of their sockets, and I was affected in the mouth by such a
bitter taste that it made me feel sick of a morning after putting in the usual restless nights that had
done so much towards making a wreck of me. Even in the daytime I was always imagining something
terrible was going to happen, and these were just the very things that Clements’ Tonic,
which I had commenced to take, showed itself to be master of, for it sent such a vigor
through my whole frame when I had been taking it a couple of weeks that I could
not help feeling joyful in the belief that at last my health had taken a, beneficial
turn. I got more Clements’ Tonic, and as each bottle was finished so did my prospects improve, for
I commenced to take my meals with a degree of heartiness which astonished my people as much as
it pleased them, and to my own satisfaction and delight the former discomforts attending my meals
were quickly out of existence. Oh, dear ! what a changed woman lam now to then ! My limbs do
not ache from weakness like they formerly did, and I can go about my work as cheerfully and as
actively as any housewife in the country. I do not spend any restless nights now, never dream
during my sleep, and the pains that used to abound have long since been driven away from every part
of my body. Well, Clement’s Tonic made me the healthy woman I am, and for the sake of other
sufferers I trust you’ll publish this interview in any way you think best.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, ALICE WEAVER, of No. 7 Louis-street, Redfern, in the State of New South Wales, Commonwealth of
Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare that 1 have carefully read the annexed document, and that it contains
and is a true and faithful account of my illness and cure by Clements’ Tonic, and also contains my full permission to
publish in any way my statements —which I give voluntarily without receiving any payment ; and I make this
solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths Act,
1900. (Sgd.) ALICE WEAVER.
Subscribed and declared at Redfern this sixth day of September, one thousand nine hundred and four, befor®
me, L. BREARLEY, J.P.

CURED BY CUTICURA
IN ONE MONTH
A grateful mother, in the following let-
ter, tells of another of those marvellous
cures by Cuticura: “When my baby
was four months old her skin broke out
with a humour. I took her to a doctor,
who said it was eczema. He gave me
medicine to give her, but she kept get-
ting worse all the time. Her little face
and body were so covered with sores and
large scales you could not tell what she
looked like. No child ever had a worse
case. Her face was being eaten away, and
even her finger nails fell off. Then it
itched so she could not sleep, and for
many weary nights we could get no rest.
At last we got Cuticura Soap and Oint-
ment, first bathing her in warm water
with the Soap, and then spreading on the
Ointment with soft cloths. I saw a change
in a week. The sores began to heal, and
she could sleep at night, and in one
month she had not one sore on her face
or body. Any mother having children
with eczema or humours will find a friend
in Cuticura Soap and Ointment, (signed)
Mrs. Mary Sanders, 709 Spring St.,
Camden, N. J., Aug. 14, 1904.”

THE J. B. WILLIAMS CO., 65 Great Russell St., London
Head Office and Factories Glastonbury, Conn., U. S. A.
Business, Robbery, etc.
Just at present the American motor factories
cannot turn out cars fast enough to supply the home
demand, although last year no less than £4,600,000 worth
were made. Now the steam car has proved that it can
travel faster than the petrol-driven machine, so thereissure
to be a boom in the odorless creation. Hitherto the
trouble has been that only the most careful watching
prevented the burning of the boiler, but that risk has
been done away with by an automatic arrangement
which will keep the boiler full as long as there is water in
the tank. The steam car has the advantage of being
absolutely noiseless, and when used for absconding with
the boodle to Canada it leaves no smell behind it to guide
the sheriff’s posse in the high-powered cars they keep
especially for chasing embezzlers.
* * * *
A woman who is making good money nowadays
moves from boarding-house to boarding-house selling some
patent brand of skin-food. Her method is simple. She
has a skin like velvet herself. She patronises only large
boarding-houses With lots of women in them. First night
at dinner her skin is admired and envied. Second day, at
afternoon-tea, she confides the reason—the alleged skin-
food, of course—and offers to supply all with it. Third
day she spends visiting the rooms of the various ladies
making cash sales. Then she leaves for another estab-
lishment.
•:.* *:> * * -
“Drifter” : Australia is the home of the cheap
excursion. Was up on the Clarence River (N.S.W.)
during Christmas, and took a trip from Yamba to
Grafton (a distance of 80 miles return) on the river
steamer. A truly delightful trip. The charge was a
shilling return, with a cup of tea for the girls thrown in.
* * * *
“ G.S.” : Melbourne suburbs are just now being
exploited by a photographic “joint.” Man calls and asks
for a photoi, which he will enlarge free of charge, only
stipulating that you shall hang it up for your friends to
see, and thus advertise his firm. Fortnight elapses, when
his partner calls to inquire what kind of frame you will
have for the enlargement—prices 30s. to £2 2s. No order
for frame, no enlargement, and you can’t even get your
photo, back unless you go to their office and worry them.
* ♦ * *
“Koko” : What swindlers are the registry-
office sharks who claim to supply squatters with all sorts
and conditions of labor, from rouseabouts to married
couples ! I knew a case of a station-owner out back who
wrote to a shark in a large western town for a good mar-
ried couple. Shark replied that he was fortunate in
having just the required article on hand. Satisfaction
was guaranteed, and he would be pleased to despatch
goods at once, fares to be paid by squatter, and service to
be entered under six months’ agreement. Fee payable
to shark, two guineas in advance. Terms and couple
were accepted, and in the course of a few days there
arrived by the mail coach a slatternly great hulk
which was the alleged better-half of a five-foot
nothing, eight-stone-seven, bleary-eyed, beer-sodden
apology for a man (guaranteed able to groom,
milk, kill, and “general useful”). The pair was
accompanied by a miscellaneous assortment of rags
and baggage, and it breathed like a distillery. After the
two had recuperated for a couple of days with the aid of
some bottles they possessed, the boss considered it a fair
thing for Mr. Married Couple to give an exhibition of his
abilities in the labor line. He therefore approached the
bleary one, who was reclining in the shade of the kitchen,
and suggested the advisability of slaughtering a sheep.
Mr. Married Couple wearily advised the boss to kill a
sheep himself. The boss called him a “good-for-nothing
weasel.” “Weasel yerself,” said Mr. Married Couple.
The boss grew angry, and called him a white elephant.
The married couple said in a thick voice, “ Elephant yer-
self.” “I suppose you want whisky and cigars and a
hammock to lie in,” yelled the boss. “ Whisky and cigars
yerself,” said the bleary one. Just then Mrs. M.C., who
was lying inside on the kitchen floor, shouted out to her
better half—“ Give it to him. Jack, for bullying a man
when he’s down.” As I said before, registry offices are
sharks.
* * * •
Way back in the fifties boots known as “mis-
fits for Royalty” were all the fashion. The maker’s
name was Lehocq, and his agent in Melbourne, was a man
called Mould, whose shop was in Swanston-street, almost
exactly opposite the Town Hall. The foot-gear was
advertised as being quite new, and as having been ori-
ginally designed to cover the corns of European kings
and such like—“some slight misreckoning in the
surement alone accounting for their non-acceptance.’
The sale was tremendous, and every Johnnie of the
period had his pair of Lehocq’s, and took care on very
small provocation to turn down the flap of the leather
tops, so that the curious might read thereon, “ King of
Saxony,” or “ Hospodar of Wallachia,” or “ Prince of
Wales,” or “King of Prussia” (no empire then), or
“ Emperor Napoleon III.”
“Drifter”: Some men are born dealers. Har-
rison missed his true avocation by being cast by the
jugglery of Fate into the Civil Service. When any man
in his branch wanted anything, from a cheap second-
hand mangle to a glass eye on time payment, Harrison
was the man to scent it out, for a small commission. He
was familiar with every second-hand shop, pawnbroker
and dealer in the city. One day Brown casually men-
tioned that he wanted to buy a watch-dog, as the burglars
had been round. The dav after Harrison appeared with
a flea bitten, half-starved tyke beneath his arm and
button-holed Brown on the landing. “ Here’s your watch-
dog, Brown, and you’ll find there’s not a better one in
the country ! He’ll keep the burglars off all right. S’elp
me ! I’d never part with him, but I’m afraid he’ll eat some
of the children at home one day. I’vercared himfrom apup.
You can have him for five bob ' Well I’ll sav four seeing
as he’ll be In good hands There’s a bi" of rope fn my
drawer 3'ou cantiehim up with till knock-off time. Say half
a dollar, then!” The dog here fell asleep, and
snored peacefully on the dealer’s bosom. “Look here,
then, you can have him for two bob ! Lor’ love me, my
wife cried when I brought him away for you ; she loved
lum. Will you go a bob, then ? ” “ No ! ” shouted Brown ;
“ I wouldn’t have that mongrel around my place at any
price.” Harrison dropped the sleeping- cur with a duil
thud, and sent it howling dismally down the stairs with
a disgusted kick. He afterwards confessed that he picked
up the faithful hound which his wife loved from under a

The Suez Canal, in which the British Govern-
ment has a large proprietary interest, is to lose £400,000
per annum through a slight concession in rates this year,
It is estimated that a quarter of this sum will be saved to
the mail companies, British and foreign, trading to Aus-
tralia. Even then the canal administration will make a
stupendous yearly profit, which recoups the British
Government in a large measure for the subsidies granted
to mail steamers trading through the canal. This fact is
always ignored when the subsidies granted from the Eng-
is and Australian ternfini are compared. *

“F.C.” : The average undertaking ghoul is a
thrifty individual, or is it the rage for outward show that
led to this piece of hypocrisy? A “respected resident”
was buried from our street lately, and the “funeral
cortege” consisted of hearse, two mourning coaches, one
private buggy, and four waggonettes ; but no sooner had
the procession turned the first corner than two of the
waggonettes transferred their half-loads of passengers to
the others and pulled off homewards. Thus for an extra
shilling or so the deceased —and the neighbors—were
treated to a really imposing funeral.
“ Janor ” : Solved the other day what had
long been to me one of the small mysteries of the
bush. Often had I searched in vain for the plant
which bore that grotesque yam-like growth known
as “ blackfellows’ bread.” Making an excavation
recently I gouged out quite a nest of the stuff
strung together on a root of a peppermint tree
which grew near by. The root passed right
through the centre of the “bread”—which is
only a low-down parasite, and not a species of
yam, as some bushmen imagine.
“ W.S.T.” : Honey-gathering in the N.S.
Wales North Coast bush was, in the old days,
both a pleasant and a profitable occupation. You
either went into the forest and found the bees for
yourself, after laboriously cutting the trees down,
or you gave a tribe of blacks an 18-gallon cask,
which they rolled into a central locality and pro-
ceeded to fill. They got £1 for the cask full,
which brought it below Id. per lb. to the middle-
man ; and, fearful stuff though it was, it brought 3d.
a pound in Sussex-street. The blacks used to eat
the young brood as a special delicacy, with a good
deal of the honey, and used to soak the old wax,
honey, and dead bees until it became what they
called “honey beer.” The whole tribe, gins and
all, used to get comfortably drunk on this. The
advent of “black brood,” which killed thousands
of bee-colonies, broke up this promising industry.
A peculiar fact was that when the blacks’ dogs ate
much of the honey, all their hair fell out, leaving
them hideous spectacles.
A MATTER OF LABELS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ike Parkinson, of Bumpalong,
Keeps an emporium,
And tradeth on his country’s wrong
For any little sum ;
He prints “ Importer ” on his bills,
But buys his goods at Sydney mills,
For he is able,
With a label,
That pays no revenue,
To shift those mills to English hills,
New York, or Timbuetoo.
So if you ask Ike Parkinson
For plain Australian jam,
One thing you can depend upon—
Its name is not a sham ;
But if your prejudice is such
That nothing home-made you can touch,
And you demand
An English brand,
’Twill be a “ take-me-down,”
For ’tis the label costs so much ;
The jam’s from Hobart Town.
“ I’ll tell you this," confided Ike,
“ There’s Brown, of Bumpalong,
An’ there is Jones, of Hornypike,
Two jam firms going strong ;
Now, Jones has tried, but cannot win
The place he manufactures in ;
An’ neither can
Our local man
Acquire the local crowns ;
But Bumpalong buys Jones’s tin,
And Hornypike buys Brown’s.
“ At first the jams were sent by Brown
To Hornypike by dray,
An’ packed in cases from that town
Jones’ product came this way ;
Then we discussed the problem, and
We saw ’twas but the ‘ local brand ’
That caused demurs
’Mong customers,
An’ so we raised the price,
An’swopping labels, local cann’d
Just tasted twice as nice.
“ No jams now come from Jones’s town
Not e’en the tins,” said Ike ;
“ An’ nary case is sent by Brown
For sale in Hornypike.
An’ though we sell Jones’ wrappers here,
An’ Brown’s in Hornypike, ’tis clear
If Brown was there
An’ Jones was where
His jam’s disseminated,
The makers then would be too near
To be appreciated.”
N.S.W.
EDWARD S. SORENSON.
