Of the two best-known processes for getting
the zinc out of the Broken Hill ores, neither was
the work of a metallurgist. Both Potter and De
Bavay, the two discoverers, are brewers’ chemists,
who carried out their experiments on quite dif-
ferent lines, and right off the track along which
metallurgists had been fossicking for years.
Just like their friends the butter exporters,
Melbourne chaff merchants have been monkeying
with the article to such an extent that they have
practically killed theexporting partof the industry.
Six years ago from 30,000 to 40,000 bags of Mel-
bourne chaff were annually sold on the Sydney
wharves; now there is scarcely a bag, save those
sent by a few honest traders who have kept
their names clean and their business brisk. Just
so with oats ; crushed oats are actually sold for
less than oats that haven’t had that labor spent
on them, because they are really one part “hulls”
to two parts oats. It is practically open, undis-
guised fraud ; and the farmer is finding that he,
not the rascally middleman, is getting such a bad
name that nobody wants his produce; so he com-
mences to call for State interference and socialistic
legislation. But the ass persistently sends Tory
agents and middlemen to Parliament to represent
him; and, of course, they are in no hurry to save
him.
The biography of Hardwick’s damper :
Jest about three years ago, me, Jock Alcock, Mun-
gindi, and a nigger cook was out in Westralia. dry-blow-
ing. We had to dry-blow because the water was too
scarce to use for anything but drinking. One night Jock
roused on the nigger for drinking too much water. One
thing led to another, and they finished up with a devil of
a row. In the morning when we got up the murky cook
was gone, also our best horse.
Not a soul in that camp knew anything about cooking,
but I kidded the other two chaps I was a bosker-warbler
of a cook. You see, I’d had about enough of dry-blow-
ing. I started to cook while they dry-blew.
There was lashings of tinned dog, but only 201 b. of flour,
and no damper. “ Well,” says I, “ 201 b. of flour don’t
seem much to make the darn thing out of, but here goes.”
I’d often seen the nigger rolling the dough out on a stump.
It seemed quite easy, so I got the bag and emptied the
flour on the stump, poured some water on an’ mixed her
up. In a few minutes it was ready to be transferred to
the camp oven.
It struck me just then that it ought to have some
baking powder in. I got the tin down, but the cussed
label with the directions was tore off. I was donkey-licked.
I didn’t know how much to put in to save my life. The
tin was nearly full, so I chanced the lot, rolled the damper
round a few more times, and popped it into the oven.
I heaped the ashes all over the top and around it, an’
got-to cleanin’ up a bit. A while after, happenin’ to look
over that way, I seen the lid of the oven stickin’ up in the
air about 2ft., an’ risin’ at the rate of a mile a riiinit. In
about two shakes I had that express-train damper out on
the stump. “ Moses,” said I, “she has rose!” It kep
risin’ an’ risin’ like a live thing. You’d nearly think it
was a frill lizard.
When Jock and Mungindi came home that dam damper
was still risin’. “ Boys,” says I, proud-like, “ I thought
you’d like a bit of a change to-night, so I made a sponge-
cake for yer tea.” “ Good lad,” says they, cornin’ over to
have a look. “ Bit yaller, ain’t it? ” says Jock. “ Where
did yer git the heggs ? ” chips in Mungindi.
When we was finished Jock starts to fill his pipe and
Mungindi to smoke a cigarette, but he couldn’t finish—he
had the hiccups too bad. I began to feel terrible crook,
but said nothink. Jock arsked Mungindi what ’pendi-
citis was like. I was jest goin’ to arst him who’s horse
that was, when four buttons busted off me weskit. I
looks over at Mungindi ; he was red in the face, like a
turkey-cock. I arst him was anythink up. “ Oh, no,”
says lie, “ nothing at all.” Jock said he felt like a foot-
ball someone was pumpin’ up, an’ if he didn’t stop he’d
soon bust.
You never seen such a circus. Jock and Mungindi
kept swearin’ and cursin’ till the swellin’ got that bad
they couldn’t speak at all. Jock went into the tent.
Presently I sees him makin’ signs to Mungindi to go in
too. In a few minits out comes the pair of them lookin’
awful savage. Jack had a piece of paper in his hand, and
Mungindi had a double-barrelled gun. I begin to feel
that it was no place for me. Jock handed me
the paper, and on it I reads : “Me and Mun-
gindi wants to know what the ’ell you put
in that blessed cake. Tork quick, or y’ure a deader.” As
I couldn’t tork quick, or any other way, I wrote on the
paper, “ Bakin’ powder—all we ’ad.” They looks at me,
then at one another, then at me again. I could see they
wanted to larf, but was frightened of bustin’.
The larst I seen of them two coves that night, they was
takin’ it in turns sittin’ on each other’s bread-baskit.
Mungindi had a piece of two-inch rope tied round his.
My clothes was awful tight, but before mornin’ I got
better and went to sleep. I got up about six o’clock, an’
was just lightin’ the fire when I was informed that I was
promoted back to dry-blowin’, an’ that my resignation as
cook was wanted immejit.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SCOTTY and I were fishing afc Wilga
when Ryan shot two of Powell’s prize
white pigs for trespassing. Scotty, who is
very practical when sober, said we might get them
cheap, and hawk them down the river in our boat.
“ They’ll be a’ richt,” he explained, “ if the bluid
ain’t set.”
Powell was laying out the bodies. He shook
liis fist reproachfully at Ryan’s pub. “ Hivin for-
give him ! It’s Jealousy wot done it, jest ’cause
my white beauties beat his black mongrels at the
show.” Scotty looked sympathetic, and offered
to cremate the defunct decently for ss. Powell
suggested burial, and Scotty said there was a quiet
spot near our camp.
Being a cook, Scotty wasn’t a bad butcher, and
before sundown the two pigs were lying, dismem-
bered and glistening with salt, on a big red-gum
log.
After tea Scotty went for a whisky. He paused
for a moment before the pork : “ Peety we ha’ na
mair, mon.”
The next morning I went with Scotty for his
morning’s whisky because he winked when he
asked me. He didn’t often wink gratuitously.
Ryan grinned when he saw us. “Oi shot wan
more of th’ ould psalm-singer’s white varmint last
noight. Go an’ hev a look at ’im.” Scotty went.
I’d seen enough dead pig. Ryan refilled my glass.
“ Sicli a boomer, he is near as big as the two black
uns Oi’ve put up for fattening. Oi sez to”
Scotty stood at the door open-eyed. “ That
dommed Powell’s a real bad un, Ryan; he’s
peented yer peeg.”
Ryan rushed through the parlor. Soon he came
back, pale with anger, and poured out a big nip
with quivering hand.
The bar-clock ticked loudly for a minute or two.
Then Scotty sighed audibly, and Ryan danced
the glasses with his fist, “Oi’ll hev the Law on
him!” He ran his fingers through his beard.
“P’raps yez two ’ll go an’ hev a yarn wid th’
ould hypocrate. He might let on.”
Scotty coughed deferentially. “Sorry to dis-
obleegeye, Mister Ryan, butthefeesh ain’t biting
well, an’ we’re shifting the day. But we’ll hae
time to remove yer peeg before the neighbors see
it for sax shillings.”
Ryan winced, and then counted out the coins.
That night we were drifting down the river by
moonlight. I was steering, and Scotty lay asleep
in the bow. The pork was stacked between us.
When I let the boat graze a snag Spotty woke
angrily. “Was ye dreaming ?” he said.
“Wondering,” I replied, “why Ryan shot only
one of his pigs. ”
Scotty frowned, tentatively. Then he winked.
“’Cos the ither black beastie upset the white peent.”-

BRONCHITIS AND PLEURISY.
A SEVERE CASE CURED BY TWO BOTTLES OF
HE ARNE’S BRONCHITIS CURE.
AFTER OTHER TREATMENT HAD FAILED.
Mr. Hearne, Chemist, Geelong 1 .
Dear Sir, —Some months ago, in Sydney, I suffered from
a severe attack of influenza, and was confined to my room
for about a week, at the end of which time, feeling some-
what better, I got up and tried to transact my business
as usual. But I got up too soon, for the very next day I
had a relapse, and suffered tortures from what the doctor
told me was pleurisy and bronchitis. The pain from the
former in my chest and shoulders was frightful, and for
four long weeks I was confined to my bed, under the care
of a well-known Sydney doctor, and all the time his medi-
cine gave me but temporary relief. The landlady of the
hotel (the Cleveland) where I resided told me of a medi-
cine—Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure —from Victoria, which had
.cured her of a bad attack of bronchitis and pains in the
chest, and begged of me to try it. I did so, and, in thanks
and gratitude to you, tell you that, after the second bottle,
my cough had ceased ; but, what is more astonishing, the
pains from pleurisy entirely left me, and, in about a week,
I was able to attend to my duties as usual. —Yours faith-
fully, J. BRAHAM,
Melbourne Punch Office, Melbourne.

Home-Sick.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I STEERED me north by the Milky Way,
and I kept the throne in sight,
Till I came to the Gate with the jasper posts,
And the heavenly hosts in the Holy Realms of
Light. . ’
Lo ! I sat me down by Peter’s ghost, and it spake
weird words to me ;
It said : “There’s a stranger here with wings,
And he won’t play harps and he never sings,
And he does—well, really—the strangest things,
and he spits in the Glassy Sea ! ”
I says to the ghost: “ I’d like to see this chap
that you speak about,
Eor perhaps it’s a cove I used to know
In an earthly hamlet down below, and who never
was known to shout.”
So Peter’s wraith it took my arm, and we strolled
along the track;
And it talked in a friendly, affable way
About some hymns they had tried that day,
When all of a sudden I yelled “Hooray ! —l’m
hanged if it isn’t our Jack ! ”
Then Peter’s ghost it turned quite pale, and it
looked suspicious at me ;
It said—and its voice was cold and sharp—
“So you know this person who hates the harp and
who won’t be nice and agree ? ”
I stares at the ghost as hard as you like, and I
claps it quick on the back ;
“ Know him ?” I said; “ I should think I did—■
Why, many’s the time he’s lent me a quid
That he’s rose on his watch from the pawnshop
yid—why, there’s no one on earth like Jack !
“Why, we lived together for years,” I said, “and
we wrote for the self-same rag;
He’s the best old pal that ever I had—
He was always cheerful, straight and glad, and a
most reliable snag.
Excuse me, ghost,” I says just here, “ but I want
to shake Jack’s hand ” ;
So I knocks a choir right out of the way—
They were making a most untuneful bray—
And I yells to Jack : “ Hoo-blessed-ray !—so
you’re here on the Silver Strand ! ”
Then Jack jumps up and he stares at me, then
grabs my earnest fin;
And the tears ran out of his bad old eyes,
As he says: “Great Scot!”—what a great sur-
prise—it’s my cobber in earthly sin ! ”
Then I takes Jack’s arm, and I drags him back to
where the ghost stood still
And I said : “Jack’s simply homesick, ghost—
He yearns for a fresh terrestrial post—
Not out on Saturn-or Jupiter’s coast, but down in
the earthly mill! ”
Then Jack speaks up and he says: “ That’s true —
it’s exactly what I desire ;
Send me back to Australia once again—
For I’d rather live there in trouble and pain than
sing in the white-robed choir.
No disrespect to you,” says Jack, “but a man’s
own land’s the best;
For there ain’t no wattles or gums up here,
And your ways are strange and your manners are
queer—
So, if it’s the same, I’d rather clear than remain
a heavenly guest! ”
Then the ghost thinks hard, and he says to Jack:
“ Er —what is Australia like ? ”
And Jack replies, “ It’s a paradise—
If you’ll just subtract the heat and the flies it’s the
best that a man could strike !
But I don’t mind flies and I like the heat, so send
me back again ;
Send me home again on some excuse,
For I’m not the least dashed heavenly use ”
Here Jack’s orbs trickled with pearly juice, and
his face grew pale with pain.
Then the ghost says : “ Well, it seems to me that
your life is a vain thing here ;
We gave you a golden harp to play,
But your mind don’t seem to run that way, so
perhaps you’d better clear ! ”
Then he turns to me, and he says : “ Young man,
I think you’re about the same;
For I notice your halo is not on straight,
So you’d better make tracks for the Heavenly
Gate —
Yes, you and your mate must absquatulate for
the sake of our Heavenly fame ! ”
So we steered us south by the Milky Way, and
we left the throne behind,
A.ye, we left the Gate with the jasper posts,
And the heavenly hosts in the town with the
pearly rind.
We tramped through space for an age-and-a-half,
till we reached Australia’s shores;
Then Jack yelled out: “ Hoo-blessed-ray !
For this is a-land where a man should stay
It’s better for me than the Milky Way, and the
harps, and the Golden Doors ! ”
VIC.
GRANT HERVEY.
Brogan.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WE were a Sydney district football
team away in the country —on one of
those pleasant little jaunts for which
the Rugby Union pays, under the impression that
they improve the standard of country football.
We didn’t worry about that. Our main idea was
to get the necessary match over and have a good
time. .
Brogan especially laid himself out for a high
old “beano,” and turned up for the match in a
divinely elevated condition. His play was some-
thing heartrending, and at half time we lost him
altogether, rather to our relief. We were a team
with a reputation, and he was making a name for
us which would take some living down. He did
not turn up to the dinner given in our honor, but,
when we were comfortably settled in the hotel
parlor afterwards, he broke in on us with much
violence and a breath redolent of country whisky.
He at once took the floor, sang us a song or two,
lectured us on forward play, gave a brilliant, if
somewhat incomprehensible, exposition of the art
of heeling, and finally tried to illustrate his theory
by forming two opposing scrums of himself. This
effort to achieve the impossible was too much for
him, and he collapsed in a heap on the floor.
When he rose, he claimed all the credit for our
victory of the afternoon, offering to fight anyone
who disputed his statements. He sparred all
round the room with an aimlessly good-natured
grin upon his hard-bitten face, but no opponent
was forthcoming. Brogan was a bad man to face
sober, and no one was anxious to try him drunk.
His sparring called up memories, and he began to
talk once more, still making feints at the tobacco-
laden atmosphere. .
“I could use me mits onst,”he said, drawing
his lips in and showing his teeth. “ When I was
a young ’un I had a scrap or two round Redfern,
an’ some blokes took me up an’ sent me to Larry
Foley. Larry, ’e was keen on me. ’E coached
me ev’ry day till ’e reckoned I was good enough,
an’ ’e put me into trainin’ for a ’eavy weight match,
I was bigger than I am now, an’ a willin’ lad too.
’E showed me all the points. I was ready for to
go with the best of ’em. And ’e was keepin’ me
dark, too—you bet.” Brogan winked solemnly.
“I’ad five hundred quid be’ind me,” he resumed,
“ an’ me talent reckoned they was on velvet.
“I was a flash young covein them days, an’
they ltep’ me in brass a treat. I ’ad plenty o’ loud
clobber —blime, it was loud—an’ when I, useter
git down the street of a night with me little black
’at and me wide pants —’strutli, I was ’ot stuff ! O’
course, me bein’ flash, like I was, I ’ad to ’ave a
girl, an’ that’s where I struck trouble. I cottoned
on to a little piece, and she took to me quite
natural. The clobber an’ the bit o’ brass must
ha’ did it, for I never was much of a beauty. She
’ad some sort of a bloke before, but I wasn’t goin’
to let that trouble me. Me an’ ’er useter go out
walkin’ every night, an’ presently this bloke of
’ers come ’angin’ about. ’E was a boilermaker,
all beef, and muscle-bound, an’ I knew I ’ad ’is
measure, but I didn’t want ter git scrappin’ ’cos
of me match. So when ’e talked fight I arst ’im,
perlite like, to wait till I’d finished with ’is
betters, when I’d be most ’appy to chew the con-
tents outer ’im. This seemed to nark ’im a bit,
an’ afterwards ’e was always ’angin’ round.
“I managed to keep outer trouble with ’im for
a long time, but one night me an’ the tart was
cornin’ by a short cut out of the park, where we’d
been takin’ the air, an’ I ’ad just got ’arf over the
fence, when I got a woodener that knocked me
inter the middle of the road. I was up again
pretty slick, an’ the boilermaker was cornin’ at me
again.
“‘l’ve ’ad enough of your foolin’ round with
my girl,’ ’e sez. ‘ You’ve got to fight now.’
“I didn’t wait to answer’im. I jus’ plugged
’im fair between the eyes, quick and lively. But
’e was ’ard in the chiv, an’ it didn’t seem to worry
’im much. Then we ’ad a ding-dong scrap, an’
the girl sat on the fence an’ looked on. > I forgot
all about me match an’ everythink else ’cept the
big lump of beef m front of me. I got all round
’im, but ’is big arms kep’ pumpin’ out like pile-
drivers, an’ I couldn’t get near enough to ’it ’im.
So I tapped ’im ’ere and there, gettin’ ’im angry,
an’soon’e began to make bull-’eaded rushes. I
side-stepped ev’ry time, but ’e pulled up too quick
for me to git to ’im. ’E moved well for a big ’un.
Onst ’e got a loose left onto me, an’ nearly passed
me out, but ’e was tirin’ fast, an’ I let ’im go a bit.
Presently ’e stumbled, an’ I whipped in quick an’
landed ’im on the point ”
Just here a diversion took place, the barman
entering with a fresh tray of drinks. Brogan
grinned a drunken grin.
“I’ll show yer just ’ow it was,” he said; and
before we could interfere he put a regular beauty
on to the barman’s jaw, and sent him down with
a crash of glassware. It took us half-an-hour to
clear away the wreck, bring the barman to, and
collect sufficient silver to salve his wounded spirit.
Brogan looked on all the time with a grin of
amused satisfaction, and, when all was quiet, con-
tinued —
“I landed ’im one on the point, an’ ’e went
down like a bullock. ’E was dead to the world,
an’ jus’ then a cop comes along, so I whips over
the fence and clears, and the girl after me. The
boilermaker was three weeks in the ’ospital, but
that wasn’t what troubled me. I ’it ’im that ’ard I
broke me floggin’ ’and, an’ I knew me match was
settled. The girl, she come round nmkin’ a fuss,
but I was too narked to take any stock of ’er. I
just cleared out and made a night of it.
“Next day I turns up to Larry, with me ’and
tied up a treat.
“ l ’Ullo,’ says ’e, ‘what’s up?’
“ ‘ Got a sore ’and,’ I says.
“ ‘ What’s the matter with ut ? ’ ’e says.
“ ‘ Broke ! ’ says I, for I knew it was no manner
of use foolin’ with ’im.
“ • Let’s see ut,’ ’e says.
“ I showed it to ’im and ’e felt the bones.
“ ‘ ’Ovv the ’ell was it broke ? ’ ’e says, quiet-like.
“So I told ’im the ole yarn. ’E never said
nothin’, but when I’d finished e took me to the
door.
“ ‘ Look there,’ ’e says, pointin’ out.
“I looks out an’ ’e gives me a kick be’ind that
sends me arf acrost the street.
“‘That’s all the use I’ve got for fellows like
you ! ’ says ’e.
“ I never seen ’im since, but I ain’t forgot all
’e showed ine. If any o’ yous blokes wants a
‘go ’ I’m yer man.”
He looked round expectantly, but no one
moved. He was a hard citizen, was Brogan.
A.L.M.H.
The People of the Pool.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE big policeman took off his helmet
and heaved a sigh of content that might
have come from the chest of an elephant.
The gardener looked up, nodded, and leant on
the handle of his spade.
“A war-rm day, John.”
“Yis, it's all that!”
“But a mahn might aisly be doin’ jooty in a
worse shpot nor this.”
“Thrue f’r you.”
There was a pause.
Then said the constable thoughtfully : “John,
cud y’ tell me whin ye’re goin’ t’ clane out the
pond up at th’ north gate ? ”
“ No, I can’t; but why d’ye ask ? ”
“ Well, I’ll tell y’! I wuz roun’ that way about
five yesterd’y mornin’, havin’ a bit av a rist on
the sate, whin th’ Vanus-die-Midicine— you know
Yanus, her that begs down East Melbourne—
comes along t’ do her hair. Have ye iver
seen her at it ? No ! It’s th’ sthrangest
thing ■ in th’ worl’ t’ see her nale on th’
ciminted idge av th’ pool an’ sthart fixin’ up her
gray locks be tli’ reflixion in th’ wather. I laffed
there till I nearly bur-rst listenin’ t’ her
swhearin’ at th’ burrds whiniver they ruffled th’
surface with their wings. She samed t’ have a
spechul set on a pair av swallows who were too
cleanly f’r her taste, an’ she cursed thim until I
thought I’d have t’ go out an’ war-rn her. Thm,
what does she do but sthrips off some av her duds
an’ has a tine wash. I know I ought t’ have
sthopped her because it’s a pond that s only claned
out once in a while, but I couldn’t find it in me
hear-rt t’ interfere wid th’ poor tiling, so I just
stayed where I was an’ watched her till she
finished an’ wint trailin’ off through the trees.
“Well, she hadn’t been gone more than three
minits be me watch whin along comes Dandy Dick
an’ stharts his mornin’ abolutions. John, if any av
thim fellies thry t’tell y’ that legend about Dick
never washin’, ye have my authority f’r saym’ he
does. That man washes—though I must confiss it
give me a sthart t’ see him douse his head an’
kape it under till I thought it was a sooeycide I
was aftlier witnessin’. Not that it wouldn’t be
th’ bes’ thing th’ poor felly cud do, y’ understan’,
but th’ bit av wather was bein’ overworked, an’ it
made me onaisy t’ see ut. I hate t’ see any dumb
thing get more than it can manage. If there had
been any soap used th’ suds on th’ surface would
have given th’ show away, but afther each av th’
poor devils had finished an’ th’ ripples had sub-
sided, th’ puddle looked as ca’m an’ innercint
as a blessed pool in th’ desert. I daresay that ac-
counts f’r th’ Wanderin’ Jew—whose habits is
cleanly—washin’ his head in ut five minits
later. I wouldn’t have lit him do ut if
I’d seen him at th’ sthart, but I’d gone
away up th’ path f’r a bit t’ have a look at
a chap who wint by carryin’ a bag, an’ whin I
got back th’ Jew had Ins head underneath. It
would have bin croolty t’ animals to have dish-
turbed him at that stage, so I procaded on me
way an’ lift him to his fate. 1 If,’ says I, if lie s
pizened an’ dies, ’twill be a solium war-nun t’ him
t’ be more careful in .th’ nex’ world—if he’s a
stharter !
“Now I can’t say what happened t that
wather while I was out of sight, but I do know
that whin I came back three sthrangers was
wringin’ out socks in the foreground, adjacent t’
fch’ puddle, while a spirit in the middle distance
was shakin’ his shir-rt at the whole wide world.
‘“Good Lord!’ says I; ‘have ye no sinse av
justice at all. Git out av that! ’ An they got.
“Well, what with vaituous indignation an
chasin’ thim, an’ th’ mornin’ bein’ close, I felt
knocked out, so what does I do but goes back t
me sate under th’ tree f’r a breather.
“ Believe me, I hadn’t finished moppin me brow
when a Chow came shufflin’ along an’ stharts
edgin’ roun’ that three-pennyworth av slush.
“‘Will, Charlie,’ says I t’ mesilf, ‘ theres no
denyin’ both you an ’ y’r war-rdrobe would be th
betther av it, but be th’ Lord Harry ye’ll not have
it there while I have th’ strength t’ kape y from
it! ’ , . , , r
“Thin I seen him get down on Ins knees, an I
knew what he were at. Be the holy frost, John,
th’ pore haythin was goin’t’ dlirink ut!
“It was more thin I cud stand. I lift irmhel-
mit lyin’ on th’ sate an’ rushed at him, wavin me
a-arms. ,
“ ‘ Sthop r I yells. > ‘Sthop that. Git out av
me sight—go on now ! ’
“ ‘ Whaffor ? ’ says he, lookin’ up. Whatfor !
“ ‘ Whaffor be damned ! ’ says I. ‘ Clear out av
that.’ . .. ~,
“ Th’ pore yellow haythin—still naylin at th
pool—pinted t’ his mouth. ‘Me wantee dlink,
says he. , , . , ,
“ ‘ Well, want! ’ says I, ‘ but if y’ attmipt t lap

“ ‘ Whaffor ? ’ says he, backin’ off.
“ ‘ I'll show ye l' says I, an’ with that I chased

“John, ye’ve no idee how a Chow can thravel
until y’ chase him —or he chases you. I remimber,

KODAK.
The Conservative West Australian
(Perth) has religiously tabooed the word “ Sena-
tor,” since the choice of the electors in 1901 fell on
.Labor men and Democrats. In its place is used
the cumbrously-constructed phrase “one of the
West Australian members of the Federal Senate.
This makes “Doctor” Hackett feel better.
“Robert Hap” writes :
I attended a wimmen’s meeting. It was my business
to be there. I could never have withstood the feminine
scowls, and the conversation, and the mingling odors of
Jockey Club and Parma Violet, had I not been fortified
with the knowledge that Reid was to address them.
When Mister Reid takes the centre of a dimly lighted
platform, and the old ladies form a crescent of millinery
behind him, he looks like the star in a ventriloquial
show. His watch chain attracted some attention
and looked like a pale streak of light across a,
globular rain-cloud. The fact that the orator perspired
(it was a hot night) called for the remark of an irreverent
female in front of me, “Lor’, ain’t ’e sweattin’.” Reid
o-ave the ladies lots of advice on how to organise, and took
his “ winder” from his eye to point out the folly of Mrs. A
refusing to call on Mrs. B because Mrs. B’s husband sold
prawns. “ We want to organise,” he said, “or the Socialists
will win,” and, although he didn’t say anything really bad
about them, he used the word “Socialists” in much the
same eerie voice as the nurse girl uses when she threatens
to call the bogie man to baby. However the remarks of
the great anti-Socialist had some effect. One old lady
who was there is an acquaintance of mine. She got an
idea that these Socialists (she calls them “ sociables,” and
believes that there are forty or fifty of them) are a “ bad,
wicked lot,” only lying in wait to rob her Chinese hawker
of his baskets, or to steal the poor widow’s mangle. Also,
she wants to know what the police are doing !

A Great Martyr
TTTHEN you are bilious, every thought of
\\ food is nauseous. When the room
' T gets warm you feel as if you are going
to be sick. If you hurry to catch a
car or a train, there the feeling is again. Head-
aches, turning off with sickness, a nasty coated
tongue, are other symptoms, of Biliousness. All this arises
from misdirected bile, which is due, primarily, to improper
liver action. This is illustrated in the case of Mrs. Edith
Hill, of 143 Wyndham-street, Waterloo, Sydney. “For
several years, ” she says, *‘l suffered terribly with Biliousness
, and sick headaches. When an attack came on I would grow
quite dizzy, and my eyesight was blurred. These attacks would come on
quite suddenly without warning, and while they lasted I suffered extreme
agony, and I lost all inclination for food. I could ndt hold up my head,
and the least noise irritated me. In fact, I could not bear to be spoken
to, I felt so irritable. As you may be sure, I was not enduring all this
misery without attempting to remedy it. I tried many advertised cures,
and also consulted doctors, but failed entirely to gain any relief. A friend
gave my husband a few Bile Beans, and recommended them strongly as
a cure for Biliousness, and although, after my experience with medicines
in general, I had little faith in Bile Beans doing me any good, I thought
I would try them. After taking a few doses, I began to feel an
improvement, and thus encouraged, I persevered with the Beans, taking
in all three boxes, and I am now thoroughly cured, and can do my
housework with pleasure. I never lose a chance of praising Bile Beans
for the great good they have done me.”

DEBILITY, FEMALE WEAKNESSES, NERVOUSNESS, BAD BLOOD, BAD
BREATH, ANiEMIA, DISTURBED SLEEP, LOSS OF APPETITE, SUMMER-END

[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
THY face in dreams, I see, dear one,
In dreams thy face I see ;
Yet ne’er can claim thy love alone,
Nor ever call thee mine, dear one;
Yet here’s my heart-felt wish alway,
A wish renewed from day to day ;
That happiness be yours, dear one,
Wherever you may be,
Though I am far from thee, dear one,
1 hough I am far from thee.
From care, God grant you’re free, my fair,
God grant from care you’re free ;
rh y life, too, blithe and debonair,
And radiant as the sun, my fair ;
The while I trust with heart elate,
That you will find a friend in Fate ;
Have each desire achieved, my fair,'
Whatever it may be ;
And gay and joyous glee, my fair,
And gay and joyous glee.
Then let me add this plea, sweet heart,
Let me then add this plea ;
That though our lives must lie apart,
You 11 sometimes think of me, sweet heart,
While unto thee and him you’ll love,
I’ll drink a health all healths above 1
A health with nine times three, sweet heart,
Whoever he may be :
Since thou art not for me, sweet heart.
Since thou art not for me.
VIC.
E.W.D.
The Superstitions of Susannah.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Superstition was the soul of Mrs.
Susannah Browne’s existence. There was
not a thing she did at any hour of the day
that did not bring some omen, ill or good. A
joy or sorrow was foretold by every chance
circumstance crossing her life, and as she took
most energetic pains to counteract baleful portents
and to avoid counteracting favorable ones she was
distinctly a disturbing element in the sphere in
which she moved. Her husband, the docile James
John Alexander Browne, bore the brunt of the
infliction. He was perpetually being called upon
to further or frustrate the fulfilment of Fate ; and,
as it never at any time struck Mrs. Susannah
Browne that an omen that could be put otf the
track was not worth worrying about, James had a
busy and an anxious time.
Whenever James spilt the salt at table he had,
besides enduring a fiery scolding, to go through an
elaborate extravaganza, throwing a pinch of salt
over his left shoulder and solemnly declaiming
“ My hand forgot
And spilt some salt,
But let me not
For my sad fault
Like wife of Lot
Be turned to salt! ”
Then if Susannah dropped her parasol nothing on
earth would induce her to pick it up herself, and
so run the risk of the disappointment which such
an indulgence in self-help would involve. Either
James had to restore the gamp or she would com-
mandeer the handiest stranger to perform the job.
Once she dropped her gingham when in full flight
from a very business-like bull, and James had to
recover it. It was a toss-up whether he would
ever return alive. The bull did the tossing, and
James came down heads and tails simultaneously,
Anyhow it felt like that.
In the Browne establishment it was never pos-
sible to look at yourself in a looking-glass without
contortions that would paralyse a circus, because,
in order to dodge the seven years’ bad luck that
follows upon a broken mirror, that article of vanity
was invariably hung so high as to be almost utterly
useless. Once, however, a looking-glass fell upon
the luckless James. It didn’t break, but he did,
and the doctor’s fees amounted to seven guineas.
But Susannah was placid. She reckoned that a
loss of £7 7s. —not counting the personal discom-
fort of James—was cheap compared with seven
years’ bad luck.
Cross-eyed men constituted Susannah’s cross,
and she floundered laboriously under her burden.
One evening, James brought a cross-eyed sailor
home to tea, and the name of Browne nearly got
into the Police Gazette as a consequence.
Susannah couldn’t help her first look at the man
of evil-boding, but she immediately applied all
the recognised antidotes for such a misfortune—
turning round three times, spitting on her hand
and making a cross, walking backwards, etc., etc.
The remainder of the evening was a sore trial to
the squinting man of the sea. Mrs. Browne was
all that hospitality and politeness demanded, but
never looked at her guest once. Her “Some
more tea, Mr. Oalcum?” “Let me pass you the
prawns?” “Do try a little pickled cucumber!”
and the rest, were addressed to the chandelier,
the fireplace, to the middle button on James’s
waistcoat, or some other irrelevant object.
Next day, Susannah accidentally dropped Marie
Corelli’s “ Sorrows of Satan ” down a well in the
backyard, and bewailed it as a misfortune due to
her having shoo’d a black cat off the premises only
that very morning !
* » * * •
Odd numbers were also very aggravating, espe-
cially because, barring 13, they always demanded
so much reverential respect. Susannah owed
many a wrinkle to her fruitless attempts to find
two odd numbers >which, when added together,
would produce a result that was not even. She
habitually rose at 7, and went to bed at 9. They
wera two odd numbers right enough, but when
subjected to addition they were 16, a fact which
disturbed her greatly. In this connection, how-
ever, her greatest trouble was 13, and when the
13th of the month fell on a Friday, she stayed in
bed, forbidding the use of fire in the house in case
of accidents. So James was debarred from smok-
ing, and had to hold his digestive fort with tinned
meat and cold potatoes. These experiences in-
variably laid him out with dyspepsia, but Susan-
nah herself was quite comfortable.
* * * *
If a party of four crossed hands in saying good-
bye, Susannah felt it a solemn duty to unshake,
so to speak, and do the leave-taking all over
again. Dreams were a constant disturbance- to
her, bringing with them alternately delight or
dismay, while the number of wishes she made on
seeing three black crows, a white horse, a blind
man, &c., &c., would have given the Recording
Angel a steady job in themselves. She was also
an energetic collector of lucky coins, which she
left about the house, on the theory that they had
to stay where they were first placed on leaving
her hands. This was one of the few bright spots
in James’s life, as he often found the lucky coins
a convenience. But perhaps the superstition
which Susannah nursed most violently was the
belief that if a garment were put on upside down,
or inside out, or back to front, it must be allowed
to remain so until the proper time came for it to
be taken off again. And it was this which caused
the revolution in the House of Browne.
* * * • *
One morning Janies, in a hurry, got the right
leg into the wrong side of his trousers, and before
he could adjust the calamity surreptitiously, he
knew from a shriek that Susannah was on the
warpath. It was no use his protesting ; the pants
had .to be put on wrong side front, and kept front
side back. It was also no use pointing out that
he couldn’t go to work like that. Susannah was
firm, and said he could wear his Sunday frock
coat, and so cover the anomaly. James glared at
her, and declared that he would be d—d if he’d
go out sign-painting in a frock coat, and asked
sarcastically if Mrs. Browne would like him also to
don a tall hat, spats, and bright tan boots. The
upshot was that he stayed at home and lost a
day’s work. There was not a more snortful man
m the Commonwealth of Australia that day, not
a more melancholy one as he sat down and con-
templated his own preposterous appearance. Still,
the time was not wasted, for there developed in
the mind of James during those hours a firm reso-
lution to free himself from the shackles of the
superstitions of Susannah.
* * » *
Next day (Friday), clothed in his right
pants, James : started on his career of sin.
He sent for a ticket in Tatts., and begged
that even numbers should be sent if possi-
ble, unless something with a 13 in it was
available. He deliberately proceeded to put
every superstition of Susannah to defiance. He
spilt the salt at every meal, and refused to reel
out incantations. He decoyed Susannah out to
lonely places, where she dropped her parasol and
had to pick it up herself or lose it. And in this
way she lost eight umbrellas in quick succession,
oeing a dropper by nature. He heaved a half-
brick at every black cat he came across, and in-
cited small boys to bring in black cats that he
might chase them out again. Also he broke eight
cheap looking-glasses. On top of all this he fra-
ternised with three cross-eyed sea-captains and
brought them home to tea ! Poor Susannah was
crushed, and when one of the captains explained
that he had a crew of 13 and expected to sail on
Friday she fell on the floor in a dead faint.
It was while she was insensible that the telegram
arrived informing James that his Tattersall’s
ticket had won £7500. Susannah was revived to
hear the good news by three cross-eyed sea cap-
tains pouring water over her.
PAUL MEL.
The Amazement of 007.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THREE of us there were in the smoking
compartment—an aggressively friendly
man, myself, and in the corner opposite me
a thin youth of 25 or so, a joyless youth of bulging
brow, and repousse chest, and spectacles, and the
pale cast. This young man carried a collapsible
butterfly-net, which looked very like a fishing-rod,
and a litter of small zinc boxes on the seat beside
him displayed labels such as “ Hym, Farn. Ichneu-
monidae,” with others of dread import. The
friendly man paused in his tale of how a dear old
pal of his had been about to invest one hundred
million pounds in N.S.W., and how that dis-
tressing business of the Six Hatters (who were
thrown into the sea by ‘That Watson’) had
changed his mind. He paused, I say; and
hiccupped. His eye caught that collapsible
deceiver.
“ Any luck, mate ? ” he asked.
The young man looked up from “Trans. Linn.
Soc., 1902.” “Oh, yes,” he said, and looked
down again.
“ How many?”
“ Um—er—oh ! about 40 ; about 40.” The eyes
behind the spectacles showed irritation.
'‘Forty! Got onto ’em. Gave ’em away, X
suppose ? ”
“My good man—no. They’re in those boxes.
Now be quiet, I want to read.”
The tramguard—No. 007—came and leaned
against the open door. The friendly man, the
honey of his nature turned acid at this impudent
jesting, whispered loudly in his sympathetic ear,
pointing anon to the net, anon to the boxes.
“ Underweight! ” said 007. “ Forty in there ?
Cripes ! Daoiv-Un’ -street ! ”
“Pullin’ the leg of a cove old enough to be’is
father,” muttered the rebuffed.
Then the scientific young man lurched hurriedly
for the door, gathering his gear, and fell ungrace-
fully into the outer darkness.
“Wish ’e’d fell on ’is ’ead ” said the friendly
man to me. “’Ullo! Wliat’s this? Left some of
is fish baskets. Now well ’ave a look at ’is 40.”
“’And’em over ’ere” commanded 007. “I’ve
got to take ’em to Bridge-street. But I ’spose
we’d better see what’s inside.”
I passed the three boxes down. "Hum. Fam.
Thynnus ,” one of them informed the world. I
sought the door.
“ I wouldn’t open them,” I said.
“ Why, Joe ?”
“ Joe / ” I left him to his fate.
“Rum name ’e’s got,” said the. doomed 007.
His friends helped him, and the boxes opened
simultaneously. A yellow flash pervaded the air.
“Damn !” said 007. He jerked his box away
from him, and said it three times in quick suc-
cession. Another open box fell upon the knee of
the friendly one. He clapped one hand to his
thigh with an anguished yell, and beat wildly in
the air with his other paw. I slipped out and •
along the footboard to the next compartment,
closing the door after me. Thence I watched 007
take his coat off and begin on his shirt. Through
the glass the cries of these two men came faintly,
and once a large yellow wasp, with a countenance
like a tiger, flew at my face. I heard the click of it
against the pane, and watched it seek out new
pastures on 007.
Then came my street. I left them to dree their
weird. But heaven help that young man with
the collapsible implement if 007 ever sets eyes on
him again. His mangled remains will be dragged
behind a maddened tram-car from Coogee to Oir-
cular Quay. •
